THE RED PAGE
Huneker’s Essays
AFTER Lowell's death America
could boast of only one critic of
importance—Henry James, and
long ago he gave up formal criticism
for fiction. How there are two critics in
America whose work has interest beyond
its home—Paul E. More, author of the
Shelburne Essays, who displays the cul-
ture and grace of the best English tradi-
tion, and James Huneker, who follows
Henry James as an explorer in foreign
literature, and devotes himself with
special zest to the works of those artists
who are regarded as morbid, decadent or
mad. Of Austro-Hungarian descent on
one side and Irish on the other, James
Huneker was brought up in America and
Paris, well trained in music, and educated
for the Church, but drifted into jour-
nalism, and is now art critic for the Hew
York Sun. He has published two
volumes of short stories, many of which
are unusual and imaginative in theme,
but unsatisfactory in treatment; he may
be no more than a dilettante in the other
arts he has practised, but his origin and
his training have admirably fitted him
for criticism in the particular field which
he seems to have chosen. In range he is
comparable with Arthur Symons. His
temperament is analytical and indivi-
dualistic ; his outlook is forward, not back-
ward ; and he is most attracted by the
moderns who are engaged in breaking up
the old formulas or paving the way to a
new world of ideas. Hence his Iconoclasts:
a Booh of Dramatists contains sym-
pathetic criticism of the works of Ibsen,
Hauptmann, Sudermann, Maeterlinck,
and Shaw; and his latest volume, Egoists:
a Booh of Supermen (per Sydney Book
Club) is equally worth reading for its
stimulating studies of Baudelaire, Flau-
bert, Anatole France, Barres, Max Stirner
and others.
Huneker seems to have a fad for single
word titles Melomaniacs, Overtones,
Visionaries, etc., just as the dramatist
Bernstein superstitiously avoids a title
with more than six letters. Heither
Egoists nor Supermen is an illuminating
description of such diverse characters as
the sentimentalist Stendhal and the
pessimist Huysmans, William Blake,
Walter Pater, and the others mentioned.
Helvfitius plausibly referred all human
actions to self-interest. Egoism is so
universal a characteristic that it is value-
less as a label, except in the case of
writers, entirely devoted to the cult—
Max Stirner and Barrfes for example.
\!/
Barr&s called hisownbooksmetaphysical
fiction, the adventures of a contemplative
young man’s mind. To study and note
the fleeting shapes of his soul was the
one thing he thought worth doing in a
life of mediocrity. He would make of
himself a mirror reflecting the changes of
his environment, and as Amiel believed
that every landscape is a state of
soul, so he avowed that his soul
was full of landscapes wherein all
might enter and find themselves. He
was as frank about his soul as Walt
Whitman was about his body, and more
interesting. Huneker describes his first
book as a Vita Nuova of egoism, and says
that “in his fugue-like flights down the
corridor of his metaphysics he never
neglects to drop some poetic rose, some
precious pearl of sentiment.” Max
Stirner, who, as the French would say,
imitated Hietzsche in advance, is the
almost forgotten prophet of egoism, whose
book, The Ego and his Own has been
translated into English. His real name
was Schmidt, and it is stated that his
wife, objecting to his egotism, left him,
came out to Australia, and became a
washerwoman. This valiant enemy of
the State, who would make a clean sweep
of civilisation, was a timid man, quiet
and unassuming in manners. And
Hietzsche, delicate from youth, “ living in
an inferno, mental and physical,” was a
poetical advocate of the gospel of strength,
of the Dionysian element in art and life,
and of egoism raised to its highest power.
Henry JBeyle-Stendhal, of whose numerous
works only Rouge et Noir survives, was
a commonplace, ugly little man; yet
he possessed an exquisite sensibility,
was always in love and showed incom-
parable knowledge of the human heart.
These writers were supermen only in
their intellectual life.
Huysmans began as an egoist record-
ing physical emotions—“ the prose singer
of neurasthenia, a Schopenhauer of the
cookshops,” who over ugliness would be-
come almost lyric.
He was a specialist, one who had ransacked
libraries for 'curious details, despoiled per-
fumers’ catalogues for their odorous vocables,
pored over technical dictionaries for odd-
colored words, and studied cook books for
savory terms. His gamut of sensations
began at the violet ray. He was a peverse
aristocrat who descended to the gutter, there
to analyse the various stratifications of filth ;
when he returned to his ivory cell he had
discovered not humanity, but an anodyne,
the love of God. Thenceforth he was inter-
ested in one thing—the saving of the soul of
Joris-Karl Huysmans.
Huneker’s studies of Baudelaire and
Flaubert are based on an intimate know-
ledge of their work, and remove a number
of misconceptions due mainly to the cur-
rency of Maxime du Camp’s reckless gossip.
His brief glimpses of two mystics—Ernest
Hello and Francis Poictevin—induce
further inquiry. He writes freshly about
Anatole France, in whom he finds some-
thing of Petronius, and not a little of
Saint Francis. In a sentence he ex-
presses his opinion of the art of Anatole,
a sentence which serves as an illustration
of his habit of accumulating epithets :
An art ironical, easy, fugitive, divinely
untrammelled, divinely artificial, which like
a pure flame blazes forth in an unclouded
heaven. ... la gaya scienza, light feet, wit,
fire, grace, the dance of the stars, the
tremor of southern light, the smooth sea—
these Nietzschean phrases might serve as an
epigraph for the work of that apostle of
innocence and experience, Anatole France.
The essays assume a good deal of know-
ledge on the part of the reader; the
style is uneasy and at times irritating
with its mannerisms, but the criticism is
independent and suggestive, and helps
to an understanding of some writers who
are worth bothering about.
De Maupassant
All short story writers should respect the
name of Guy de Maupassant. America can
find no greater praise to bestow upon her
greatest living exponent of the art of the
short story, 0. Henry, than favorable com-
parison with the great French master, the
man whose fame after tfie publication of one
story immediately eclipsed that of Zola and
Daudet, who became at a single bound the
most prominent writer of his age. The
story in question is the short, rather cynical,
anecdote relating to a petty incident in the
Franco-Prussia war, called ( ‘ Boule de
Suif.” It, however, took one year in the
writing, which fact should be pregnant wioh
meaning to nearly all writers of the present
day.
The son of a woman in whom that strange,
yet far from uncommon, combination of
genius and madness freely mingled, Mau-
passant seemed fated from birth, predestined
to a life of fame and melancholy, a death of
terror.
To the novelist Flaubert he owed his first
lessons in the art of which he eventually
became the world’s acknowledged master.
v;-“jYou have intelligence,” said Flaubert to
his pupil, after glancing at some work of his,
“ only no originality.”
But how to attain that for which all
writers are continually striving ? Flaubert’s
formula was a hard one :
Go out, he said, “to the first cab-rank
and study the first horse ; then write a brief
description and show, by one word if possi-
ble, in what that cab-horse differs from all
other horses in Paris.”
By one word if possible ! That was the
goal of . Flaubert, which, after studying
under him for seven years, Maupassant
triumphantly reached.
In 1887—Maupassant was then 37 years of
age ; healthy, rich, renowned—the sword,
which since infancy had been suspended
over his head, fell. One evening the door
of his study opened, a figure entered, sat
down beside him, and dictated a story. The
story finished, Maupassant, pen in hand
looked up ; but the figure had vanished.
Then he realised it had never been, was bu
the wraith of a diseased imagination.
From that moment he was a haunted man
His brother, Henri de Maupassant, six years
younger, married as a youth, living the quiet
regular life of a gentleman farmer, had re-
cently gone mad ; what hope was there, then,
for the elder brother, living at best in a
world created by his own fancy, peopled with
the denizens of a teeming brain. • Gqy de
Maupassant fled to his yacht and sailed away
in search of peace. Did he find it 1 The
question is answered in “ Sur 1’ eau,” the
gloomiest yet most beautiful book of travels
ever written.
Then one witnesses as brave a fight against
outrageous fortune as was ever made by
mortal man. In the very furnace of suffer-
ing his whole character became purged. The
bitterness of younger days is gone, and over
his writings there now breathes an infinite
love, an almighty compassion. What in
Tolstoi or Balzac can compare with ‘ ‘ The
Field of Olives ?”.... Returning one night
from the theatre, he found all the furniture
of his house had become alive ; chairs
trotted out past him into the garden ; sofas
waddled, like alligators, on tiny legs ; even
the grand piano hopped in concert with its
fellows ; moreover, as a crown of thorns to
a cross of agony, he, at that period, fell, for
the first time, in love.
The feelings of the lady, a brilliant wordly
woman, history has been unable to gauge.
If she feared, and treated such a lover with
coldness, who can blame her ? It is known,
however, that one evening after leaving her
louse in some bitterness, his mind gave
way completely, and he was moved to Passy,
where, on July 6, 1893, he died.
ALDRIDGE EVELYN.
Two new novels with a Maoriland setting
have arrived: one, The Tragedy of a Flirtation,
by Harry B. Yogel (Greening); the other,
Philip Loveluck, by Charles Owen (Bell).
Yogel has four or five books to his credit,
and shows a practised hand in the manage-
ment of his story up to a certain point.
His heroine, beautiful and all the rest of it,
refuses all offers, and explains to her aunt:
I don’t want to marry a man I don’t like—at
least not yet. .. . Sooner or later I’ll have to go
to the stud. .. . That’s what I’m wanted for.
If I must, I must. Only I want to postpone it.
She goes to Maoriland instead, and flirts with a
settler who eventually forces her into marry-
ing him. She likes him but hates the life,
and on discovering that he has had a Maori
mistress, she returns to London. Then the
story goes to pieces, and ends in crude melo-
drama. Philip Loveluck is not a bad love
and murder story with an unexpected end-
ing. It also boasts an irregular Maori lady
and a sacrificed white wife, but is stronger
and more closely knit than Yogel’s yarn.
Fingerposts scattered here and there, such
as ‘ 4 Brown eyes that prophesied approaching
tragedy,” “She did not know that a great
shadow was going to fall between Philip and
herself,” are signs of the amateur which will
disappear ; but Charles Owen does not seem
to have any sense of humor, and is, there-
fore, not a very promising proposition as a
novelist.
A. R. Goring-Thomas, formerly of Sydney,
certainly has humor, though not much else.
Mfs. Gramercy-Park (John Lane) is a smart,
satirical account of a wealthy American
breaking into English society. Her first
experience was of a Bohemian gathering at
Mrs.s de Courcy Brown’s, where one of the
literary ladies, who is clairvoyant, relates a
talk she had had with Robert Browning, just
as she had come in from her bath :
“ Did you think to ask him about the Chester-
top book?” her husband asked. “Yes. I was
frightened at the effect of my question. Robert
shook in ether, like a bad cinematographic pic-
ture. I really think that Robert was about to
swear. His agitation was so intense that it caused
him to pass away without replying.”
There is a solid relief character from New
England, who comes over as factotum for
Mrs. Gramercy-Park, is tenderly senti-
mental, and bags a duke; while all the
amusing efforts of the brazen hyphenated
person end in failure. The book shows a
feminine acquaintance with women’s toilets,
and the worst of feminine characteristics in
a spiteful attack on another ex-Sydney
writer.
In The Bed Bose of a Summer (Sydney
Book Club) Louise Mack makes use of her
experiences in Italy. That Jtaly of which
she dreamed in an old Bulletin poem :
Oh, Italy, dream Italy !
Are you so fair ?
proves to be even fairer than the dream. She
writes now as might a native of the country,
in English mixed with Italian and scraps of
other languages. Her characters drink their
caffe latte on the terrazzo, eat risottos with
little minced-up fegatini and adorable things
fried in batter in the fritto misto, and when
they are not simpatica or simpaticissima,
they are tedesca. But the polyglot chatter
is very bright and amusing, and the simple
love story is well told.
Splinters on the WaU” and other Verses ,
by “ Narranghi Boori,” alias J. S. Ryan
(published by W. Mahony, Is.), is a col-
lection of humorous jingles written for Aus-
tralian newspapers, which many readers will
be glad to have in a cover designed by
Weston. They are mostly the rollicking
rhymes of one who declines to take life
seriously, and declares that:
My manifest virtues are fewer than yours.
And my life is a blanky sight bluer than yours ;
I’ll take an oath strong
.That both lives are wrong,
But mine has a sweeter allure than yours.
Some of the verses are “suitable for reci-
tation,” others would fit music-hall tunes,
and all are amusing.
Sosh and Anti-osh
The Syduey Worker reprints a series o:
explanatory articles on Australian Socialism
under the title of The Rising Tide , by Dr
H. I. Jensen. After defining Socialism i
general terms and stating that the name
also applied to the movement towards tha
objective, Jensen gives a simple explanatio
of the doctrine of evolution, the effect o
environment, the evils of the present systei
and the way out. He describes the Labo
party as the party of Evolutionary Socialist
as distinguished from the Revolutionar
Socialists, whose scheme “has no scientifi
basis, is destructive to all the noblest in
stincts of man, and is foredoomed to failure.
. . . The case for Evolutionary Socialism i
stated clearly, moderately, yet forcibly, anc
can be read with advantage by anyone.
A. St. Ledger, a Senator for Queensland
publishes a more elaborate volume, attack
ing the Labor party. It is called Austrcdian
Socialism : An Historical Sketch of its Origin
and Developments (MacMillan), and endeavors
to prove that Socialism received its origina'
impulse in Australia from William Lane
that the ideals of Bellamy, Marx and other
European Socialists are the ideal's or ob-
jective of the Australian Labor Party, anc
that the main work of the Labor Party has
been to profess this Socialistic gospel on the
platform and suppress it in Parliament—“in
other words, that its Parliamentary, as dis-
tinguished from its platform and special
press, campaigns, have been one long decep-
tion of the public.” In another word-
fudge ! The charge of deception is absurd,
as his own book shows. The most vision-
ary Socialist when in Parliament must
confine himself to immediate practical
proposals. The ideals of all Socialists
are much the same; their definitions
vary in detail. The influence of Lane,
considerable enough in reality, has been
greatly exaggerated. St. Ledger regards the
birth of Australian Socialism as an unpre-
cedented event, a weird phenomenon in a
new country, during a period of progress and
comparative prosperity. “It fell like a bolt
from the blue, like a comet unheralded.”
Instead, it was a natural growth which
went on quietly until the abolition of
plural voting broke down the political bar-
rier and suddenly let in the crowd with a
rush. As an attack upon the Labor
party, St. Ledger’s book is futile ; but it has
some value as a contribution to the history
of Australian Socialism, and the appendices
are useful for reference.
Another attack upon the Labor Party
comes in the form of a play suggested
by The Englishman's Home. The author
remains in obscurity, and w r e are afraid
Reaping the Whirlvnnd (Websdale, Shoo-
smith, Ltd.) will not bring him into the
blaze of the footlights. The action begins
in “the near East,” which geographically
ought to be Norfolk Island, but is ap-
parently intended for Japan. A British-
born Russian subject has escaped from
prison on Saghalien and is captured by the
Japs. He is offered freedom and wages on
condition that he goes to “Austral Asia ” to
stir up strife in the Labor Party, and throw
obstacles in the way of defence. A whole
act is occupied by a public meeting at which
this Jap emissary, turned Labor agitator,
declaims against conscription and advocates
a universal strike. The author is not with-
out abilitybut he has more flatulent
patriotism than dramatic sense. He makes
sure, however, by a liberal use of under-
lines, that the actor shall not miss the
proper emphasis, and he wisely directs that
the curtain should be “quick.”

A GREAT SUFFERER
Tried Many Medicines.
“ There is no question of greater importance to a
community than health,’’ said Mr. George Alfred
Willis, of No. 7 Bourke Street, Zetland, when re-
cently engaged in conversation with a reporter,
“ for when that is lost matters remain at a stand-
still pending its recovery. That was my experi-
ence, at all events.” he continued, ‘‘for when I
was taken ill some time back, I was not able to
get along at all.”
“Was your work of a laborious nature, Mr.
Willis?”
“ Carpentering—not the easiest job on earth,
you know ; but if it had been quill driving it would
have been all the same to me, as I had no energy
whatever. As a matter of fact, I was confined
to my bed, and things looked very blue, for I was
getting no assistance from the physic I was taking,
and it seemed to me that I was a done man. I
had read of Clements Tonic, and asked if I would
give myself a last chance with that remedy. A
relative who had used it said ‘ yes,’ very emphati-
cally, and gave me her reasons.”
“ You were in such straits that you were willing
to accept almost any advice, I suppose ? ”
“Well, you see, there was scarcely anything
left for me to take, and as all other remedies had
proved futile, what was the good of going back to
them ? So I soon got some Clements Tonic, and
what a God-send it was, too. I was just about as
low as I could be, not having been outside the
house for a fortnight, and if it hadn’t been that
Clements Tonic was taken just in the nick of time
my ailments may have taken such a hold on my
system as to have defied all efforts at eradication. I
think it was pleurisy that had seized me, and, oh !
what a terrible cough I had—so painful and so
exhausting, that each bout aggravated my feeble-
ness. A great deal of phlegm came away, and one
would almost think I had consumption, to judge
by the pallor of my cheeks and the shrunken
state of my body. The pains in my lungs were
very acute, making me dread the necessity of
drawing my breath, as each time I did, a sensation
like a knife cutting made me writhe in agony. It
was not a life I cared to cling to, believe me,
for one would be far better off dead, and out of the
world altogether, than linger in such misery.”
“ What kind of nourishment were you ifi the
habit of taking ? ”
I was existing on cereal foods, and even that
there was a difficulty in digesting. Always head-
aches in the mornings, a nasty fur roofing my
mouth, which made me continue washingit during
the night, the dryness, too, being troublesome ;
and at least two mornings in the week I would
find myself vomiting. I was pitifully nervous and
shaky, and my mind was not at ease, either da_v
or night. I could not help thinking that some
great trouble was hanging over mvhead, threat-
ening, like the sword of Damocles, to descend
with destruction at any moment, and to tell you
the plain truth, my life was a burden to me.”
“ Were you long using Clements Tonic before
you felt any benefit ? ”
“Some little time passed before I noticed
an improvement, but then you must remem-
ber the circumstances—how thoroughly broken
down I was, and I think the marvel is
that I pulled round so well. But Clements
Tonic kept building me up, and restoring
my internal organs to a healthy state, until I was
as sound possible. What a remarkable thing
Clements Tonic is to make a person eat —wliv,
even on the hottest days it gave me a good
appetite, and such a splendid remedy for nervous-
ness and insomnia, too. For years I had suffered
from giddiness in a bad form. If I stooped to lace
my boots or anything, when I rose up I’d seem to
go round like a top, and even when out walking
I’d give a sudden reel occasionally, like a man who
had been indulging in something stronger than
lemonade.”
“ Do you get those sensations now ? ”
“No; they have quite left me, thanks to
Clements Tonic, and I have nothing to thank but
that medicine for ridding me of my cough, and
ultimately restoring me to the very soundest
health. So now you can publish these facts
broadcast.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, GEORGE ALFRED WILLIS, of 7 Bourke Street, Zet-
land, in the State of New South Wales, Commonwealth of
Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, and that it contains
and is a true and faithful account of my illness and cure
by Clements Tonic, and also contains my full permission
to publish in any way my statements—which I give
voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same
to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths
Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Waterloo, this twenty-
seventh day of February, one thousand nine hundred
and eight, before me,
5] WILLIAM ALLEN, J.P.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jenny.
When Jenny feels good-natured how I love to see
her smile !
She is bright, and she is open, and so wholly free
from guile.
But when Jenny’s really naughty she’s a perfect
little dear,
And the language that she uses is an awful treat
to hear.
Then her golden hair goes curling in a most
delightful way,
And there comes a sudden lightning in her eyes
of sparkling grey ;
When she calls me names I listen, and I mostly
can agree
That I am the pig and donkey of her wrath’s
menagerie.
When her step is like a whirlwind and her tongue
is like a fire,
Then I feel that pretty Jenny is my very heart’s
desire;
Then the broken tea-cups scatter, and the jam is
on my shirt.
And Jenny is a cyclone in a whirling frilly skirt.
But the climax of enjoyment and the summit of
delight
Is when all her railings falter and her hands she
clenches tight,
When she’s said the things she wanted, and has
told me all I am,
And she stamps her wicked slipper on the rug
and utters “Damn.”
MAOKILAND.
D. M. W.
The Little Grinning Devil.
There’s a Little Grinning Devil jeers our efforts to
be good—
He has been our boon companion ev’ry day from
cradlehood ;
And in school, or church, or temple—aye, no,
matter when or where,
The Little Grinning Devil is predestined to be
there !
Let us will where we may wander, let us wander
where we will—
But the Little Grinning Devil is our bosom com-
rade still !
He’s the mocker of our morals, he’s the siren of our
sins
(He’s a Cunning Little Devil who contemptuously
grins !);
He’s the champion disturber when our virtues are
serene—
He’s the most outrageous demon that the world
has ever seen !
He’s at daggers drawn with sorrow and on kissing
terms with glee—
In that Little Devil’s absence what prosaic things
we’d be !
Never yet was maid so proper, never yet was maid
so chaste—
But the Little Grinning Devil had his arm around
her waist!
Never yet was maid so simple, never yet was
maid so shy—
But the Little Grinning Devil caught and kissed
her on the sly !
And he didn’t hear her protests, and he didn’t
mind her screams—
For the Little Grinning Devil was the Lover of
Her Dreams !
Many men have tried to squelch him on the holy
altar stairs—
But beheld him in the pulpit, where he parodies
our pray’rs !
And in deep and dark seclusion we have striven
to atone—
But the Little Grinning Devil hates to see a man
alone !
He’s the Unsubjected Ego that runs riot through
our lives—
And the gods must pack their baggage when his
Majesty arrives !
He’s the little fiend that gladdens, he’s the tiny
imp that cheers —
What though he digs us pitfalls in the Passage of
the Years !
For he is our patron Devil —and, regarding him as
such,
For myself I’ll make confession that I love him
very much !
Leave the Ten Commandments idle, put the
Bibles on the shelves—
For the Little Grinning Devil is a Portion of
Ourselves !
N.S.W.
GILROONEY.
The Real People’s Land.
Do you know the real Real People’s land ?
I will take you there if you so command
To strip the veil from the past away,
To draw forth souls from the light of day ;
To show where the roots of motives twine.
Are you afraid lest a vagrant ray
Upon the hearts in our own breasts play,
Showing the self hid in yours and mine?
Would we enter the real Real People’s land ?
We must hold no brief at the world’s command,
No painted smile for the lying lips ;
Truth is stark from soles to finger tips.
No gracious fashion, no pretty phrase—
The bones peep white from the built-in vaults,
Lank skeletons of our buried faults
That were pink and white in the fleshly days.
It is far—-the real Real People’s land.
Shall we seek it, dear one, hand in hand?
All that the light of its suns can prove
In my heart for you is an honest love;
But you shake your head, and I hear you say,
With man’s mistrust for a woman’s whim
(For you like your lamps rose-shaded, dim)
“Tis not well to go there ev’ry day !”
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tub Bulletin would oblige the paver
(and, to an extent, themselves) by talcing some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no- ease be to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor. Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “ copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
ojiicials. The person may not be there; the official
always is.

THURSDAY, AUGUST 26, 1909.
ALFRED DEAKIN FIXES UP A
FINANCIAL ARRANGEMENT.
After much conflict, the secret caucus of Pre-
miers has arrived at a sort of arrangement as to
the future division of the tariff revenue. Two of
the seven Premiers who made the arrangement
hang on by a majority of one ; and three, includ-
ing the two who have a majority of one (the figures
sound like the tariff at a cheap variety show) de-
pend entirely on the recently purchased support
of their previous enemies. So the gathering
wasn’t a strikingly representative affair. Alfred
Deakin met the State Premiers in the position of
the man who rushes headlong into the Hebrew’s
office and says he must have the money at once ;
and the State Premiers met Deakin as does the
Hebrew whose premises are thus rushed, and
said, like the Hebrew, that if the “monish”
was wanted in such haste as all that the
interest would be high. The Braddon clause
can’t be abolished except with the consent of the
State Parliaments, until the end of 1910 —16
months ahead. For another 16 months the States
are entitled to three-fourths of the Customs and
Excise revenue whether the Commonwealth sinks
or swims, and whether the Commonwealth pays
old-age pensions or not. After that time the
Commonwealth can, if it desires, keep all tile
Customs and Excise revenue. Therefore any
Australian Government which can keep financi-
ally afloat for another 16 months can make its
own terms. But Deakin has failed to keep afloat.
He doesn’t float any better than an anchor. As
the price of office lie abandoned all ideas of rais-
ing further revenue by a Federal progressive land-
tax or income-tax. He joined himself with the
crowd which says that the builders of the Consti-
tution never “contemplated ” Federal direct
taxation ; also to the crowd which objects to
the raising of any further revenue by means
of the tariff. With so many possible
sources of revenue cut off, the Fused Government
found itself, the other day, with a prospective
shortage of £1,200,000, and to face a general elec-
tion with that incubus would be little different
from political suicide. The “monish” was wanted
at once. So a Fuse, with a hunted expression in
its eye, put this question to the State Premiers :
“How much a year for ever afterwards (to be
paid by the community at large) is the price at
which you will relax the Braddon clause now,
and thus enable us to get through and possibly
keep our billets?”
* * • *
The price was high—naturally. A person who
seems as if wild dogs or police were after him can’t
raise the wind instantly at a low rate. Even the
most conscientious pawnbroker charges extra if
the customer who is trying to pawn the cruet
looks as if he had just committed murder.
The arrangement arrived at is that the State
Governments are to receive for ever (though
that really only means until the people see tit to
alter the Constitution again) £1 ss. per inhabitant
per annum out of the Customs and Excise receipts.
(There are certain temporary concessions to Wes-
tralia, but they don’t seriously alter the situation.)
Then the Commonwealth becomes responsible for
old-age pensions—hithertoa purely Stateliability—
and on present calculations they represent about
9s. per inhabitant per annum. That isequivalent
to leaving things on the old footing and guaran-
teeing the States an annual subsidy of 345. per
head. If the Commonwealth wants 16s. per in-
habitant for itself—though unless there is to be
very heavy Federal direct taxation the amount is
absurdly small, being only about half what Britain
spends on defence alone—it must raise 50s. per
inhabitant yearly by the tariff. That would
really leave it just about where it would be if the
Braddon clause had been made perpetual. If
the Commonwealth wants 265. per inhabitant
annually for itself without resorting to direct
taxation, then, in order to get it and to pay the
State subsidies and old-age pensions, it must raise
£3 annually per head by tariff means —yet 265.
per head is less than the amount which Britain
spends on defence alone. Roughly speaking, this
is the situation on the £3 basis
Allotted thus.
Popu- Amount to State O. A. Cominon-
lation. be raised. Subsidies. Pensions, wealth.
5,000,000 £15,000,000 £6,250,000 £2,250,000 £6,500,000
10,000,000 30,000,000 12,500,000 4,500,000 13,000,000
20,000,000 60,000,000 25,000,000 9,000,000 26,000,000
And so on ad infinitum.
The number of objections that can be urged
against this wretchedly incompetent scheme are
almost past counting. To begin with, it assumes
the raising of an almost impossible tariff
revenue. Germany is a rich country with a
massive tariff, yet it only collects about 20s.
annually from each German out of Customs and
Excise. France is a rich country with a high
tariff, yet it only raises about 10s. per head per
annum from Customs ; and when everything that
can by any reasonable pretence be called
Excise is placed under the tariff heading the
total doesn’t seem more than 255. per head. The
United States, high as its tariff is and rich as the
country is, only contrives to raise about 13s. 6d.
per Yankee annually by Customs and 12s. 6d. per
annual Yankee in the way of “internal revenue”
—making 265. in all. The fact is that Aus-
tralia’s Customs revenue is wholly abnormal
and artificial. It might be briefly described
as bogus. This country imports far more
per head than it can pay for—far more
than it will be able to import when things settle
down to a fair, ordinary level. The enormous
amount it has raised by land sales, the immense
gold output and the stupendous foreign borrow-
ings have enabled it to buy foreign goods at a rate
in proportion to its population which no other
country has ever approached, and these im-
ports have temporarily bloated the tariff returns
out of all recognition. They suggest the un-
healthy enlargement of one who has been three
weeks drowned. The falling away of land sales,
the decline of the gold output, and the fact that
there must be a limit to foreign borrowing make
it absolutely certain that this country will have to
buy less abroad and make more goods for itself,
and a stupendous falling away in the tariff re-
venue per head is therefore as certain as the sun-
sec. In fact, it is visibly beginning already.
Between 1903 and 1905 the drop amounted to
about a million and a quarter, despite the growth
of population. Then the new tariff shoved the
figures up again; but as local manufactures con-
tinued to take the place of imports, another
decline set in immediately, and is still in pro-
gress. When things reach their true level, it will
probably be found that Alfred Dkakin, as the
price of a temporary aid to his muddled and
incompetent finances, has promised the State
Governments permanently more out of the tariff
revenue than the whole tariff revenue will amount
to a few years hence.
■*•-***
Regarded as a permanent arrangement that 255.
per head per annum is superfluity as well as im-
possibility. It takes far more money per head
to govern a thinly-peopled than a thickly-peopled
country, for the simple reason that while a town
of 1000 people may require a magistrate and a fire
brigade, oneof 3,000,000 people doesn’t necessarily
require 3000 magistrates and 3000 fire-brigades.
N.S.W., with 1,500,000 people, may require
£1,875,000 a year, or 255. per head, to carry on
with, but it is sheer lunacy to. suppose that a
future N.S.W., with 15,000,000 people, and with
a State Government that has no land or sea
defence, or lighthouses, or postal and telegraphic
extensions to pay for, can require £18,750,000 to
struggle along with—that in addition to its rail-
way and tramway profits, land-tax, income-tax,
land revenues, stamp and probate duties and sun-
dries. Superfluity is no name for such an
arrangement.
* * * *
The agreement which Alfred Deakin has
fixed up as the price of relief from the financial
difficulties of the next few months gives the State
Governments a first mortgage on the existing
tariff revenue as well as on all future increase of
revenue. The States are to be preference share-
holders. or debenture-holders. If the revenue
drops, the loss is to be wholly borne by the Com-
monwealth ; if it rises the States are to share in
the profit, for it can be fairly assumed that the
revenue won’t rise except along with an increase
of population. Moreover, the State claims upon
the tariff revenue will increase whether the
tariff revenue increases or not; and they will
increase even while the tariff revenue is growing
less. The demands of the States won’t stop at
the three-fourths fixed by the old Braddon
clause ; they will almost certainly get far beyond
that, and will probably amount in time to the
whole lot or more than the whole lot. If a State
brings in 20,000 poor and frugal immigrants they
may add very little to the tariff returns, but they
will certainly add £25.000 a year to the State’s
claims upon the tariff returns. Assuming that
an immigrant lives after arrival here for an aver-
age of 30 years, that means a total bill for
£750,000 on account of these 20,000 frugal immi-
grants, to say nothing of the bill for their children
and their children’s children, and quiteignoring the
additional claims they will make on the old-age
pensions fund. Yet with every addition tothepopu-
lation the tariff receipts per head will grow less.
The gold yield, the land sales revenue and the
loan money, which made a notable addition to the
importing capacity per head of 500,000 people,
won’t make much visible addition to the import-
ing capacity of 5,000,000 people, even if these
three sources of income were permanent—which
they are not.
* * * *
The proposed agreement can’t legally come into
force until a popular vote is taken upon the sub-
ject at next General Election, which is due seven
or eight months hence. Nevertheless it is to come
into force at once in an informal fashion, and the
payments to the States will be immediately cut
down. But they won’t be cut down very far.
The Fused Government sees a deficit of £1,200,000
ahead as the result of its incompetent financing,
notwithstanding the fact that it takes no account
in its estimates of the cost of the Northern
Territory, the two transcontinental railways and
the Federal capital ; that it largely shirks the
defence question and wholly shirks the immigra-
tion question. In return for pawning Australia’s
whole future to the State Treasurers, the Fused
Government is to be allowed this year an extra
£OOO,OOO. That will leave it with £GOO.OOO of a
deficit. Still the reduction of the deficit may
help it a little in the miserable struggle
to retain its Fused job. But if the people
decline to sanction the proposed arrangement all
this extra money will have to be paid back to the
State Governments, and then the Deakin-Cook
accounts will look a lot worse—will look, in fact,
as bad as they do now. Until the people sanc-
tion the proposed change any extra money which
the Fused Government may acquire under it is
mere trust money, so the Fused crowd will simply
appeal to the community as an unreliable bailee
who is short in his accounts. And it is quite pos-
sible that the people will repudiate the whole
business. They will certainly repudiate it if they
realise that for the sake of enabling the Fused
push to make a better showing before the electors
it is proposed to sacrifice all the benefits that were
expected when the Braddon clause ran out, and
to create an ever-increasing liability which will
in the end swallow up everything.
Even if the people endorse the proposal, the
endorsement isn’t likely to hold good for more
than three years at the outside. By that time the
community will have had time to realise what an
impossible responsibility it has been fooled into
un.lertaking on behalf of the State Treasurers,
and another amendment of the Constitution-
undertaken, perhaps, without any pretence of
consulting the State authorities —will sweep the
whole arrangement away. There is no means by
which a subsidy agreement can be nailed down
and made fixed and permanent—none whatever.
The immovable system of the Medes and Persians
is played out. The only way in which finality
can be arrived at is by the complete separation of
State and Federal finance—by the States abandon-
ing all claims upon the tariff revenue and per-
suading the Commonwealth to take over their
debts, or part of their debts, as a quid pro quo.
This was William Lyne’s proposition, and it would
have spelt finality; but unfortunately the benighted
State Premiers couldn’t see the point. If the debts
were once taken over by the Commonwealth that
institution might want some day to hand them
back, but the short and effective reply of the
State Parliaments would be: “They- are your
debts now. If you don’t like to keep them you
can drop them or repudiate them, and the dis-
grace will be yours. But we are done with them.”
So there would be a definite end to the financial
controversy, but there is no other definite end in
sight.
THE LAMENTABLE IMPORTANCE OF
UNIMPORTANT PEOPLE.
Australia has lately been annoyed by the habit
which some very small people have of mistaking
themselves for the entire community. The most
notable fly that got in the road was Mr. Wade,
State Premier of N.S.W. When the secret caucus
of Premiers met recently at Melbourne the pain-
fully affable Deakin propounded a theory whereby
the six State Parliaments, by passing six Bills
through 12 Houses of Legislature, with three
readings of each Bill in each House, should
transfer to the Commonwealth the sole control of
industrial legislation ; and the Australian Parlia-
ment, by passing one Bill through two more
Houses, with three readings in each House,
should accept the said control. That is
one way by' which a very essential re-
form might be brought about, and not only
is it a large and circuitous way, but it has the
serious drawback that it embodies no reference
to the people, therefore the State Legislatures
might surrender the authority in question when
the community really didn’t want it to be sur-
rendered. The other way of arriving at the same
end is to leave the State Legislatures out of con-
sideration altogether ; to enact-the transfer of in-
dustrial legislation to the Commonwealth by an
Act of the Australian Parliament; and then to
submit that Act, as an amendment of the Con-
stitution, to the people for acceptance or rejection.
Thus the people would decide, whereas, unless
the people were directly consulted, they would
have no chance to explain their views. The
amendment of the Constitution is distinctly the
better idea of the two, though the lamentable
affability of Deakin inclines him, as usual, to the
worst way.
* * »
About the need for the transfer of industrial
legislation to the Federal power there is no pos-
sible doubt. Australia is divided into six States,
and five of them have made some pretence of
dealing with this question by creating Wages
Boards and the like. The sixth State (backward
Tasmania) has no industrial legislation worth
mentioning, and wages thex-e are just as low and
hours just as long as the employer can contrive to
make them. Nowadays the Commonwealth has a
uniform tariff and no border duties, but while the
State which pays fair wages is protected against
the foreign sweater it has no protection against
the inter-State sweater. The ultimate result must
be that the most work will go to the place
which pays the worst wages, and so there
will arise a diseased form of internal com-
petition which can be very little better than
the worst variety of foreign competition. Out of
this evil grows another, for old age pensions are
now a Federal concern, chargeable to the country
at large. In the extra low-wage State the
worker is least able to provide for his old age by
his own efforts, so it follows that the low-wage
State will have an absurdly high proportion of
pensioners, while the better-managed State, with
a lower proportion of pensioners, will have to
carry part of the low-wage State’s burden. In
other words, a low-wage State like Tasmania will
become a huge pauper, receiving alms from places
like N.S.W., Queensland and S. A. These States
have already one horrible and expensive pauper
on their hands, for the great land monopolies of
Victoria have driven the young agriculturists out
wholesale, and made Bent’s country pre-eminently
the land of old derelicts. Victoria’s proportion
of old-age pensioners is so absurdly high that it
promises to be very considerable of a pauper,
living on the charity of its neighbors. But that
unpleasant fact is no reason why Australia should
be searching for another pauper. The remedy for
one kind of pauper is a Federal progressive land-
tax to prevent any one State becoming essentially
the home of land monopolies. The remedy for
the other is Federal control of industrial legisla-
tion to prevent any one State becoming essentially
the home of sweated labor.
» * » •
As might naturally be expected, State Premier
Wade, of N.S.W., the very ordinary lawyer with
the harsh countenance, rose in vigorous opposition
to Alp. Deakin’s mild proposal. Wade points
out that industrial legislation was left to the State
Governments by the original Constitution, and
so it is one of the State Rights of which Wade
is the self-appointed defender. If Tasmania can
be induced to establish a Wages Board system or
some similar device, Wade remarks that the
States will all have industrial legislation of some
kind, and if there are six different varieties of
industrial legislation in the Commonwealth,
Wade badly wants to know what on earth anybody
can want with one uniform system of industrial legis-
lation. In any case, Wade is prepared to fight to
the last against “Federal encroachments.” It
doesn’t matter to Wade that there is no guarantee
that Tasmania will follow the lead of the other
States. Nor does it signify to Wade that the
different State courts fix different hours, wages and
conditions ; that no two States can agree as to
what trades should be regulated and what
should not; that there are all manner
of discrepancies as to whether awards should
extend over the whole State or over part of the
State'; that the laws are wildly diverse ; and that
if one State proposes a reform in its methods the
chance of getting the other five to agree to exactly
the same reform is practically nil. These valu-
ations may be inconvenient and disastrous to the
Australian people, but in the view of Wade
“State Rights” are of far more importance than
the well-being of the Australian people. In this
matter the amazing woodenness of Wade and his
class asserts itself. They can see the advantage
of a uniform Customs tariff because that was in
the original Constitution. They can’t see the
advantages of a uniform industrial tariff because
that wasn't in the original Constitution. Change
of any kind is abhorrent to the Wades of this
world.
a * * »
The State Rights creed is such a gigantic and
insolent denial of the self-governing privileges of
the Australian nation that a more crude and law-
less community might be moved to protest with
violence, and hang some prominent State Righter
from the top of an herb. The people who
desire to add the control of industrial legislation
to the Federal powers, or to make any other
additions to the Federal powers, by amendment
of the Constitution, do not propose to trespass in
any way on the rights of the people. They simply
propose to ask the people by referendum whether
they prefer Federal industrial legislation or not.
That is the only way in which the Federal Con-
stitution can be altered or enlarged—by the vote
of the people. It is suggested that if the people
badly want Federal industrial legislation they
should have the chance to say so, and if they
don’t want it they should be allowed to explain
that they prefer to do without it. Therefore
those who advocate this amendment of the
Constitution desire most particularly to respect
the rights of the people, and of the States which
are the people. On the other side of the case
stand Gregory Wade and his State Rights
friends. Gregory Wade can’t see what possible
claim the people can have to govern themselves,
or what claim anybody has to govern them except
Gregory Wade and individuals of his sort. It
may be—it probably is—that the people of the
State of N.S.W. want Federal control of indus-
trial affairs. If they do they can’t announce the
fact clearly at either a State or a Federal election,
for when two dozen questions are all mixed to-
gether no one can, simply by voting for Smith or
against Jones, fully express his views on all of
them, or even on any one of them. In the
absence of the plain, business-like referendum,
the only way in which it is possible that the
people of N.S.W. or any other State may declare
their opinions on Federal industrial legislation or
any other matter is by the taking of a vote on a
proposed amendment of the Federal Constitution.
And here Gregory Wade rushes in to explain
that it would be an outrage upon the rights of
N.S.W. and the other States if the people of
N.S.W. and the other States were permitted to
freely express their views on any such subject.
In the matter of encroachment upon the rights of
the States Wade and Co., who thus attempt to
prevent the State’s citizens declaring how they
want to be governed, seem the most bumptious
and impertinent gang of encroachers that Aus-
tralia possesses.
* » * *
A good deal of the trouble lies in the utter
inability of people like Wade to understand
the position they occupy in the scheme of things.
State Rights are certainly very real and very
important. They consist of the right of the
States —that is of the people who compose the
States and who also compose Australia—to say as
clearly as possible how they desire to manage their
own affairs. They include the right of the people
to say how far they want their affairs to be
managed in a Federal sense by Deakin or Fisher,
and how far they want them to be supervised in a
mere local sense by Kidston, Wade, John
Weary, Peake, Newton Moore and Elliott
Lewis. If the Australian Parliament passed
a Bill providing for the transfer to the
Federal authority of the control of industrial
legislation it must submit it to a popular vote
before it could become law. But the proposal to
submit any such measure to the people of the States
is denounced by Wade as an encroachment on
State Rights, because, in Wade’s view, State
Rights consist of the right of Wade to deprive his
State of the right to even consider any proposal of
which Wade doesn’t approve. This viewof thecase
suggests a wholly inordinate and absurd concep-
tion on Wade’s part of Wade’s importance.
Wade may be very important, but he isn’t so
important as all that. The entire doctrine of
State Rights is based on the off-hand assumption
by Wade and Kidston and John Weary and
their tribe that the people of the States must, as
a matter of course, want to be ruled as much as
possible by Kidston and Wade and John Weary
and co., and as little as possible by Deakin or
Fisher. They profess that this fact is so self-
evident that any attempt to permit the people to
express any opinion to the contrary is an outrage
on the people of Australia, who are also the States
of Australia. And, unfortunately, Alfred Deakin
is too futile and too affable to treat these
impertinent little tyrants as they deserve.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
Imperialists and Confidence-Tricksters Too.
In the words of Reuter (17/8/’O9), " the arrange-
ment between the Admiralty and the Common-
wealth in connection with the Defence Conference
is entirely satisfactory to Australian national
sentiment.” Reuter is correct. The British
Admiralty, according to the obviously inspired
cable message of last week, in direct opposition to
th© views of our local amateur strategists, has
laid it down that Australia shall be defended in
future, not by a more or less negligible section ot
the British navy, but by its own fleet. The sea-
defence of the Commonwealth is to be put up,
not in the distant Baltic or North Bea, but in the
adjacent Pacific. The personnel of the ships
will necessarily be mainly British for a while.
But "the establishment of one or more train-
ing colleges ” is projected, in order that Aus-
tralians may be taught how to man their
own vessels, and, as soon as may be, an inter-
change of naval officers will begin between British
and Australian warships. The valuelessness to
Australia of a Dreadnought is recognised ; and as
the United Kingdom doesn’t want one of these
massive craft from this country, the Dreadnought
" gift ” money is to be allocated to the construc-
tion of a couple of fast cruisers which shall
operate in Australian waters, and form part and
parcel of the Australian fleet. And, last and most
important, the said fleet, when it eventuates, is to
belong absolutely to Australia. S. M. Herald
put the position fairly when it remarked (19/8/’09):
Even a possible Labor Administration will find nothing
to grumble at in an arrangement that leaves complete
control of our ships to us in peace time, and leaves it to
us to place them, we hope unreservedly, at the disposal of
the Empire in time of war.
But there are some features of this latest develop-
ment of the local naval defence position, which
the Herald omitted to mention. Three months
ago Alfred Deakin decided to put the Labor
Government out of office and seize the Prime
Ministership himself. His expressed reason was
Fisher’s mad and dingoistie conduct in opposing
the Age and Sydney TELEGRAPH-fomented Dread-
nought idiocy. Deakin owned to being in agree-
ment with most of the fiscal and industrial
legislation which Fisher’s party projected. Also
he favored the same party’s proposals for coastal
defence, tie found but one defect in the Fisher
make-up and that of his party-—namely their in-
difference to the craze for giving Britain a Dread-
nought. So Deakin went regretfully away and
fused with Joe Cook. Now, consider Deakin’s
position. The Motherland refuses to Vie given a
Dreadnought wherewith to defend itself from
Germany. Nor will it accept any other present
of the kind. Bo Deakin is holding a Prime
Ministership which he acquired under false pre-
tences. He dispossessed Fisher of the job on the
plea that the immediate presentation of a Dread-
nought to Britain was a crying imperial necessity.
The whole idea has been turned down at the
headquarters of Empire for the blamed foolishness
it is. The headquarters of Empire has decided
firmly that in this matter Fisher was a wise man,
and that Deakin and Cook were fools—and
Fused ones at that. Yet, even after this
severe snub, Deakin bangs on to office as
though the move that landed him there had
been enthusiastically ratified in the Cold Land.
Equally anomalous is the position of Mayor
Allen Taylor, of Sydney, and his amazing
“ Dreadnought Fund.” Even as Alfred Deakin
collected the Prime Ministership on the false pre-
tence that he was going to use it to provide the
Motherland with a battleship, so Allen Taylor
has, unless the Anti-Sosh papers lie, collected
nearly £lOO,OOO of N.S.W. cash on the same
bogus understanding. The last shadow of a possi-
bility of Australia spending a farthing on a Dread-
nought or other article for the purposes of purely
British defence, vanished with the publication, last
week, of the Defence Conference’s decision. Cer-
tainly so-called Dreadnought money will fie dis-
bursed; but it is to be used for a purpose (the
augmenting, namely, of the infant Australian
navy) which is calculated to drive the normal
Dreadnoughter into a frenzy of passion. It
is painful to conceive the frame of mind of the
excitable and ungrammatical Abbey Dangar when
lie learns that his £lO.OOO may fie spent, after all,
on the "miserable proposal” for an Australian
fleet. And great will be the grief of Willy
Kelly when he appreciates that his £lOO is to
travel the same route ; and the anguish of Little
Amy from Leichhardt, when she grasps that her
3d. is to tie misapplied, will be heart-rending to
witness. There is only one logical and honest
course for Alfred Deakin to pursue, viz., to
resign his unjustly-acquired billet forthwith ; and
as for Allen Taylor, the sooner he desists from
wringing cash from Ma State Imperialists by
false representations, and restores such coin as he
has already wrung from them, the better for his
good fame.

The Proposed Deviation of the Transcon-
tinental Railway.
Some fatuous people want to turn the transconti-
nental line into a zig-zag, in order to reach the
"beautiful country” in sou’-west Queensland.
Hereunder “ Mallarapoonie ” describes some of
the aforesaid "beautiful country”:-
Six or seven miles northerly by the Bedourie mailman’s
bridle-track from the little decayed township of Birdsville,
at Queensland’s back gate, stands Mount Lewis—barren,
bald and grim.
The view from the summit of that naked limestone
knob is rather extensive, and can be highly recommended
as an object lesson to all who favor the deviation of Aus-
tralia’s proposed transcontinental railway through
south-western Queensland. I have gazed out on some
pitikss scenery in different parts of Australia in
my time, but I hand the Mount Lewis view the
damper for desolation, barrenness arid the blight of utter
despair. Good seasons or bad, the view is much the same,
and is characteristic of a great deal of the sou’-west
corner.
Eastward from the summit of Mount Lewis one looks
for miles across the innumerable ensts of undulating
sandhills, trending mostly north and south. At about
12 miles the regularity of the sand-dune succession is
broken by a long low line of dark-green river timber, which,
winding among the hummocks of glittering sand, comes
out of the haze of the N.E. horizon, and sinks into haze
again. This is the fringe of the scattered channels of the
Diameritina.
Beyond the river channels, beyond the dark fringe of
timber, and looming in strong- relief above the distant
green, rise the bare, wind-swept summits of furtherdunes
—hummock beyond hummock to the horizon —red-grey
sand, baldly glistening in the sun.
To the south, south-west and west lies a sea of sand
which sweeps against the foot-slopes of Mount Lewis, as
an ocean upon some headland.
Sand, sand, sand as far as the eye can reach, red-grev
and forbidding.
Sandhill is reared behind sandhill, crest beyond crest,
sand-wave beyond sand-wave, out into purplish blue,
where a brazen, cloudless sky stoops down to the shimmer-
ing wastes.
Far to the south-west, hardly distinguishable by the
unaided eye, hangs a pall of deeper purple. It liangs
over that terrible tract—Sturt’s Stony Desert.
Northward, from west to east of north, outspreading
from the feet of Mount Lewis to the remote horizon, piti-
less and vast, and only dotted here and there with naked
limestone knobs, unbroken by tree or shrub, flat, water-
less, and drear, stretches the Oacory Desert, arid,
sinister and red.
Desolation and silence environ Mount Lewis, and
stretch to the horizon and beyond. It is the Great Dead
Heart.
And why it should be proposed to drag a railway
through those lonely wastes only Heaven and some
cranky politicians know.

For Public Information.
This paper took prompt measures against those
organs of public opinion which reprinted the
gross libel that had its origin in the Brisbane
Worker. As a result, the Sydney Watchman on
August 19 published the following in its own
columns, and those of a number of other papers in
Queensland and elsewhere, in addition to paying
all legal costs :
THE BRISBANE “WORKER’S” SHOCKING
REVELATION.
Slandering “ The Bulletin.”
CORRECTION AND APOLOGY.
Under the heading of “ A Shocking Revelation,” an
article appeared a couple of weeks ago in the Brisbane
Worker, in which it was stated:—
“People who wondered why Sydney Bulletin so
suddenly ceased its condemnations of the Kid-Piiilp coali-
tion, need wonder no longer. A question asked and
answered in the Queensland Assembly last week has
solved the mystery. The sum of £2O per issue for 12
months is being ladled out to the red-faced Bully for
advertising the advantages of this State as a field for
settlement. Just prior to the fixing up of the contract
the Bulletin, in the excess of its disgust at Kidston’s
coalition conduct, had declared it wouldn’t be found dead
in the same paddock with him. And those who still re-
tained faith in the democracy of the great Protectionist
organ that is printed on Freetrade paper, rejoiced exceed-
ingly thereat. With the formidable Bully on their side,
they felt that the coalition would soon receive its quietus.
But, alas, a change came o’er the spirit of their dream!
The Bully’s denunciations of Kidston’s treachery suddenly
petered out. A deathly silence about the coalition per-
vaded its columns all at once.”
This article was reprinted without comment in the
Watchman. We find now that the Worker’s charge is
absolutely without foundation, and we very much regret
the republication of the article, and take this opportunity
to unreservedly apologise for any reflection cast on the
reputation of Tim Bulletin.
Tim Bulletin points out that it never made any con-
tract with the Coalition Government, that its contract
was dated three months before the coalition took place ;
that when it denounced the Kiustox-Piiilp Government
it was denouncing a profitable advertiser, and that not
only was it not in any way to cease its hostility to the
Queensland Coalition, but that a perusal of its pages
would show that its hostility had never ceased. The
Worker story it denounces as a complete fabrication.
On inspecting the documents, the Watchman finds that
The Bulletin’s account of the circumstances is absolutely
correct.
VIEW IN THE MACDONNELL RANGES, CENTRE OF AUSTRALIA, where the alleged
“Desert Railway” (Port Darwin to Port Augusta) is to pass.
Victorian Bushrangers : A.D. 1909.
The unconscionable cadgers who live on the big
and mostly unused blocks in the Rodney irriga-
tion district (Vic.) moved even Agricultural
Minister Graham to violent language the other
day. When Elwqod .Mead reached Australia,
that competent irrigationist speedily found that,
but for a few inconsiderable exceptions, Victoria’s
irrigation policy has so far been a swindle and a
fraud, worked for the advantage of the land-
owner. Mead’s idea was that the State should
bring water along main channels and charge every
landowner enough to pay expenses and interest on
the outlay, and that it should also make him pay
whether he used the fluid or not. Likewise he
reckoned it a fair thing that the Anti-
Bosh landowner should construct his own dis-
tributing channels, just as he has to do in every
other country where irrigation is practised. Here
he struck his first snag. The landowners in the
already irrigated districts—Rodney, Bwan Hill,
.and the like —-had had their distributing channels
built for them by a foolish Government—which
was mostly Duncan Gillies and Alfred the
Affable—so the men in the new and proposed dis-
tricts demanded the same concession. Mead had
to do as his Government bosses told him. “ Very
good,” he said. "we will bring the water right to
their doors, but they will have to pay working ex-
penses and interest charges on the whole amount
expended.” That sum he worked out at ss. per
acre per annum per foot depth of water, which is -
about half the sum charged elsewhere. And not
only would the new men have to pay it, but the
landowners of Rodney and other "old” dis-
tricts would also pay on the same basis.
As soon as Rodney had grasped the idea, it arose
as one robber and roared. Then it sppke to
Minister McKenzie, Swan Hill’s member, who
listened attentively and replied softly. Then it
tackled Murray, who sent up Elwood Mead,
Minister Graham going with him. But Rodney
declined to listen to Mead. It had a list of ques-
tions it said it wanted answered, but it absolutely
declined to show Mead the list or ask Mead the
questions. Nevertheless, like its sow at home, it
was quite firm on one point—it wanted to return
to its wallow. "Gimme back me Trust,” it said.,
“ Gimme back me Trust.” And then Graham let
himself go in this good plain English :
I am surprised at the deliberate attempt now being
made in Rodney to rob the State of a large sum of money.
It is nothing but robbery. . . The Rodney irrigation
scheme has cost the State £620,000, out of which they are
required to pay interest on £270,000 only, the State
having shouldered the balance of £350,000. When they
originally became a Trust they voluntarily offered to pay
£32,472 per annum for the use of the water. They have
never kept their word, and now, when we offer them
double the quantity of water and better terms, they
refuse to pay even £21,000 perannum. Why, in Heaven’s
name, should the waterless farmers in the Mallee and the
flooded-out settlers in Gippsland, who have had far less
State assistance, be taxed to bear the burdens of these
comparatively luxuriously-situated landowners?
Among other grievances, it seems, Rodney com-
plains that the Trust won’t construct any further
subsidiary channels until the land to be served
is cut up and settled. The landowner wants
channels built by the State all over his hold-
ing ; that done, he could sell at a gorgeous
price while carefully passing the bill, in the
shape of increased charges for these smaller
channels, on to the purchaser. In other words,
he wants to have the channels made so that he
can charge the purchaser of the subdivision for
them, though he knows quite well that the
purchaser will have to pay the State in the shape
of increased rates. But fortunately Mead knows
the won’t-irrigate sharks, and so their game is
spoiled—provided always Mead gets the requisite
backing from Government and Parliament.

More “ Desert Railway.”
Providence has dropped a lot of rain recently
upon Australia, but very little of it has fallen
upon the route of the proposed deviation of the
transcontinental railway through Western Queens-
land and Western N.S.W. ; and the deviaters,
who want to turn a great national work into aline
built for the purely local profit of certain States,
feel hurt at this omission on the part of Provi-
dence. The deviaters are short of arguments,
though they hope that sheer parish-minded selfish-
ness may get them votes, and if the torrents which
have, lately been wasting themselves at Ballarat
had fallen instead upon Birdsville, and given them
an opportunity just fora moment to declare that
their awful route wasn’t such .a desert after all,
they would have felt better. But Birdsville, in
south-western Queensland, the country through
which it is proposed that the railway
deviation should go, remains dry. Birdsville,
in fact, almost always remains dry. During the
hist 14 years its average rainfall has been less
than 6& inches per year, and in two years out of
the last seven the year’s record has been down to
about inches. Windorah and Thargomindah,
also in south-western Queensland, but much
nearer the coast, average about Ilf inches each
per annum, though in 1902 Windorah had less
than 6in., and in 1905 it had less than 4in. ;
and Thargomindah had less than inches in 1902
and less than 4f inches in 1905. It is with the
view of bringing the transcontinental line, at great
additional length and cost, through this West
Queensland country that Australia is urged to
abandon the straight, short line from north to
south, and build a wandering, dissipated sort of
railway into the Eastern States. After the line
has passed through the country lying between
Birdsville and Thargomindab, where the rainfall
drops at times to 3in. per annum, it is to go on
into N.S.W. and gladden the country up by
Milparinka in the north of the State (about Bfin.
of rain per annum), and Wilcannia (lOin. per
annum) and join with Broken Hill (9in. per
annum) and finally hurry on to Euston and Went-
worth in the south of the State (rainfall about
12in.). Almost all these places have a lower rain-
fall than Alice Springs, which represents prac-
tically the centre of Australia and the centre of a
direct Port Darwin-Port Augusta railway, and
this point makes it hard to build up a case for the
deviation, seeing that it will (1) involve much
extra mileage and cost; (2) involve leaving most
of the Federal property of the Territory un-
developed ; (3) involve the spending of a vast
amount of the money of all Australia on
the development—if such a country can
be developed—of two States; (4) place the
transcontinental line under four separate
controls—those of the Commonwealth and three
States; (5) deprive the Commonwealth of control
of the southern terminus of the line ; (6) make
connection with Westralia practically impossible ;
and (7) involve the abandonment of some hun-
dreds of miles of line already constructed. If
there had only been floods last week at Birdsville,
instead of Ballarat, so as to make some semblance
of green feed along the deviation track, it would
have been a comfort. But the deviation people
hope that everybody will vote for bringing the
railway as near as possible to his own backyard,
regardless of the interests of Australia ; and as
there are more backyards in Queensland, N.S.W.
and Victoria than in S.A. and Westralia, the
backyard vote may win in the end.
An Army of Children or Adults—Which?
At Goulburn (N.S.W.) last week District Com-
mandant Gordon delivered himself of this bit of
foolishness, and the Fused press applauded it
warmly :
He thought that in training the youth in the early
stages Australia was creating a precedent which would
run through the whole of the Empire. There was the
idea a little time ago that there should he compulsory
universal training from 18 to 21. He thought there was
a flaw in that scheme. They lost the boys from 14 to 18.
They lost a lad at the time he was most fit. Between 18
and 21 most young men were settling themselves in com-
mercial life. That was the very time it had been sug-
gested to take them away from work. A lad should not
be taken away from his work when he was getting into
business.
It is a point on which any civilian who knows
conditions abroad is competent to express an
opinion. France and Germany, the two great
nations which have had practical experience in
the above regard, rebut utterly the theory that
the compulsorily-trained soldier gives most satis-
faction, or, indeed, any satisfaction at all, when
caught in childhood. In the Kaiser’s country
liability to join the Imperial army begins
at 17 years of age. But in effect the
recruit is 20 before his service starts. He
puts in two years’ hard training, and is
then passed into the reserve. There is one
point on which every German whom the writer
has encountered—and he has met some hundreds
in his time—is agreed, namely, that if any defect
is to be found in the Fatherland’s military system
it lies in the enlisting of recruits too early. There
are many Germans who advocate more matured
recruits; practically none who hold that the
young men of the nation should start their train-
ing at an earlier age than that now fixed by law.
That it should be seriously suggested in Germany
—as it is in Australia by the Deakin-Cook Fuse—
that a national army be inaugurated with lads
of 14, would appeal to the nations that know
as a horribly bad jest. And so in France,
where both liability to serve and service itself
starts at 20 years of age. Exploded these 20
years is General Gordon’s idea that it is detri-
mental to the nation to take its youths away for
military training purposes at a time when they
might be settling in commercial life or to
other work. That was the reactionary cry
in France immediately after Sedan. So skilled
and impartial an observer as Hamkrton has
actually picked out (vide Chapter on Patriotic
Duty in "French and English”) as one of the
principal benefits to France of the Franco-Prussian
war, "the healthy revolution concerning uni-
versal service ” which followed upon it. He notes
that in France, under universal service, “ the
humblest military duty is now honorable,” and
the army which was regarded by the people in the
days of the “caste” system with distrust has
come to be looked upon as the pride and strength
of the nation. Nor has trade or individual
wealth suffered in the slightest. This later
passage, from the same work, is suggestive in
another connection :
The absolute unity of sentiment between the military
and civil populations is a great compensation for the
burden of universal service. Another is the increase of
manliness and the improvement of national health. Of
the reality of this improvement I cannot entertain a
doubt, having frequently known young men who had
gained greatly in strength and activity by their military
service, and who felt and acknowledged the benefit.
The sons of peasants acquire Home education in the army.
The sons of gentlemen, besides the benefit of physical
exercise, are often stimulated by the hope of promotion
to improve the education they already possess.
This is the experience of every nation that has
tried the compulsory service system. At the
back of all straight-out local opposition to uni-
versalservice, and behind alltheexpedients to block
anything effective in that line, is, of course, the
typically Anglo-Saxon and fatuous view that
there is no real danger; that we shall muddle
through all right in the end ; and that hence it is
unnecessary to worry. It is impossible to sup-
pose that General Gordon believes in his heart
that Australia is going to create any real defence
by training children of 14 and upwards
in the art of war —though courtesy to the
politicians who control his billet may compel
a certain unwilling acquiescence with their plans.
It is improbable even that Joe Cook, who would
be employed lay-preaching at a Parramatta
street corner if he weren’t Australian Minister for
Defence, considers that this is the soundest way
to ensure the Commonwealth froqadisaster. Simply
Gordon and Cook, like many another, decline to
believe that disaster is conceivable. Yet a few
months back. Admiral Poore, who sees a con-
siderable amount of the world in the course of his
profession, gave Australia a limit of five years in
which to prepare for trouble. It is a tragic thing,
in the circumstances, that incompetent politicians
should continue to blither about an army of small
boys as a main stand-by for the nation, and more
tragic that a responsible soldier should be found
hex - e and there to support and abet such a pro-
posal.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The workers of Geelong, the Slack City of Victoria, are
suffering- through lack of employment. Soup kitchens
have been kept going during the winter as an alleviative
measure. —Information disseminated by Melbourne’s Con-
servative press.
Oh, the chops grow thick on the plains around,
and the blankets are there in droves,
While the butter and cheese in the clover roam
and the beef in the green-stuff roves ;
There’s trouble enough in the slow old town —
there’s suffering and to spare,
But the tucker is plenteous on the plains where
the trespasser must beware.
The trespasser must beware, of course. As it was
in the days of yore,
The man on the perish gets little or less, and the
squatter gets rather more ;
The man with the land is a king, all right, and he
ruffles it, right or wrong ;
But they’re running the rabbit with jugs (for
soup !) in the alleys of old Geelong.
Yes, old Geelong’s in a parlous state, with the
wolf at the worker’s door,
And never a fat, roast sheep inside ; the bed’s on
the naked floor,
The furniture’s down at the auction-rooms, and
the clock is up the spout,
But the king o’ the rolling plains gets three square
meals a day, no doubt !
Three meals a day for the full-fleshed boss, and
something at bed-time, too ;
Though the worker of old Geelong goes off to his
slumbers, afraid and blue,
And the kids support with a pint of soup faint
spirit and fainter flesh,
And his missus can see in her dreams of Heaven
good red meat, corned and fresh.
01), corned or fresh,it’s all the same to the starvers
of old Geelong;
The sheep are fat on the plains around, but the
mutton-demand is strong,
And the worker is hollow enough, God wot, though
the charity toilers stoop
To keep his body in fighting trim, and to save his
soul with soup.

Two clever articles in the August Lone
Hand show that the best brains of Australia are
busy over the Defence problem. And quickened
by judicious applications of D.C.L., the whisky of
the wise, they must reduce that problem to one
word—population. Add the word land, and you
have the necessary magic for conjuring the country
into prosperity and safety. Progress laughs
itself into prosperity at the merry twinkle from
the amber eye of the scholarly brand.*
than about getting married. But now Common-
wealth Statist Knibbs has dug out similar figures
for all his dominions, excepting Tasmania; and
on the whole the other States, taking them in the
lump, can’t afford to shake a stick at N. S. Wales.
A calm survey of Knibbs’ long array of figures is
positively startling. Leaving out Tasmania,
25,708 firstborns arrived in Australia last year;
and no less than 8691 of them reached the Com-
monwealth anything from nine months to one
month ahead of schedule time. There were 2859
others who arrived a month or less ahead of the
schedule ; but some of them may be given the
benefit of the doubt. Excluding all these last
there still remain 34 out of every 100—and that’s
a proportion big enough to make Australia think
hard. And the chances are that the story is, in at
least one respect, worse than that. Thus of the
25,708 mothers of first-born children, 4050 were 30
years old or over ; and if they were left out of the
reckoning it looks highly probable that the 34
would rush up to 40 or thereabouts so far as the
younger brides are concerned. Just what is the
cause of it all this paper doesn’t know for cer-
tain ; most likely there isn’t merely one cause,
but a dozen. It may be that Australia doesn’t pay
the young man the wages that he ought to get; it
may be that the young woman isn’t content to start
on the housekeeping business on the modest basis
that she ought to be content with for a begin-
ning ; it may be that the imported mother, or
the home-grown mother of the first generation,
doesn’t realise that the truer type of Australian
girl rushes into womanhood at tremendous speed,
and therefore postpones giving the girl a few
valuable hints until there isn’t much left for
mother to give hints about. There are, no
doubt, a dozen more reasons, good and
had. But at any rate the figures stand.
And even they don’t show the blackest side
of the picture. The social “respectability”
of these mothers is saved. They have roped in
their husbands. But there must be hundreds who
can’t; and their infants appear in two other lists.
One needn’t be referred to; the other is to be
found in the records of the coroners’ courts —the
appalling records of the infants (unknown many of
them) who have been done to death. In these
cases the mother hasn’t been able to save her
name by legally getting another, so she struggles
after much the same thing by getting rid of the
little impediment.
