Some More About Moneylending.
This paper commented last week on a reading of
the Moneylenders and Infants Loans Act (1905),
which came as a shock and a surprise to the
members of the N.S. W. Bull Court when it was
brought to their notice in the form of what was
practically an application to summarily set aside
an unconscionable moneylending bargain. Since
it seemed reasonable to assume that what was new
in the legal line to the N.S.W. Bull Court was
likely also to be a novelty to the general public,
which isn’t paid a big salary to plough through the
mazes of the law, the matter was discussed
at some length. But it wasn’t exhausted by any
means. There is another aspect of this im-
ported and too-little-known Act which can
do with ventilation. How many people, one
wonders, appreciate that in N.S. W. all money-
lenders require to be registered? How many
lawyers, policemen, burglars, laymen and so
forth, understand that if folks who habitually lend
money for profit (apart, that is, from such as
bankers and solicitors and those traders—ware-
housemen, for example—who lend money to clients
as part of their businesses) omit to register them-
selves they are liable to heavy fine and long im-
prisonment ? Undoubtedly very few; and as a
result, the identical abuse which the original
British Act (of which ours is a copy) was
framed to scotch is rife in N.S. W. In
the West End of London a decade ago
eery third tailor, hotel-porter, haberdasher,
■ ot-maker, etc., was an amateur usurer.
These traders specialised mostly in accommodat-
ing the temporarily-broke, but normally more or
less gilded, youths of the nation—the sort who had
outrun their paternal allowances and wanted a bit
to go on with at any price. The poorer quarters
of the metropolis and the big provincial towns
contained another class of cash-provider on terms.
People in a small way of business, or in no way of
business, who had a little loose capital, let it be
known that they were open to help their indigent
brethren at a fair figure. To them flocked those
who badly needed ready coin but were too proud
to enter a pawnshop or other place where the
frankly impoverished foregather. And the
amount of extortion and wicked swindling that
went on was stupendous. There are not many
citizens of Sydney or any other big N.S. W town
but can call to mind an acquaintance or two or
three that is prepared to supply cash at a good
•stiff rate of interest outside his regular avenue of
toil, and despite the fact that he is not a pro-
fessional hirer out of gold. The thing is regarded
widely as a perfectly legitimate side-line to a score
of callings. The writer knows personally one
worthy church-member who considers that the
fact of his having on different occasions advanced
most of his fellow-worshippers cash at rates vary-
ing from 30 to 100 per cent, reflects credit on his
kindliness and good feeling. Nor are his church
acquaintances the only members of the community
whom he has thus benefited. Yet he would
fall in a swoon if it were suggested that
he should proceed to the Registrar-General’s
office and there cause himself to be inscribed as
a professional moneylender. Though he doesn’t
know it, he is certainly liable to a large
fine, and gaol as well. The same fact
applies to the numerous person in a big
office or warehouse or Government depart-
ment, who habitually “obliges” his fellow-
employees for a steep consideration, and, pro-
bably, to many of the time-payment fraternity.
The definition of the word “moneylender” in
the Act leaves it to the Court to say how many,
and what sort of transactions lift a man from the
class of beneficent fairy, so to speak, who is act-
ing the part of usurer merely to oblige a friend,
into that of the “ habitual ” who deliberately and
persistently holds himself out as one from whom
bullion may be had if his conditions are coinolied
with. It is quite safe to say, however, that
not many of those doing business in this line
at present would come within the former
category. The taste for moneylending resembles
the passion for beer, in that it grows on a man.
But those who are bitten by the former craze
cannot be too warmly urged to note that the wages
of unauthorised usury are likely to be even more
costly than those of beer, once debtors, the legal
authorities and others concerned start to realise
things.
♦
N. S. Walks has a chance now to calmly consider
its own Stinking Fish habit of assuming that the
imported goods must necessarily be better than any
it could produce itself. Between them Car-
ruthers and the Sydney morning papers chased
Charles Oliver out of office as Chief Railway
Commissioner, and appointed Johnson, a man
whom none of them had ever heard of; but it was
enough for them that Johnson had had some sort
of job on an English railway, and wasn’t an
Australian. Johnson naturally read the Sydney
morning papers, and took their word for it that
the Australian was a poor person who wasn’t even
worth consulting. Consequently, when New-
castle wanted more coal-shipping facilities, John-
son absolutely disregarded the Advisory Com-
mittee appointed in connection with Newcastle
Harbor, though (in the absence of a Harbor
Trust) this committee had some sort of official
standing. As the only man who knew anything,
Johnson went ahead and ordered four MoMfXER
hoists at a cost of between £15,000 and £20,000
each ; and, in spite of protests, he absolutely cut
off 1600 ft. of useful wharf frontage in making
an approach to the only one of them which
has ever been taken out of its packing.
That one, however, was quite enough ; it smashed
the coal, and was otherwise so unsuitable and
disastrous that mine-owners at last refused to
jeopardise their market by loading any
vessels drawn up alongside it. The row
was so great that the Government ap-
pointed a Commission to practically sit on
Johnson as a jury, and the verdict has just
been announced. Such a howling failure is the
whole business that the Commission “feels
compelled to make the drastic recommenda-
tion,” not merely that the three hoists
should remain in their packings, but
that the one already erected should be
torn down and removed. So far as
Johnson’s wild experiment has gone,
the cost to the State has been £68,071;
and probably the State will be able
to get for the whole outfit about as
much as it will cost to shift the im-
pediment which Johnson raised. It is
a lot of money for a small State to pay for
the joy of importing Johnson ; but as the
Stinking Fish dailies were largely respon-
sible for deceiving the poor man, no doubt
they will open another Dreadnought list to make
good at least some of the consequences of their
folly.
♦
That sturdy Anti-Socialist, the farmer, has done
so well out of Socialism that his neighbor, the
country storekeeper, is also reaching out for a
portion of the benevolent Curse as an antidote for
too much Private Enterprise ; and the indications
are that his anger will be terrible if he is prevented
from clutching it. The circumstances of the case
are so bright and glaring, and they show up
the hoofs and tail of Anti-Sosh in such a
beautiful light, that plain black ink and white
paper cannot do them justice; they deserve a
page to themselves arranged like an illuminated
address. In the first place he it known that,
owing to the high price demanded for its sugar by
the great Private Enterprise co., the N.S.W.
town grocer and the country storekeeper have for
years past sold the sweet substance at practically
cost price. On sugar they have hitherto given
their labor, profanity,- shop-space, etc., for
nothing, and have recouped themselves by making
a little more profit on other goods in which the
great sugar co. has no interest. The town grocer
has borne up under the infliction simply because
he had to sell sugar for the convenience of his cus-
tomers and because there was no remedy in sight.
His country relative, however, after numerous
profitless years, thinks he sees a way out of the
difficulty. He has descended on the imported
Railway Johnson with the perfectly staggering
request that he (Johnson) shall lower the carriage
rates on sugar so as to enable him (the country
storekeeper) to make a profit ! The Commission-
er asserts that the reduction asked-' for would
entail a loss of £lO.OOO to the department of
Johnson ; yet, in spite of that awful fact, the
storekeeper is waiting on Johnson’s doormat with
the pathetic expression of the helpless person who
wants to know what is going to be done for him.
The town grocer, meanwhile, remains carefully
at home ; his sugar is not brought to his door
by a Socialistic railway, and he isn’t going to
waste time endeavoring to induce a Private Enter-
prise firm of carriers to cart it to him at a
dead loss. Even the Sydney Telegraph
recognises that the storekeeper’s demand is too
terrible, and it is hustling him off the premises
with some muffled advice to go and see the
Colonial Sugar Refining Co. about it. Amongst
other things it points out to the storekeeper that,
even if the taxpayer made him a present of the
£lO,OOO a year, the bloated co. would probably put
up its rates to correspond, and mop up every
penny of the State subsidy. The Telegraph’s
own remedy for the trouble is a little more Free-
trade ; but it would prescribe the same way for a
broken leg. The otdy ailment Freetrade has ever
been known to cure is that mysterious one de-
scribed in the patent medicine advertisements as
“ a feeling of fulness after meals.” Fora feeling
of emptiness before meals it has no remedy to
offer.
It isn’t in accordance with the fitness of names that
Rose Musgrove should have married a Mr. Garrick, with
the intention of presently quitting the stage. “ Mrs.
Douglas Garrick ” would look great on the posters.
The dogs of Rosina Casselli are vegetarians. The whole
18 of them exist on biscuits, fruit, vegetables and milk.
Persimmons and passion-fruit are their favorite menu
items.. It is said that they have not sampled meat since
they bit a Dago years ago as a protest against his con-
duct in driving them off his banana counter.

♦
“The King of Cadonia,” which arrived at
Her Majesty’s (Sydney) on Saturday night,
deserves a cheering word, in that it is largely a
new thing in the musical comedy line. It possesses
a distinct plot. The action is laid in a region
unexploited, hitherto, by those who keep the wolf
from the door by the manufacture of such
pieces. A good deal of the music is strangely
unreminiscent of other music in other enter-
tainments. The jests are mostly fresh, and
where they aren’t they give evidence of having
been carefully selected and edited and renovated
so that they appear almost as good as new. And
several of the leading performers—besides being
sound artists—are unknowns so far as Sydney
is concerned, and hence acceptable to theatre-
goers aweary of old faces and familiar methods.
Furthermore, the show is exquisitely dressed and
skilfully stage-managed, so that it presents a
series of most attractive pictures.
“THE KING OF CADPONIA," SYDNEY
HER MAJESTY’S.
Lo. Danse des Apaches by BERT GILBERT
and LOTTIE SARGENT.
Herbert Clayton, the co.’s main baritone,
is Kins of one of those uncharted lands wherein
regicide is supposed to be as popular as alcohol,
and more prevalent. Rumor credits his job
with danger at the hands of an anti-King League.
The League corresponds, in the estimation of the
Cadonian royal family, to the local Tiger, Sosh,
and keeps the Palace and
environs in a chronic fer-
ment. Whatever occurs of
an unpleasant nature is
ascribed to the League. If
the household cat passes
over in strong convulsions,
an indictment is filed
against the League, and it
is unanimously found guilty
of compassing the animal’s
taking-off. Should the cook
give notice or impudence,
the disconcerting fact is put
down to the blighting class-
distinction-destroying influ-
ence of the League. Some-
one of position files his sche-
dule. The calamity is ex-
plained by the fact that the
mad tactics of the League
have forced the insolvent
reluctantly to send all his
capital out of the coun-
try. In brief,League ”is
Cadonian for “ Caucus.”
Things reach such a pass
at last, and the Palace is
growing so jumpy and de-
moralised with apprehen-
sion, that the King is driven
to conceal himself behind a
clean shave, and make per-
sonal investigation of the
Monstei’. He becomes a
member of the allegedly anarchistic body. In
the course of his duties as Leaguer he meets,
loves, and is loved by Princess Marie. Mean-
while Bert Gilbert is holding the marrow-freezing
job of Regent, with the ghastly prospect adjacent
of being crowned King unless the vanished
sovereign reappears. To appreciate the horror of
Gilbert’s position it is necessary to realise that his
faith in the criminal intentions of the Leaguers is
as profound as that is of a female Conferencer in
regard to the reality of the Sosh Menace. In
Gilbert’s view, there is nothing of which the
League is incapable. He would leave his native
land for ever ; but he fears lest the League may
intercept and despoil him on the way. He lives for
the most partin his pyjamas on a roof—to escape
the Leaguers. Things end satisfactorily by the
discovery on the part of the King that the League
scare is all a piece of foolishness, fomented,
as far as can be gathered, for political purposes
by the managing General Bonski. So the King
marries Princess Marie, and Gilbert dresses and
hurries down from the roof, and everyone is
immensely relieved
* * • *
Herbert Clayton is about the most im-
pressive-looking king the writer has yet encoun-
tered. He has a fine, resonant voice, and his act-
ing is far above the average monarch’s efforts in
that line. He provides a curious instance of
the effect of face-herbage on personality’. With
his beard on he is wooden and- stiff.
Reaped, he becomes immediately laughing, boy-
ish and debonair, all that is sprightly and
attractive. Miss Dorothy Court, who plays Prin-
The New Beauty
Actor CLAYTON.
cess Marie, owns an excellent light soprano
voice, and she acts with quiet distinction
in a part necessarily restrained and dig-
nified. Bert Gilbert carries on his shoul-
ders practically the entire comedy of the
show, and gives the utmost satisfaction. Miss
Lottie Sargent is bright and taking in a sou-
brette part. Her “Danse des Apaches” with
Gilbert in the second act is the hit of the piece.
Miss Susie Vaughan, Arthur Appleby, Pat
Bathurst and Victor Prince play minor parts
well.

At this theatre, on Saturday afternoon
next, a mixed show will be presented. The Syd-
ney Comedy Club intends to play “When We
Were Twenty-One,” and afterwards some of the
haughtiest members of N.S.W.’s old noblesse will
appear for the first time on any stage in tableaux.
These things are being done in the name of
Charity, and the Civil Ambulance and Transport
Corps is the excellent institution which is to
benefit by them.

“ The Bondman ” still pervades Sydney
Royal, and the crowd continues to surge in with
the object of personally considering his miseries.
But aiack ! it is not to be for much longer. The
Bland ’Olt co. goes to Melbourne shortly for
a six weeks’ season, and then it will dis-
band. Bland has found it increasingly hard of
late to get theatres wherein to produce his
marrow-coolers. An air of gloom broods over the
whole combination, from the normally-eheery
head and front of it down to the cow whose milk-
ing turn occurs in the farmyard scene,
** * *
Mrs. Melba embarked on another farewell
concert as this paper went to press. Albeit,
writing a while before the event, it is safer
than backing favorites to state that Sydney Town
Hall was packed with a distinguished and
appreciative audience; that the Jewel Song
from “Faust” induced rapturous applause;
that the Mad Scene from “ Lucia ” was
responsible for a furore of enthusiasm, and that
at the conclusion of the Inflammatus (“Stabat
Mater”) an encore number was vociferously
demanded. Also that no critics burst with
admiration, though several of them felt dashed
like it.
** * *
The free list continues to be entirely sus-
pended at Sydney Criterion, which is to say the
panto, is drawing well. The suspension of a
panto, free list indicates as surely that the public
cash is flowing into the box-office as the absence
of any mention of Dreadnoughts by our great
dailies does that the public cash is not flowing
into the Mayor’s office.
** # *
The sale of the late artist Brunton’s pic-
tures began with a fair prospect that none will
be left on the walls of the amateur auction mart.
All the theatrical managers and several incorri-
gible first-nighters secured one or more apiece,
and the sale is to continue during the current
week.
** * #
A new songstress who may break into
melody in Sydney within the next few weeks is
Miss Lucelle Benstead. At present she is just
young enough to make it unsafe to prophesy what
she may become, but there are authorities who
reckon she will go far. If she should arrive she
will be the first nightingale that her State has
turned out, for she is a daughter of the far interior
as well as of her parents ; her birthplace was Alice
Springs, in the southern part of the Territory and
almost in the exact centre of Australia. The
singer’s father and mother were station-owners at
Alice Springs in the ’Bo’s and ’9o’s of last century.
“ I spent six years there,” says Mrs. Benstead ;
“they were the happiest years of my life, and the
place is a Paradise.’’ Possibly these weren’t
drought years—still the remark sounds well.
* * *
Sydney Tivoli was darkened last Saturday
by the arrival of Charles J. Johnson, cake-
walker, who is alleged to be a cousin of that
other Johnson who danced in a hempen square at
the Stadium some time last year. Dancing,
apparently, is an incurable habit of the Johnson
family. Charles J. is billed as the champion of
the world in his own particular line, and he is
certainly an elegant, light-footed person ; but it is
not a particularly large stunt to travel the earth
on. The man who came from Paris to play
Diabolo is the Tivoli record in the way of the
long distance and the short stunt. Blake and
Granby supply the other new turn of the pro-
gramme, which consists of a crockery-breaking
sketch. It exhilarates, but isn’t particularly
amusing. The Kremka Brothers and Rosina
Casselli’s midget dogs are first-rank performers.
The dogs are now barking their farewells, and
they will sport their jewelled collars and drive
their motor-car for the last time on Thursday
night (26th). The dancing of the Two Prices and
the patter of Vaude and Verne deserve a word of
approbation, and the fishing village of the First-
Part continues to be an astonishing spectacle.
* at * *
Adelaide Royal is for the moment a one-
man show. The Comic Operas have finished up
with a week of “Dairymaids” and “Gotten-
berg,” and made way for Foster Fraser—just off
the mail-boat. His lectures vary from scenes of
massacre in Russia to comic incidents in the
House of Commons. Meanwhile, politicians and
other people vie in driving him round and filling
him up with information for the book on Australia
which is bound to follow. Cinquevalli is put-
ting in his final week in this country at the Tivoli,
and R. H. Douglass has come in to monologue in
his support. The University Conservatorium has
broken out with a revival of romantic drama,
mostly Irish ; and the literary section of the com-
munity welcomes a chance of making acquaintance
with W. B. Yeats and Synge. Anything more
essentially dramatic could hardly be devised than
t'ne quiet game of chess played by Deirdre and her
young king while the executioner waits on the
threshold. These things might not pay commer-
cially, but they are a true artistic success.
Bryceson Trehearne fathers the movement, and
composes the incidental music, and has got round
him an unexpected wealth of amateur talent.

Royal, and many immortal lines are revealing
their origin to a public that knew them only as

quotations torn from their context. After going
20 years without a revival of “ Othello,” the city
wanted it hadly for educational purposes. Out of
every 20 sententious citizens who profess to give
a “round unvarnished tale” of a commercial
occurrence, in which they “nothing extenuate
nor set down aught in malice,” there is not more
than one, on an average, who can locate the exact
whereabouts of those little hits of literature. To
all such stereotyped expressionists The Bul-
letin would say: “Put money in thy purse
and buy a ticket for the show aforesaid. Go and
batten on this Moor. Othello has the medicine
that physics ignorance.”
* * * *
Besides, the play of “Othello” contains
more human interest to the square yard of text
than any other work of Shakspeare. It is good,
meaty melodrama all the time. One may go home
and dream of horrors after it. Indeed the man
who has been applying to his blood the hot,
rebellious liquor which rnaketh thirsty where
it most doth satisfy, is in danger of finding green-
eyed monsters seated on the counterpane when
he retires to rest after seeing how the dusky
warrior did Desdemona’s quietus make. Perhaps
the terrible fifth act is the more impressive for
being shorn of some of its old attractions. The
introductory scene is omitted where Roderigo is
wounded by Cassio, and finally killed by lago.
The curtain rises on the bedroom scene, so the
audience hears nothing of Roderigo’s death until
Emilia brings the new§ to Othello. The injury to
Cassio’s hind leg, inflicted by lago, is practically
suppressed in this version, and the assassination
of Emilia by her husband is sickbed o’er by a pale
cast of doubt. Emilia doesn’t die in a bold Shak-
spearian manner. She merely subsides into a
high-backed chair with her figure turned from
observation, and whether she lives or dies
is a matter of no concern to her friends, seemingly.
These cuttings and alterations in the fifth act are
less remarked than the omission of the two open-
ing scenes in the first act. The performance
begins in the council chamber, and the audience
hears nothing of Desdemona’s elopement until her
infuriated parent comes along with his complaint.
* * » *
Although Othello is one of the “fattest”
characters an actor can wish for, the character of
lago is apt to take a lot of the shine out of him.
I hey are striking individuals of almost equal
weight, and lago piles up a lot of points ere his
rival gets fairly going. Also he is in a posi-
tion to put in a lot of fine work after the
noble savage has started to roar. The actor
who can play Othello without being somewhat
overshadowed by the villain of the piece should
be capable of completely overshadowing the other
actor if they were to change places. With Oscar
Asche and Herbert Grim wood, in the present pro-
duction there is no question of a divided
sovereignty. Asche, in the name part, so domi-
nates Grimwood that there seems something
wanting in the play. The audience cannot get a
proper sense of the relative proportions of the
characters. Why should Othello, or even Cassio
and foolish Roderigo, be so easily persuaded by
an lago in whom there is no personal magnetism ?
Herbert Grimwood (he was excellent as the
father of Katharine in “ Taming of the Shrew”)
is a small man. He has a curious, mincing man-
ner of speech that would awaken suspicion rather
than confidence in the people he wants to impose
upon. It is not unlike the manner of a young
Hebrew who has some diamonds to dispose of.
lago is not bound to be a bluff, rough, jolly vil-
lain, but. he should be a very likeable fellow.
Everybody calls him “honest lago.” Dis-
simulation must come natural to him. But
the actor in this case draws an lago
of the modern conventional kind, whose evil de-
signs are conspicuously obvious. The performance
is lacking equally in strength and subtlety. Oscar
Asche, on the other hand, is exceptionally power-
ful in everything except two or three of the
declamatory speeches, which every actor rants.
Ascbe’s Othello, in a frenzy of jealousy, is
great but not gigantic. His falling in a fit
of epilepsy seems natural evidence of weak-
ness, whereas there have been Othellos whose
epilepsy merely emphasised the strength of
their passion. They had to have a fit or burst.
It is in his speech to the Senate, his tender
moments with Desdemona, his masterful appear-
ance in the courtyard when he dismisses Cassio,
and in his melancholy murderousness and final
despair in the bedroom scene, that Oscar Asche is
at his best. His dualogues with lago are fine in
places, and his make-up is convincing. The
colored alien is a very real person. There is even
a touch of the tar brush in his deportment and his
voice.
* * •* *
Miss Lily Bray ton, in appearance, hardly
realises one’s idea of meek and gentle Desdemona.
The defiant glare of Katharine the Shrew still
lingers in her eyes. But her performance is pretty
and pathetic. Miss Robertson, as Emilia, is as
effective as her moderately strong voice will allow
her to be. Gerald Souper is a rather heavy but
very capable Cassio in his drunken scene, and the
men in smaller parts are either good, or better.
“Othello ” will be played all this week, and next
it will take alternate nights with “The Taming
of the Shrew.”
** * *
Some night, a few years hence, the Mel-
bourne Town Hall may be again crowded with an
expectant audience, waiting to hear whether the
voice of Amy Castles is as good as its latest
European reputation. Last Saturday night was
Melbourne’s third time of waiting. The first
audience of 10 years ago heard a glorious voice—
the voice of a young girl who did not know what
platform affectations meant. The second advent
was passable, and it passed. It may now be
packed away with the forgotten bygones. The
latest edition of Amy is a self-possessed young
woman ; placid, unemotional, but still pleasing in
her naturalness. There is evidence of very
thorough training of what is obviously a
rare voice. Opening with a recitative and
aria from Meyerbeer’s “La Stella del Nord,”
the singer expressed the light, shade and strength
of the music with all the ease of a great artist.
There was a double flute obligato, and in conjunc-
tion with the two instruments the remarkable
flexibility of the voice was displayed at the full
strength of its purity. An aria from Giordano’s
“ Andrea Chenier,” an intricate but superb com-
position, was also sung with consummate ease ;
but—the pity of it ! —without the slightest
emotional expression. An encore, a simple song
of a child and broken toys, failed to convey
its possibilities in the direction of quaint
expression. Two English songs, however, scored
heavily, and in the final encore—there were
encores galore—Miss Castles struck her only
emotional note in a fine rendering of the inevitable
“Home, Sweet Home.” The dominant impression
of the artist—advertised by an enterprising man-
agement as “The Australian Jenny Lind is
that she is good enough to be Amy Castles without
dragging in the name of any deceased Swede. It
is a superb natural voice, and the owner, in spite
of her placid temperament, is fully equal to sing-
ing the most involved of soprano compositions.
But, by the shades of Jenny Lind, is there no one
able to arouse her into emotional expression of
dramatic strength ?
* * * *
Of the company which supports Amy, the
first mem her to make Saturday’s crowded audience
sit up and listen was Anderson Nicol, a dark-haired
tenor, well up to the fleshy standard of his class.
He could easily pass muster if the Taits were to
bill him as Signor Angelotti Nicolini. A good
start was made with “Celeste Aida,” which prob-
ably has not been beard in Melbourne since
Larizza sang in Verdi’s opera. As an encore.
Nicol(ini) sang “Take a Pair of Sparkling Eyes,”
with a richness of voice that has not
often been surpassed in Melbourne, though
the singer hardly suggested a blithe and gay
young gondolier in a Gilbert and Sullivan
opera. Adrian Amadio, a youth who has come
into local prominence as a master of the instru-
ment with which the late It. Swiveller used to
gain consolacion, was not the least prominent
feature of an excellent concert programme. The
youthful flautist, in addition to rendering three or
four solo pieces, was in strong evidence with his
admiring father in the double flute obligato to the
Meyerbeer aria. The piano soloist is Mr. Victor
Biisst (with two dots over the “ u”), and another
lady-like pianist, Mr. Harold Elvens, made no
mistakes as the accompanist. A very big gun,
Peter Dawson, was named on the programme to
sing “The Mad Dog” and other songs, but the
real mad dog was in the manager’s room. There
a telegram was received from the little man with
the big voice, who explained that floods and
broken bridges had blocked the Adelaide express.
Peter’s part (a good name is Peter) was taken by
a young Melbourne baritone, W. E. Lewis, who
would have fully satisfied the audience had he only
forgotten to be so unnecessarily nervous.

At Her Majesty’s (Melbourne) the
Williamson Dramatic Co. will be engaged until
Friday with “The Flag Lieutenant,” which, in the
advertised words of the local Aegus, “swings
the breath of the ocean over the footlights.” But
on Saturday the flagship of Admiral Titheradge
will steam round to the Princess’s, and the gay
Lieutenant and his companions will swing their
oz.onic cough over the footlights of the theatre in
Spring-street. Her Majesty’s is wanted for the
Comic Opera Co., which is returning for its
annual season. It opens its joyful proceedings
with “ The Duchess of Dantzic,” which gorgeous
work is new to Melbourne.
* * *
Hugh Ward and co. are passing away from
the Princess’s in the same old cloud of farcical
complications that bore them to the premises six
weeks ago. “The Fencing Master” has given
place to “ A Bachelor’s Honeymoon,” and the co.
will make its adieux in that revived frivolity.
Then, as was afore stated. “The Flag Lieutenant”
will sprinkle salt sea spray on the big drum, and
the odor of the mighty deep will be loud in the
stalls.
** * #
More in sorrow than in gladness this paper
notes the fact that Melbourne no longer thrills
responsively to “The Face at the Window.”
Business is not up to its usual mark at the King’s
since “ The Face ” re-appeared. When the lights
go dim and low, and a wailing cry, like unto a bad
toothache, announces the coming of the Gruesome
Visage, the audience that shuddered of yore is
now content to titter. Even the gal vanised corpse
is hailed with faint demonstrations of mirth. But
on Saturday the Andersonians will turn their
attention seriously to the new Australian drama
by Jo Smith. The title of Jo Smith’s story is
“The Bushwoman,” and its interest is rural and
domestic. In short, a play of Australian up-
country life with no bushrangers in it.
* * * *■
At the Athenaeum Hall (Melbourne),
where Taits’pictures are boarding for the present,
the programme is the usual procession of the
tragic, the tickling and the tidy. A mailman’s
devotion to duty ends in his death, which occurs
with the satisfactory support of an angel
and a mob of kneeling cowboys. An in-
teresting and dazzling fable is “The Hotel
Thief,” which provides details of a burglary.
Foolshead, an intense Italian humorist, this time
becomes a sportsman, with more or less mirthless
results; while “Drunkards’ Fancies” is one of
the strongest temperance arguments this paper
has ever beheld. But the monarch of the menu
on Saturday was a brilliant and skilfully-tinted
picture of Colombo, described in the classic tongue
of ’Erb and Benno as “a perfect boshter of a
place.”
** * *
West’s films at Melbourne Olympia on
Saturday included a couple of French storyettes,
told with characteristic vivacity. The first. “Jim
and Willie,” is a study of a man hunt, conducted
by ladies whose curious slenderness suggests that
they belong to some odd breed of waterfowl. “ A
Street Waif ” is a tale of the rescue of a dishevelled
maiden. Anotherrestfulnarrative, “AFalseOath,”
is seemingly one of those matrimonial wrong-
angled triangles, where a blameless hypothenuse
bares the capers of the other two sides. There is
a further picture of Edward of England in the
middle of grovelling minions. The days and
nights of West’s pictures at Olympia are num-
bered. Early next month—the 9th, or there-
abouts—the new West’s building will be ready to
receive visitors, and the pictures will there come
to light under the most up-to-date conditions as
regards seating accommodation.
** * *
A benefit to Albert Norman, whose career
as a character actor —and a clever, forcible actor
at that—has been cut shore by ill-health, will take
place at the King’s Theatre on Monday, Septem-
ber 6. Mr. Apderson lends the theatre to the
good cause, all the leading managers and mum-
mers in Melbourne have volunteered their assist-
ance, and the patronage of many personages of
importance has been promised to the big enter-
tainment that is fixed up for the occasion. Phil
Goatcher and Joseph Daniels are the honorary
organising secretaries of the Norman benefit. A
cheque addressed to either of them at the King’s
Theatre or the Green .Room Club, will be duly
acknowledged with its equivalent in tickets for
the show.
** * *
Foster Fraser, the travelled journalist
who talks so brightly of his experiences and illus-
trates his monologue with dramatic pictures on
the screen, comes due in Melbourne from Adelaide
next Monday (30th) at the Masonic Hall. Fraser
gives six lecture entertainments in all. The first
is “ America Up to Date,” and the one specially
designed to attract womankind to a matinee on
Wednesday is “The Women of Many Lands.”
** * *
The Melbourne Opera House programme
is losing Chung Ling Soo from its midst after to-
night (Thursday). The vacuum left by the retiring
wizard will be tilled by Rosina Casselli’s perform-
ing dogs, to the best of their ability. Florence
and Lillian, likewise the Bells, are staying on
awhile longer, and the- rest of the proceedings
includes the reappearance of Guest and Newlyn,
whose sad discourse is not fully atoned for by their
dancing.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“E.D. ”: It would be interesting to know on what
grounds the theatrical manager of great experience, who
is supposed to have developed remarkable sagacity in
giving the people what they want, selected “The Flag
Lieutenant ” as the sort of play Australia pants for.
What peculiar elements satisfied him that this particular
melo-comedy wduld take well and give pleasure to all?
I can see good points in the play, but where are the
likely appeals to the favor of Thomas, Richard, and
Henry? I ask this even in face of the dim possibility of
“The Flag Lieutenant” booming like thunder while I
write. It seems to me that the play’s weak—utterly help-
less—ending is sufficient to damn it on sight with any
stage-manager of parts and experience, since it is amply
demonstrated that the public hates an indefinite finish.
Could a conclusion be more inconclusive? The authors
create an interesting problem, and then bolt in absolute
terror from their psychological Frankenstein. Ibsen
would have started “The Flag Lieutenant” just where
its authors have ended it, and intelligent people, however
they may quarrel over the morals and meanings of the
shaggy Norwegian, admit that he is a master craftsman
in the making of plays. The Major is set up in life,
covered with honors and adulation for a deed of heroism
performed by the Lieutenant, who is scorned and con-
temned, and cannot explain. It’s a pity to throw such
fine possibilities into the dust box just when the pro-
blem is beginning to prob., in a manner of speaking.
“S.S.” : The Hugh Ward co. is composed practically of
comic opera graduates. For many years Hugh himself
earned a humble crust by flirting a nimble toe and lifting
up a somewhat gritty voice in frivolous musical plays.
Rose Musgrove left comic opera for genteel comedy;
Celia Ghiloni has hitherto been known as a successful
interpreter of Gilbert’s elderly contralti and a portly
romp in more bouffe-headed burlesque ; and Miss Grace
Palotta was a Gaiety Girl.
The cry of the stage super is still loud in Melbourne,
and he adduces some odd stories to. support his noise.
One is to the effect that Her Majesty’s Theatre in the
Yarra city applied for the services of a dozen members of
the naval forces to act as Supers-presumably in “The
Flag Lieutenant.” The remuneration offered was the
fabulous sum of 2s. fid. a night without any discount for
cash, and it was said that the naval men’s acceptance of
the offer would have the effect of depriving legitimate
supers of a job. The legitimate supers seem to harbor
the belief that they are capable of impersonating naval
forces. Alas ! it is a mistaken belief. The average super
can impersonate nobody but himself. Even for doing
that, however, ss. a night ■wouldn’t be too much.
One of the “suffragettes” who achieved some bold
advertisement in the British press by chaining herself to
the grating of the ladies’ gallery in the House of Com-
mons, so that she couldn’t easily be chucked out when she
made a disturbance, turns out to be an Australian.
Beaumont Smith has engaged her for an Australian
lecturing tour. The lady’s name, by the way, is Muriel
Matters. One would think it might be better for Muriel
to stay in England and keep chaining herself to the House
of Commons. She can gain little by tying herself to
public buildings here. Our women have the suffrage
already.
Randolph Bedford : First saw Oscar Asche in Beerbohm
Tree’s production of “ Ulysses.” Asche played Antinous,
and cut a wider swathe that did Tree, if only by reason of
his physical superiority. L*ter I saw him play Benedick
in “Much Ado,” at the Imperial Theatre—a pretty little
building made of marbles like Gorgonzola cheese. Ellen
Terry was the Beatrice, and nearly all the charm of the
show was hers ; yet, for all the charm, “ Much Ado” was
mostly played to the chairs. Later, at the Adelphi, Asche
did “ The Shrew.” I like the Melbourne production more
than that of the Adelphi. The scenery is worth the
money. There’s an atmosphere that isn’t English—the
atmosphere of Italy—the feeling of a big bulging violet
night blackened by cypress and stone pine ; of a century-
old rose climbing a five-hundred-year-old ruin ; the sense
of the belief that fireflies must be there low on the ground
among the myrtles. It brought Italy back to me again.
As for that street in Padua, there isn’t a shadow wrong.
“Zany”: Wellington (M.L.) recently suffered a mild
epidemic of Tahmmy Burns. The big-little stousher
didn’t catch on, and only showed for two nights at the
Opera House—to half a decent-sized audience both times.
It was a good moving-picture show; but the Tahmmy
Burns part of the programme was quite shocking.
Tahmmy, after a few wallops at the punching-ball, began
to puff like a motor-car with the croup, and he got tired
almost before he started. Then he danced round for a
few moments with his sparring partners, and after that
put on his Napoleonic overcoat again. Wellington believes
that he is disgracefully out of form, and his talk of being
never fitter in his life is just Tahmmy Burns.
There has been a death in the Sydney Cri. pony family.
“Cinders,” perhaps the shapeliest of the four-in-hand
which nightly supplied the traction-power for the panto,
heroine’s ball-chariot, is dust. A motor ran into him the
other night, and he sped to the pastures of Elysium.
“ S.S.” : An exasperating piece of foolishness in “ The
Flag Lieutenant” escapes attention. Lieutenant King-
ston and the doctor put up their whole scheme to foist
the glory of the former’s heroism on to Major Cyril
McKay. Yet the latter is seated a few feet from them,
listening to their conversation, and the fact that he
understands is manifested by his exulting cry, “ Men-
tioned in despatches ! I’m mentioned in despatches ! ”
This occurs after the major has recovered consciousness
and when his think-machine is in proper working order
again, and it makes the show look a trifle foolish.
“O.K. ” : Talking of the merry “Twelfth Night,” in
which Atholwood, Edmund Gwenn, Miss Waldorf, and
the thin comedian who played Andrew Aguecheek were
conspicuously good, E. I), remarks that May Chevalier,
who made a young and humorous Maria, was “ of small
consequence previously and since.” Marry come up !
Likewise Odds Bodikins and Grammercy ! Maj r Chevalier
was a bonnie actress of considerable consequence in all
she did. She gave a strangely interesting performance of
Miss Faulkner, the girl who captured the affections of
Sherlock Holmes. She was a charming sweetheart to
the Admirable Crichton what time he reigned on the
island, and her brief tfite A, tfite with that abnormal
butler after he had resumed buttling for her noble family
was full of subtle meaning. Nor should it ever be said
that May Chevalier was “ inconsiderable ” as the heroine
of “If I Were King.” Would that all the actresses im-
ported to Australia were qualified to hold a candle to her !
Seemingly she is among the favorites in the country she
came from and returned to, for last year I saw her billed
in London as appearing at a Kennington theatre (Ken-
nington where the cricket oval is) in “ Sherlock Holmes.”
Her husband, Hamilton Stewart, was running the show.
Melbourne’s new centre of attractions, south of the
Yarra, which originated years ago in FitzGerald Bros.’
circus-building and was finally established by the ill-fated
Prince’s Court, is about to spread itself considerably.
TheFitzGerald structure of galvanised iron was demolished
to make room for Olympia, built by the Wirths for circus
purposes, hut evidences of Prince’s Court are still in
existence. The water-chute and sundry side-shows will
hold their ground during the next summer season, any-
how ; and now Wirths are going to open a big roller-
skating rink close to Olympia and the Chute. This great
boss rink is to have a floor of reinforced concrete,
measuring 300ft. x 100ft. A sliding roof and sliding
sides to the structure will make it very airy in hot
weather, and the further end of it. consisting of two huge
doors, will open to another rink laid out as a garden.
The floor will be ready for business on August 28, a month
or so previous to the closing of the ice-skating season at
the adjacent Glaciarium. When the skaters quit-the Glassy
Harem, the building will be reconverted into the
home of Tait’s Pictures. Meanwhile, West’s Pictun s
(long- time identified with Olympia) will enter into posses-
sion of a home of their own, leaving Olympia to be utilised
once more for circus purposes. This forthcoming domi-
cile of West’s has the most commanding site in the whole
show market. Almost facing the Glaciarium, at the
junction of two streets, and right in the way of the
stream of humanity that flows southward from Melbourne,
the new building gives to the surrounding neighborhood
an air of show-place importance which formerly it lacked.
Is will be opened on 7th September, or thereabouts. The
theatre managements in the Bourke-street district are
bound to feel some twinges in the pocket when West’s
and the new rink and the circus and the Glaciarium
are all on the job. Their full power of attraction may
be estimated at not less than 12,000 on Saturday nights.
The Government of Groperland does its little besr,
seemingly, to discourage theatrical managers from send-
ing- costly shows to that district. There is a loss
of four days in conveying a company from Adelaide
and Perth, and when the co. arrives at Fremantle
there is an inhospitable impost of 4s. per ton on its lug-
gage, providing that it is put on the trucks for Perth :
otherwise it goes throug-h the Customs sheds, and theie
is an extra charge of fid. per ton. Once landed at Fre-
mantle the company’s tons of scenery and effects cannot
be shipped out of Westralia until another 3s. 9d. per ton
has been levied. Wm. Anderson’s representative in the
West, Michael Joseph, lately ventured to expostulate
with the Chief Secretary re these exorbitant ehargis.
and that political luminary referred him to the
local Harbor Trust Secretar}-, who practically up-
braided him for interloping. “Theatrical companies
are not asked to come here,” said the Harbor
Trust secretary, and in a brilliant afterthought he asked:
“ Why don’t you have the scenery made in Western Aus-
tralia 1 ” He might as well have asked why the touring-
managers didn’t get their plays written and their actors
manufactured in Perth. Another grievance that show-
men complain of is the bad construction of the two exist-
ing theatres in Perth, which exposes them to harassing
interference by the Central Board of Health. His
Worship the Mayor is the proprietor of the two theatres,
and although his tenants have to suffer for the short-
comings of his property, he himself has been able to
avoid compliance with the Board’s order to provide an
asbestos curtain at the Theatre Royal, which order was
served on him three years ago. The Mayor is truly
Perthian in his business policy. He reserves the right to
sell lollies, peanuts and cordials in his theatres, and
lately his daughter took over the cloakrooms.
BEWILDERMENT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At the end of “ The King- of Cadonia,” Mr. Bert Gilbert
and Miss Sargent brought down the house with the
larrikin valse of Paris, “La Danse des Apaches.” It
called for both the art of facial expression and the most
exact exhibition of simultaneous activity on the part of
booh the performers, and was so cleverly given that it is
likely to be the most talked of feature of the piece.—
Uighly-Moral Morning Daily.
Miss Sargent stepped aside from her “comedy busi-
ness” in the second act of “The King of Cadonia” to
join Mr. Gilbert in the ugly and repulsive “ Danse des
Apaches.” This brutal “dance,” which has nothing
whatever to do with the piece, should be cut out. —
Highly-Moral Evening Daily.
Is it naughty ? Is it good ?
Should one go to it, or should
You and I, whose purity
Is above all question, flee
When our friends advise us to
Buy a seat and view it? Do
Lottie Sargent’s capers and
Those of Gilbert harm the Brand
Newly-snatched? . . . Blank ignorance
Clouds my brain anent this dance.
Is it smartly done or not?
Is it Art or blessed rot?
Does it call for cleverness
Poise, agility, address?
Or, upon the other hand,
Is it amateurish and
Likely, therefore, to inspire
Cultured folks with scorn and ire?. . . .
On these vital points my mind
Is an utter blank, I find.
Is it brutal, vulgar, crude—
All that’s horrible and rude?
Are the dancers’ hammerlocks
Clamant of the ’Loo and Rocks?
Is the whole performance one
The sesthetic type should shun ?
Phrases such as “ quite refined,”
“ Truly sweet,” “ instinct with mind ”
Say, may these be used of it ? . . .
I can’t judge myseif a bit.
Finally, resolve me this:—
Will it cause the public bliss?
Will they cheer at it and yell—
In a word, “ receive it well?”
Or will naught but snorts of rage
Greet the antics on the stage
Of Miss Sargent and her friend?
Weary days and nights I spend
Puzzling thus. . . . I’m all at sea
When my dailies disagree.
N.S.W.

LINES WHITTEN BY AN AMOROUS BLOKE TO HIS
TART.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The “Face at the Window ” was played to a crowded
house at Melbourne Royal on Saturday night.—iVeirs
Item.
O love, now that the day’s harsh toil is o’er,
Come with me. We will brave the Early Door,
And, fighting fiercely through the surging crowd,
We’ll gain our seats within the House of Gore.
From Care and Worry we will seek release,
And from fell Pain we’ll find a sweet surcease
While list’ning to the death wail of Le Loup ;
We’ll crunch our peanuts, wrapped in perfect peace.
Dear love, we’ll see a most exciting chase—
This is the great detective’s toughest case—
He follows up a Clue through three long acts,
And in the fourth is slaughtered by the Face.
But vengeance on the Face is not delayed,
For Science to the Righteous lends her aid,
The cold, cold corpse is galvanised to life —
Thus is the villain’s villainy displayed 1
To see the Face, the Blood, the Blazing Fire,
To see the monster trampled in the mire,
And saintly Virtue triumph over Vice,
All for a bob ; what more could Love desire ?
victoria.

WINES
ON THE UP-GRADE.
ARISTOCRATICUS : “ Who are your people descended
from then?”
BREWERYUS: “No one. We've ascended!”
The aldermanic hosts from the Sydney
suburban councils have decided to hold an anti-
Greater Sydney Conference of their own. This is
like the peers of England (with apologies to the
peers) meeting to discuss the abolition of the
House of Lords; or the hand weavers of the old
days meeting to discuss the inadvisableness of
having improved machinery on the ground that
the machines were the best possible and were
working very well as they were, and that Provi-
dence was against anything new. The aldermen
are also acting on the general principles of “Let
sleeping dogs lie,” “ Quieta non mover e” “Better
not,” “ Do not poke the animals,” “Don’t shoot
the pianist; he’s doing his blanky,” “Let well
alone.” “Keep off the grass,” “The time is not
ripe,” and many other maxims which, doubtless,
were whispered to the Creator on the first of the
six working days by the Devil. And when the
last trump sounds, and all the souls are about to
begin the Resurrection, an aldermanic ghost from
one of the suburbs of Sydney will be heard above
the blare of the trump protesting that the move-
ment is premature.
The Oldest Inhabitant discovers some
more venerable pubs :
Five hotels will disappear, at least for a time, amid the
great repairs in Oxford-street, Sydney. One is the
Burdekin, known in days gone by as the Blind Beggar.
The old house was kept by Alfred Bradford. The Ex-
change did not exist in the days of the Blind Beggar, but
where the London Bank now is was the Eagle, kept by Jas.
Lees, who is alive to day, and the sole survivor in Sydney
of the 11th Regiment. The Kelso is on the site of the old
Forester’s Arms, once kept by W. A. Thompson. • Tne
Crecy is on the site of the Sportsman’s Arms of half a cen-
tury ago. Where the Oxford is (corner of Bourke-
street), a pub called the Cottage of Content was
once kept by Edward Johnston. Perhaps, of the five,
one may be rebuilt. It is a pity that some of
these old pub-names weren’t preserved. The Blind
Beggar and the Cottage of Content and their kind sound
far more picturesque than most of the legends we see on
drink signboards to-day. When one considers that in
Sydney there are six pubs called Albert, three Captain
Cooks, six Commercials, four Crowns, eight Victorias,
11 Railways, and all manner of other duplications, one
ceases to wonder at the growth of Local Option.
“ F.C.” on some of the ways of the sea
As to the missing Waratah, I had my doubts when the
cables told us that four days after being overdue the
underwriters passed on their insurance at a cost of 45
per cent. ; and a few days later the price went up to 90
and 93 per cent, of the total risk. This is a case where
money talks. Afterwards, as if to explain that, talk,
a cable said that the vessel took in 2000 tons of coal at
Durban, of which 300 tons were on the bridge debk—
which may or may not be true. Now conies the skipper
of the Bannockburn, who left Capetown for Australia on
July 24, with a large quantity of coal on his main deck.
On July 26—the day the Waratah cleared from Durban—
he ran into the worst gale he ever remembers, which
“threw the steamer on her beam ends, and kept her
thereabouts until all the coal had been washed overboard. ”
Had that not happened, he doubts if his vessel
would have righted itself. Still I was posted
missing myself once, and I’m alive at time of writing.
In November, 1886, I was helping to drive an
ocean tramp with 3000 tons of cotton screwed
below her decks from Galveston (Texas) to Liverpool.
The greedy owners had filled the main bunkers with
cotton, and sent us orders to call at Norfolk (Virginia)for
enough coal to carry us across. We filled the side
bunkers and then planted 300 tons on the after deck,
carefully battened away from the wheel-chains and other
top-hamper. A gorgeous Atlantic gale started as soon as
we got outside, and about the middle of my watch she
began rolling so that I thought the engines would fall
over on fop of me. Presently the second engineer came
down, put the engines at half speed, and sent me, the
firemen and trimmers and every available soul on deck to
bear a hand. The deck coal had started rolling,
and had carried away the battens and hampered the
wheel chains. The after wheel was too stiff to work, and
the chains were stuck, anyhow ; so presently, with an
extra roll, the old tub got over on her side with the deck-
awash, and stayed there. It was a case of haste or Davy
Jones, so we put every scrap of that coal overboard in
record time. Then we had to nurse the remaining fuel
and call at the Azores for more, and as there is no”cable
thence, we were posted missing before we saw the
Queenstown lighthouse. That vessel was the St. Mungo,
of Glasgow, and she was afterwards wrecked on the
Canary Islands when taking railway-iron to Buenos
Ayres—and there was worse trouble over that ; but I
wasn't taking any more St. Mungo by that time.

include some rudimentary instruc-
tion as to the effects of tobacco
smoking on human nature, physical

reasonable precautions

measures of self-develop-

astic guardians of the public ill-health.

“ Jean 5.,” in distress, from Melbourne :
Of all Australia’s foolish imported habits, one of the
most foolish is that of making a smoke, a smell, and a
noise, none of them pleasant, on the anniversary of this,
that, and the other “royal” event. The other day it
was the anniversary of Somebody or other’s coronation.
I was in the St. Kilda-road at the time, and a drizzling
rain was falling, when about 20 soldiers marched our of
the barracks, and on to a rise in the Domain, where they
solemnly loaded and fired off some pieces of artillery.
Just as solemnly they lined up and marched back to
the barracks after they had finished; and I won-
dered why some people regard this sort of cere-
mony as distinctly right and proper. Nobody was
a straw the better for the whole tired business.
The gentleman who wears the crown couldn’t either hear
the noise or notice the smell. Besides me and the 20
men who walked up the hill and down again, there were
only a few loungers and travellers and cows in the neigh-
borhood ; and it didn’t seem to improve any of them.” I
doubt if one of the cows gave even a pint extra that
night. And it certainly didn’t improve the guns ;in
fact, they are the worse for every discharge, and we may
want them some day when we are firing for keeps. Also
the soldiers were the worse for getting wet. Must we
go on doing this silly business for ever? If we are loyal,
can’t we find some less idiotic way of saying so ?
“ Montalex ” : Was moved to much wrath
and disgust, the other day, by the efforts of a
Sydney “John ” to despatch a disabled horse with
his revolver. He had planted three useless bullets
in the invalid’s head, when I was injudiciously
moved to exclaim: “Here, man! give me that
thing, and I’ll settle the poor brute with a single
shot !” The brass-buttoned bungler haughtily
surveyed me for a moment before he fell back in
good order upon the time-honored, “Mind your
own business !’’ As I retired in a properly humble
frame of mind, I heard three further shots, as
ineffective as the others—for I learnt afterwards
that the atrocity had to be completed with an axe.
Now, I donot recommend for Johannes aforesaid an
elaborate course of anatomy, but I certainly think
he should be taught where to plant an instantly
fatal bullet in the caput of a seriously injured
animal. And, by the way, if all our local police-
squirts are like the one I saw misused on this
occasion, I’m not surprised that the pursuing
copper should miss his man as a regular thing.
The pistol appeared to be the Weblev bulldog, a
solid little 45-calibre article with a Sin. barrel,
meant only for very close quarters, and wholly
unreliable, even in the hands of an expert, at more
than a few yards’ range.
In the absence of a Foundling Home,
strange things may happen to a little Australian.
For instance, there is the ease of (say) He Smvthe,
jun. Annie De Smyth increased Victoria’s popu-
lation by one on August 8, 1908, at Geelong. The
new-chum’s presumed father-—(say) Frederick
De Browne—had previously called on Nurse
Reed, at Coburg, and arranged for the adoption of
the infant, paying her £3O, no less. The day after
the child was born, Nurse Reed’s daughter, a
damsel of 18. went to Geelong and called for the
£3O worth of goods, and delivered the parcel at
Mrs. Reed’s. After this the parcel becomes
vague, but from the inquiries of constables, who
put interfering fingers into the question of the
child’s non-vaccination, it seems that Mrs. Reed
handed the infant (which had been worth
£3O to get rid of) to Mrs. Newman, who passed
it on to Mrs. Milburne. Mrs. Reed seems to have
had great difficulty in discovering Mrs. Newman,
whom she alleged lived at Mordialloc but had gone
to Tasmania ; still after some police pressure she
presently discovered her at Fitzroy. Also the
police discovered that Mrs. Newman was Mrs.
Bell when she wasn’t Mrs. Newman. But Mrs.
Newman, who is Mrs. Bell sometimes, said she
didn’t know anything about the infant, having
merely introduced Mrs. Reed to a missing Mrs.
Milburne. What looked like a dead end was
reached here; but just then the police happened
across Mrs. Reed and Mrs. Newman (if she
wasn’t Mrs. Bell. They were at a doctor’s, getting
a baby vaccinated. Asked if this was the
bahv De Smytbe, Mrs. Newman seemed painfully
vague about the matter, and Mrs. Reed couldn't
swear it was ; so far as she knew, it was just a
baby. Further, the constable discovered thnt
Mrs. Milburne was really somebody else—in fact,
she is possibly two or three people. So to go on
with, Nurse Reed was charged with several offences
against the Infant Life Protection Act; but the
honorariums considered it might be as well to let
a P.M. sort the matter out, and the case was .ad-
journed. It is mentioned that there are at least
half a score of charges that might be fired against
the parties concerned ; but the chief one—that of
mislaying a valuable Australian baby—doesn’t
seem to have been heaved at anybody so far.
In the Cold Land no provision was made
till quite recently to supply pensions to the very
poor. The Tories and a big majority of Whig
“Liberals” in Parliament after Parliament de-
nounced the idea with horror as “ rank Socialism, ”
and rent it and stamped on it fiercely whenever it
bobbed up. Instead there has existed for upwards
of half a century an arrangement for giving
pensions to the affluent. Henry Lucy, the S. M.
Herald’s tame London chronicler of social small-
beer, provides (21/8/’O9) some instances of the way
rich Britons have swindled the State out of
pension-money. For example :
One of the earliest beneficiaries was 0. P. Villiers. He,
of course, made the statutory declaration as to his private
means which is a necessary preliminary to bestowal of the
pension. After receiving it for a period exceeding a
quarter of a century he died, his will revealing possession
of a large fortune. Another very old-age pensioner is
Viscount Cross. Considering that he ranks as a country
gentleman of comfortable estate, was at one time, if not
now, partner in a banking firm, it has always been a
matter of commiserating comment among his friends that
he should have been dependent all these years upon
assistance from the public Treasury. Oddly enough, ever
since the Act came into force it is members of the landed
gentry, the aristocratic class, that have formed the
majority of pensioners. Up to the eve of the fall of Mr.
Balfour’s Administration the statesman then known as
Sir Michael Hicks-Beach was on the pension list. He re-
tired with a peerage, just in time (and there was not
much to spare) to allow the Premier to transfer the
pension to his own brother, Mr. Gerald Balfour, who now
enjoys it.
If an Australian working-man (say) augmented
his screw by this sort of thing he would be (1)
ashamed to look his associates in the eye during
the process, and (2) an almost certain candidate
in due course for hard labor on charges ranging
from obtaining money under false, pretences
to perjury. The Briton of position on easy
State-aid bent isn’t hampered, seemingly, by
these considerations.
* * * *
General Gordon got a broad hint lately
about the need for compulsory military training.
He spent a lot of time travelling to Nowra
(N.S.W.) to inspect the senior cadets, and found
only 12. The rest were absent because their em-
ployers wouldn’t let them attend, and there is no
law to compel the employers to let them attend.
Even if there was such a law, it wouldn’t do the
cadet much good ; the employer would end the
trouble by sacking him and hiring another boy.
And if real war comes, the chances are that,
under the present fool system, the cadet won’t be
allowed to attend then either—mot that the
cadet would amount to so much in real war that
his attendance would make any serious difference.
The voluntary system is always bumping up
against the employer. There was great joy m
London a few months ago because a big insuranc ;
office decided that it would engage no new men
who didn’t join the Territorial Army. This com-
pulsion by the insurance office was regarded as a
triumph for the voluntary system. So the young
man who got a billet with the co. joined the
Territorials and wore his uniform with pride.
Then he lost his billet for some cause or other,
and other employers said they didn’t want any
Territorials as the periodical absences for training
were a nuisance ; and now there is a compulsory
volunteer about who doesn’t know whether it is
the voluntary or the compulsory system he should
curse most.
THE BETTER (SPOUT) WAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Adelaide, Monday.—Rev. 0. H. Neild, head of the
Anti-Gambling League, paid his second visit to the races
on Saturday, to watch the betting fraternity and the
t.otalisator at work. Mr. Neild described the racing as
deadly dull.— S.A. Neivs Item.
The citizen who’s looking for excitement
Need not repair where “books" with punters strive
In future. Racing (i ride Neild’s indictment)
Is boring. He who yearns to feel alive,
And hankers for a really stirring heart-thrill,
Will flee the race-track at a fearful rate
Henceforth, nor falter in his headlong dart till
He hears the thrippence tinkling in the plate
Within an edifice where ceaseless battle
Is waged ’gainst Satan. Here a paltry cash
Donation gives all hands the right to rattle
Old Nick, what time he tries to make his dash.
No stern officials watch with int’rest burning
To see that each contestant gets a fair
And level go. Sharp bumps at ev’ry turning
Baulk Nicholas ; and pocketings impair
His chances when he hastens up the last stretch ;
So that the crowd is fain to lay its sox
Against the harassed, battered and aghast wretch
That staggers, ultimately, past the Box.
E’en if, despite the popular derision
. And hatred, Nick contrives to win, a howl
Of disapproval burgles the decision
From him ;—he’s promptly turned down on a “foul.”
Sport on mere licensed racing tracks is not on
A par with this alluring game, wherein
The Kirk perennially “ puts the pot on ”
Of him who wears the cap and sleeves of Sin.
N.S.W.
10-2-4.

How to get Rich.
Take a quantity of silica costing one-
fourth the price of oil ; mix it with oil,
and sell the compound to the public at
the price of pure oil. It is such a com-
pound the public get when they buy
common soaps. In Sunlight Soap the
public buy a pure and well-made soap.
Sunlight Soap reduces expense by pro-
longing the life of the articles washed with
it, which is much more profitable to the
public than common soaps, which soon re-
duce the liner; to holes.

309-311 Georg'e Street, Sydney,
The feature that out-stood in the Clarke
divorce case, heard last week in Melbourne,
was its beautiful, orderly symmetry, and
slickness and happy despatch. The stage-
management of it was masterly. Punctually to
the hour Rupert got him out of the trouble zone,
where his presence would have been nought but
an embarrassment and a thing of supererogation.
The petitioner had the exactly right amount of
evidence to meet the requirements of the case—
no more, no less. Nothing violent or uti-
gentlemanly was alleged to cause the respondent
to be condemned as a “brute”; nor any-
thing of the mawkishly sentimental order
to make him look ridiculous. Just a
little discreet evidence regarding evenings
spent with the co-re. ; and a fraction of still more
discreet testimony re money-payments to the
fascinating home-burster ; and finally a couple of
the dearest little billets doux from the same damsel,
of a type, both of them, that might be left lying
on any drawing-room table without fear of
damage to the morals of the youngest of young
persons. The court was crowded, but the curious
ones reaped scant sensational reward for their
trouble in butting a passage in there. The busi-
ness went through without a suspicion of a thrill
or hitch—a veritable model for all ceremonies of
the sort. The verdict of Society (the genuine
article which spells itself with a large S) is that
all parties have “behaved extremely well” over
the unfortunate business. Especially warm are
the encomiums heaped upon the absent Rupert.
He is held to have comported himself not merely
like a man, but like a man of the world.

They have a quaint taste in memorials on
the' N.S.W. South Coast. Central Iljawarra
municipality, “the home of the dairying indus-
try,” had its 50th anniversary, and at the usual
banquet—
Instead of erecting a fountain to commemorate the
inauguration of the municipality, Alderman McDonald
suggested that steps should be taken towards effecting
the release of an old resident who had, unfortunately,
been imprisoned for four years on a certain charge.
Evidently the banqueters approved of the idea
cordially, it was so beautifully cheap ; and Mr.
Morton and Mr. Nicholson, of the State Parlia-
ment, promised to assist in the matter.
“ J.G.”: The hand of the land monopolist
is heavy upon Victoria, right up to the threshold
of the metropolis. Travel the first 30 or 40 miles
out of Melbourne along the Sydney-road and you
will see struggling townships now and then, with
here and thereafarm ; but the bulk of the country'
is given over to sheep, briar,
bracken and rabbits. All
the townships are about 50
years old ; but, instead of
growing, they are fading
gradually out of exist-
ence. The few odd
cookies are also being
gradually strangled
by the land sharks.
About Wallan, one
of the oldest town-
ships on the Syd-
ney' road, thous-
ands of acres of
land that could,
and should, be
maintaining
hundreds of
families, are
given over
to a
smattering
of the woolly
animal.
The land is
held in such
big areas
that the
owners do
not try to
keep the
briars,
bracken,
rabbits and
other pests
down, with
the result that
the whole
countryside
suffers. The
cocky puts up a
good fight, but too
frequently lie is
knocked out after a few
rounds, gives the land
best, and sells out to the
squatter. Then the township suffers through
the loss of the farmer and his family. In this
way, Wallan is losing its population, and, if
something isn’t done, the place will soon be dead.
Nearly all the townships in the district are wasting
away of the same disease. It is gruesome when
you remember that the blighted district is only
about an hour’s rail from Melbourne, and that it
contains some of the best land and about the best
climate in the State.
A paper of tremendous importance has
just been presented to the several Parliaments by
order of the various Figureheads—a paper pre-
pared “ At the Court at St. James’s, the 2nd day
of April, 1909. there being present His Royal
Highness the Prince of Wales (in large capitals),
Lord President, Lord Steward, Lord Haversham,
Lord Pentland.” “It is expedient,” says the
Court at St. James’s, after running itself out
of breath through reeling off some appalling
sentences —
Ifc is expedient with a view of equalising as far as may
be the conditions under which His Majesty’s subjects in
the British Dominions beyond the Seas shall have a right
of appeal to His Majesty in Council, and to promoting
uniformity in thepractice and procedure in all such appeals
that the said Order in Council dated the 13th of Novem-
ber, 1850, should he revoked and new provision
made for Appeals from the said Supreme
Court to His Majesty in Council.
Therefore His Royal Highness the
Prince of Wales, being authorised
thereto by writing under His
Majesty’s Sign Manual, doth
make some new and import-
ant rules; the more par-
ticularly relating to the
Record, or Statement of
the Case. And the
working part of the
tremendous revolu-
tion made by His
Highness, etc., being
authorised, etc., is
contained in a
schedule, as follows :
I. Records and Cases
in Appeals to His Majesty
in Council shall be
printed in the form
known as Demy Quarto
( i.e ., 54 ems in length
and 42 in width).
11. The size of the
paper used shall be such
that the sheet, when
folded and trimmed,
will he 11 inches in
height and 8J inches in
width.
111. The type to be used
in the text shall be Pica
type, but Longr Printer shall be
used in printing accounts, tabular
matter arid notes.
IV. The number of lines in each page
of Pica type shall be 47 or thereabouts, and
every tenth line shall be numbered in the margin.
Then, no doubt, having breathlessly authorised
this new Magna Charta, His Highness the Prince
of Wales staggered home and wondered what
other burden a nation would next think of putting
on a poor prince for a beggarly forty or fifty
thousand a year.
* * * *
‘‘ Malacoota : ” Along the Vic. coast, be-
tween Green Cape and Wilson’s Prom., population
is scanty and wrecks fairly frequent. The people
don’t pray for wrecks down there, because it is a
land without religious services, but they keep a
watchful eye seaward in bad weather. Old hands
talk enthusiastically of past windfalls, when eases
of kerosene and whisky and other portable com-
modities were gathered in great quantities from
their beaches. An American flour ship is a living
memory, also a German loaded with general mer-
chandise. One man got a case of repeating rifles
out of this vessel; he buried them in the sand
until a favorable time should arrive for their re-
moval. But an interfering police force stayed so
long in the vicinity of the wreck that he forgot the
place, and has been vainly endeavoring to locate
his cache ever since. Apropos, part of the hull
and the winch of an unknown ship were discovered
not so long ago near the Wingen. No.one knows
what the vessel was, or when or why she went
ashore. Another of the unsolved mysteries of
the sea.

The mysterious fascination of the Chow !
In Liverpool (England), where there is a regular
“ Chinese quarter,” the police report the greatest
difficulty in keeping young girls away from it.
One of the more respectable Chinese restaurants
has actually had to put up a notice, “ Ladies will
not be served.”

“ Cantab,” from the land of Ward and
Dreadnoughts:—
It is generally acknowledged that
the Maoriland police force is wretch-
edly underpaid. A constable starts
at 7s. 6d. per day, working seven days
in the week, and gets 6d. per day rise
every four years. A man must have
served 12 months in the force before
he is permitted to marry, and then he
receives an extra shilling a day as
“ house allowance.” An eminent
actuary has estimated the daily cost
of a policeman’s wife at is. The
allowance of holidays is 12 days in a
year. A constable must pay for his
uniform—which costs from 355. to £2
—and he also has to buy his own boots
and settle his own washing bills. In
some barracks the police, by catering
for themselves, manage to reduce
the cost of victualling per head to
£2 15s. or £3 for the month. But at
the smaller police stations it costs a
man fully £1 a week for his mess. I
believe the Commission now sitting
will recommend Is. a day increase in
pay and a few more holidays a year.
If the Maoriland thief destroyer isn’t
all that he might be, it must be ad-
mitted that he doesn’t get much
encouragement to repair his ways.
The shilling wife especially must be a
severe trial.
In quite a simple way the Law gob the
surprise of its life last week at Bega (N.S. W.). A
local parent was summoned under the Neglected
Children’s Act for not sending his boy to Sunday-
school. The boy had been before the Children’s
Court, and the magistrate had committed him
under the Act to the custody of his father on
probation. Now the Act provides that in such a
case the parent must comply with five conditions,
the fifth being that he is to despatch his offspring
regularly to a church and Sunday-school of his
own religious denomination. The Law carefully
proved all the formalities, such as the existence of
Bega, etc., and otherwise justified the charge.
Then it breathed heavily and demanded an ex-
planation. The accused simply said he hadn’t a
religious denomination, and the case fell dead on
the spot.
“ Hard Drinker ” writes :
The York Hotel, once Adelaide’s most famous caravan-
serai, is going to make a hid to recover its old-time
glories. Built far down Bundle-street, it is adjacent to
little but the Exhibition and the Zoo; so other pubs,
handier to the theatre, the railway station and the oval
have taken all the actors and cricket teams, and everyone
else who can afford big prices for hoard and lodging. A
year or two ago Foy and Gibson descended on Adelaide
and built an enormous series of shops just beyond the
York, thus bringing people into a district which was
falling into the hands of market-gardeners and Chinese
laundry-men. The Foy people bought up the York, and
it was thought that they might simply forfeit the license
and absorb it, as their chief rivals, Marshall and Co., have
eaten up the Globe, once the racing-man’s gathering
place. But they are rebuilding the house instead, with
the idea of making an up-to-date pub triumph over the
accident of its position. Syd Ferry, the old amateur
steeplechaser, has been manager of the York for years
past.
* * * *
At Pentridge Gaol (Vic.) a man recently
made an effort to hang himself, and died in the
attempt. Shortly afterwards, a medicine man, in
practice at Warracknabeal, wrote to the papers
deploring the state of affairs which led to the
man’s death. The deceased, it appeared, suffered
from the pot-hooking trouble. He had been in
the Warracknabeal doctor’s employ, and had
shown himself a good and willing worker ; but the
shifting of beer-pots had him down continually,
and a charge of vagrancy resulted in his being
shot in for a useless, expensive stay in a gaol.
VVith all his talk of Inebriate Retreats and Homes
for the Fallen Cigarette-Fiend, John No Hurry
still allows people suffering from the drink
disease to be passed in among the rogues and
thieves and wife-bashers, where anything in the
way of scientific treatment of the victim is out of
the question.

“ Philoyid ” writes :
There is said to be an absurd Masonic scheme for the
third re-building of Solomon’s Temple. (The second
re-building was by Herod the Great, shorty before the
appearance of Christ.) Why trouble over the luckless
structure? There seemed to be a curse on it from first
to last. It was the beginning of the disruption of the
kingdom ; when Jerusalem in Judah was made the
centre, not only of the government but of the national
religion, and the rest of the k.ngdorn was beggared to
build golden adornments for this one city, the great rival
tribe of Ephraim felt it was time to secede, audit took most
of Israel with it. Solomon was the child of crime ; his
father murdered his mother’s first husband in order to
,ecure her. And he lived up to his record, for he was
probably the most garish and expensive fool of his time.
There is no reason to believe that he wrote a line of the
works ascribed to him, and his reputation for wisdom was
based on his dabblings in magic and his pottering after
afreets, djinns, evil spirits, and such like trash. Also, he
was great on strange wives and strange idolatries. His
harem, his palace, and his temple got on Israel’s nerves.
Not only did he tax the people heavily to pay for them,
but he struck the gorgeous idea of making his subjects
tramp, at their own cost, to his public works every three
months, putin a month’s work there, and then tramp
back. As a result, the miserable backblocks Israelite
seems to have had about three months in the year at
home. No wonder he objected. On top of all Sol made
his hungry subjects come bodily to Jerusalem to see him
burn and destroy 22,000 buliocks and 120,000 sheep
at the dedication of his sacred building, and the
smell of overdone flesh was probably smelt as far as
Ephraim. Israel was a very small country, and the
chances are that the people had to live on vegetables for
years after that appalling waste. Solomon was the boss
centralisation man ; Jerusalem was made the only place
of worship, and the remote Hebrew was expected to hoof
it about 100 miles to church. Israel bucked at that, and
it probably bucked harder still when Solomon gave away
a slice of the kingdom to an adjacent idolator in return

for gold with which to decorate his amazing kirk. The
last, straw must have arrived when the nation found that its
great monarch was a cur. When the empire began to
fall to pieces, Solomon seems to have hidden behind the
kitchen sink ; anyhow, there is no mention that he ever
went out to battle, ltehoboam, his son, appears to have
been the original cigarette-fiend, or something like that;
which, considering the kind of father he had, was hardly
wonderful. No, that temple had better be left alone. It
brought no luck to anybody.
A PLAINT OF SENTIMENTALISM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Peninsula and Oriental Company has sold the
steamers Victoria and Britannia to an Italian company.
Cable Item.
Bartered away for Dago cash ! Ah me,
The callousness of trade !
It proves once more that sentiment can’t be,
Where money’s to be made.
For decades past these vessels have conveyed,
Along the sea-route worn
’Twixt here and London town, man, matron, maid,
Most of them British born.
There is no nook or cranny from the bows
Unto the twin-screws aft
Of these old ships but’s echoed to soft vows
And passionate. Abaft
Each hatchway, by each smoke-stack, ’neath each
mast
Fond folks have lurked and told
Their loves, careless of any tropic blast,
Undaunted by the cold
Regardless even of the Seedee boy
And quick eared Lascar who
Prowled ever round —and aye, they voiced their joy
In the tongue Shakspeare knew.
There’s not an inch of all that wide deck space
Which is not haunted by
Some Anglo-Saxon figure, voice or face
That’s now a memory.
It is no ordinary deal, this last—
The point’s clear at a glance—
More than a vend of ships ’tis. It’s a vast
Job sale of Old Romance.
N.B.W.
