The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The agreement which the Fused Deakin fixed
up the other day with the State Premiers looks pretty
disastrous whichever way it is considered. For the first
year the Commonwealth will have to pay the States about
£5,375,000; it will have to pay old-age pensions, formerly
a State liability (about £1,800,000); and it is to make a
special payment of £125,000 to Westralia ; total about
£7,300,000. That means that if the net revenue from
Customs and Excise gets down to £9,125,000, the Com-
monwealth will be just as badly off as ever. Now, the net
tariff revenue in 1906, according to Knibbs’s figures, was
down to £8,721,896, less £154,709 rebate of the sugar excise,
making a mere £8,567,187. The new tariff rushed the net
tariff revenue up to £11,333,145 in 1908, less £584,622 re-
bate on sugar—total £10,748,523. It is now rapidly on the
down grade again—it dropped £BOO,OOO by June, 1909
for the growth of manufactures is reducing the imports
of dutiable goods. So it is fairly probable that, even in
the first year of the new arrangement, the Common-
wealth will be no better off than it was under the
Braddon clause, before the Commonwealth took over the
old-age pension liability. Then the payments to the
•States are to increase automatically ever3 r year for all
time, \\-hether the Federal revenue increases or not—they
are to increase with the growth of population. And the
old-a<re pension liability will also increase. As the price
of this utterly disastrous agreement, the States are to
allow the Commonwealth this year £600,000, to which it
isn’t entitled under the Braddon clause, but that
is much less than N.S.W. and Victoria save through the
Commonwealth taking over their old-age pension liabili-
ties. And even then the Fused Government will, by its own
calculations, end the financial year and stare the new
arrangement with £600,000 of a shortage. For a Govern-
ment that is pledged not to levy direct taxation, and
which has the Northern Territory, the two transconti-
nental railways, the Federal capital and the defence
policj r on its mind, the situation could hardly be worse,
and the agreement made with the State Premiers couldn't
be much worse.

The Melbourne municipalities formed themselves into a
Trust, and borrowed the money to build the tramways ;
then they handed them over to this co. on lease till June
30, 1916, which is seven years from now. The co. was re-
quired (1) To pay interest on the Trust’s debt; (2) to
provide a sinking fund for the extinction of the Trust’s
debt; (3) to keep the lines in repair and hand them back
in good working condition. The sinking fund contribu-
tion was £33,000 a year till 1905 ; it was then to be
£45,000, at which figure it still stands. So, considering
the tramways as a business, this is how they have panned
out during the past two years :
„ , 1908. 1909.
Pi ofit as shown above .. .. £148,315 £167,906
Sinking fund for Trust .. .. 45,000 45,000
Trust’s debenture interest .. 74,250 74,250
_ . Total £267,565 £287,156
Dess interest on co.’s investments 26,190 28,165
Net revenue of undertaking .. £241,375 £258,991
The total income was £580,495. All working expenses,
including wages, salaries, rates, insurance, fuel, repairs
and maintenance (£69,681), and depreciation of lease-
holds were thus £321,504. All working expenses thus
absorbed 54 "4 percent, of the revenue, leaving 44 - 6 per
cent, for interest, dividend, or return of capital.
Remarkable progress has been made by the co.
during the past 10 years. In the table at the top of this the
dividend is calculated on the £250,000 actually paid-up
by shareholders; but another £230,000 has been capi-
talised out of profits. Notwithstanding that dividends
calculated on this basis have steadily moved up from
Hi Per cent, in 1899 to 38 2-5 per cent, in 1909, the co.
has saved up liquid assets to the extent of £720,697
during these 10 years; but as the debts have increased by
£144,915, the net accumulation for the 10 years is
£575,782. The movement of liquid and other assets has
been as follows;

It will be seen that “ freeholds ” and “ other property ”
have shrunk in value. (“Other property ” consists'of
cars,; material, rope, stock, etc.) There may have been
some sales ; but apparently these assets have been mostly
written down out of profits. Whether, however, they
are worth their book value there is no way of testing.
They may be worth much more—if they can find a buyer.
The municipalities take over the business on July 1, 1916.
Of course, the cable system will be superseded by the
electric, which will destroy most of the value of many of
these assets. But in the interval between July 1, 1916,
and electrification something will have to be used ; ’so the
management of this co. evidently reckons that its cars,
grips, ropes, bell-punches, etc., will be worth a good deal!
The reserves as shown above (including the
£230,000 which has been transferred to capital) are
£937,336. This amount is represented as follows (figures
for 1899 and 1908 added for comparison) :

The goodwill item mentioned above was the interest on
the Trust’s debt and the liability for sinking fund instal-
ments accumulated while the' lines were being con-
structed. This “ asset ” was gradually written off out of
profits. Probably some of the reserves are not of the best
character—the renewal reserve, for instance: this is
being drawn upon to a small extent. Assuming that
during the next seven years the past seven years’ history
will be repeated, we get this result:—
Seven years’ profits £873,609
Present reserves 937,336
Capital 250,000
Total £2,060,945
Provided the freeholds and “other property” realised their
book value, that would give £2 2s. lOd. for distribution
per each 960,000 th share during the ensuing seven years,
or as soon thereafter as the business is completed.
But in connection with that forecast three things
have to be remembered: —(l) The profits have increased
very rapidly, and they may possibly go on increasing,
while in this forecast only the average of the past seven
3'ears has been taken and applied to the next seven years;
(2) the interest on the accumulations swells every'year ;
but, on the other hand (3), the co. may possibly not
realise book value for its freeholds and “ other property.”

This co. had some heavy losses in the’nineties, both by
fire and by depreciation of securities. It has, however, a
fine, solid, and cheaply held connection in Victoria, and
has made a good recovery ; yet it isn’t by any means the
most hustling office in Australia, and the growth of its
business hasn’t been at all remarkable. Its expense rate
is, however, extremely moderate; and that has been
largely responsible for the very fair accumulation of
reserves in spite of the big dividends. The paid capital is
only £50,181; but adding the reserves to that, the total
funds equal 183 per cent, of the past year’s premiums ;
and taking the premiums to be the best measure of the
risks, this is very satisfactory. For 1907—the last year for
which figures are yet available—the average proportion of
capital and reserves held by the 19 great British fire
offices was only 141 per cent. Ten years ago the Victoria
Co.’s position was v»3aker than the present British
average—it then had only 129 per cent, of reserves com-
pared with premiums—but to-day its position is just
about one-third stronger, on figures, than even the British
average ; and the British average is far stronger than
that of most of the European Continental cos. with
which so many of the British offices have re-insurance
treaties. Some other comparisons to show the Victoria’s
history

The co. does a little accident and guarantee business
also ; and that makes its expense rate seem all the more
moderate. Its assets are more liquid than those of some
offices ; over £82,000 is on deposit, apart from branch
balances. Another £46,333 is in real estate (book value) ■
and that is probably a weak feature. Anyway, the
interest return, on the average of all the invest-
ments, is only about £3 6s. per cent. With so
much out on deposit a high return is not to be looked
for ; but £3 6s. is a very low average, and suggests either
over-valuation of the real estate, or that the co. does not
credit its interest earnings with rent for its own office
premises—which would, of course, mean on the other
side that the expenses are not charged with the same
item. But whether this is the practice or not you can’t
tell from the accounts. All that you can tell is that both
interest earnings and expense rate are very low, and the
lowness of the one rubs some of the gilt off the lowness of
the other.

The co. is doing one of two improper things. Either it is
writing off too much for depreciation, and is thus under-
stating its true profits ; or if it is stating its true profits
correctly, then it is paying away too much in dividends—
for the second term it has paid away more than it is shown
to have earned. And it is not as though it was over-
whelmed with reserves ; all it has, in fact, are. these :
Share premiums .. . .£13,687
Marine insurance fund .. 3278
Undivided profit .. .. 128
Total .. .. £17,093
The share premiums ought not in any circumstances to be
paid away in dividends ; the marine'insurance fund is not
the best sort of a reserve, being, in fact, an accumulation
against a risk which hasn’t expired ; and all that is left
over and beyond that is £l2B. Of course there may be a
hidden reserve, created by using profits to write down
ship and other properties below their true value. It would
be an amazing thing, though, if a management deliber-
ately wrote off too much and left insufficient net profits
to pay the dividend. Certainly the Profit and Loss
account for the half-year does show enough of a balance
to pay the dividend ; but from that balance has to be
deducted £422, which has been taken out of the marine
insurance reserve, presumably to make good a loss
Altogether, the position of the co. is most uncertain. It
was in very low wafer for a long while, and had a fleet of
mostly decrepit arks ; but then it sprouted afresh, got in
more capital, bought two or three new boats, secured
quantities of land for picnic places, and generally reached
out for the harbor traffic. This shows how the fi«- ures
nave moved:— 0

The “ other assets” include £3600 of secured debt, pre-
sumably money owing- on land sold by the co. Liabilities
have been reduced rapidly, partly out of new capital and
premiums, partly out of sales, partly no doubt out of
revenue written off ships to cover wear and tear. The
ship item has been reduced by £3809 in 18 months ; and
as no additions have been made to the fleet, while one
small vessel, the Pearl, has disappeared, it does not look
as though very much has been written off for deprecia-
tion. Probably some renewals have been effected ; but
even allowing for that the true profits appear to have
been by no means under-stated. In that case the divi-
dends are being barely earned.

The society was landed in the usual trouble by the tidal
wave of the boom in the late ’eighties and early ’nineties,
and from 1892 to 1900 it paid no div. The progress
towards filling up the hole was, however, so small, and
the absence of a div. was putting such a cheek on busi-
ness, that the directors finally took the hippopotamus by
the horn and wrote off half the capital. After that
things looked better. A contingency fund was created
to cover possible losses on sales ; but there have been
none of much consequence. In fact, as one column in
the foregoing table shows, properties have usually given'
a net surplus over their written-down values. Since the
cleaning up, various items have moved like this
Jan.,’ol. June,’oB. Jan.,’o9. June,’o9
Deposits, etc. £37,015 £28,710 £30,741 £31,174
Advances .. 19,998 43,188 50,055 53,418
Foreclosures. 72,305 51,886 49,528 47,814
Cash, etc. .. 2,778 2,760 758 109
The society has thus been going very slowly. Though
the deposits and other liabilities have grown lately, they
are still less than they were in 1901; and though the
advances have in 8£ years gone up from £19,998 to £53,418,
the money has been got either from the sale of foreclosed
properties—-mostly old written-down properties—or from
accumulated profits. The paid capital is £47,797 ; so the
borrowed money in the business is little, and consequently
the risks are unusually small. Indeed, only the severity
of the uriting down and the excellence of the rent
return from properties in possession—about £6 7s. 6d.
per cent, net—enable so good a dividend to be paid.

N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice Co. (Sydney)

Dec., 1905.. 7127 Bp.c. = £3669 3458 23,062

Dec., 1906.. 7897 8,, ~ 3669 4228 30,620
June, 1907.. 7546 10 ~ ~ 4586 2960 33,580
Dec., 1907.. 6287 6 ~ ~ 2752 99 3436 37,016
June, 1905.. 5635 6 ~ ~ 2752 99 2784 39,800
Dec., 1908.. 4996 6,, ~ 2752 2244 42,044
June, 1909.. 6458 6,, ~ 2752 3706 45,750
The profit and loss account shows for the half-year “Net
profits after providing for renewals, etc., £6272”; but,
besides that, £lB6 has dropped into the Redemption fund.
Renewals, it will be noticed, are charged up with
working expenses. There is, however, a Depreciation
fund, £21,141 ; and a reserve for renewals and eontin-
g-encies, £10,654. Possibly 7 these reserves are in whole or
part a reserve against actual wear and tear and loss of value;
and in that case the additions to these reserves are not true’
profits. In other words, if the renewals (size and extent
unknown) don’t fully counterbalance the depreciation of
plant, then neither the profits nor the reserves are really
as large as they are said to be. If they were, of course,
the surplus in excess of dividend just now would be
very fine. One suspicious circumstance is that five
years ago the assets were re-valued, and no less than
£118,293 had to be written off, partly out of reserves, but
mostly out of capital. This practically meant that for
many years the profits had been badly over-estimated,
and that some of the dividends had been paid out of capi-
tal without the directors knowing it. And the last is
easily possible. It isn’t merely that plant wears out, but
in this sort of business it is liable to become obsolete very
quickly, whereupon it has to be superseded. What
revenue ought to be provided against this probability
can only be guessed at. If a management guesses too
little, then it makes the profits too high—for the profits
are really only a guess, too. In this case the directors
apparently write off nothing at all ; they simply pay for
renewals. Yet.it is quite likely that something extra in
the way of renewals will arrive presently, and then some
of the present reserves will have to be written off—which
is another way of saying that some of the past alleged
profits will have to be written off. The better profits
shown for the term just ended are, no doubt,
partly due to the bigger export trade in frozen
meat (this co. supplies the cold storage). The
high prices which ruled after the last drought meant that
it didn’t pay to buy for export ; so this co. suffered.
Now, however, there is a big surplus, and it will go on
swelling until the next drought comes along, and sweeps
off a few million head. The co. is now in 183,460 shares
of 12s. paid to 10s. The financial position has improved
considerably since the last cleaning up :
June, 1904. June, 1909.
Liabilities £31,188 .. £17,361
Liquid assets (including stock) 42,267 .. 49,766
Surplus .. .: .. £11,079 .. £32,405
Most of this improvement occurred prior to June, 1907 ;
in the past two years practically all the undivided profit
has gone into new plant, etc.; and possibly this was
necessary merely in order to make good the wear and
tear of the previous three years.

The year’s results look better on the surface than they do
underneath ; for only £4l has been written off against
depreciation, and that was only on a side line. The
machinery, plant and fittings stand in the books at
£22,170, exclusive of buildings. This machinery, no
doubt, becomes obsolete very quickly ; still, assuming
that even 10 per cent, per annum is enough to provide
for renewals, £2217 ought to be put aside to cover wear
and tear, etc. If that were done, an actual loss would
be shown. Of course, it may be said that so much
was written off two or three years ago that it isn’t
necessary to write anything off now. But that
doesn’t alter the fact that the true profits for
the year are less than the published figures show—
it would simply mean that the necessary writing-off was
done in advance and out of capital. However, the posi-
tion has obviously much improved. Under the old State
tariff the co. didn’t pay anything reasonable, but it paid
something; in 1896 the dividend was 2£ per cent., equal
to 5 per cent, on the present capital. It was clear that
the industry couldn’t do with less Protection than one
which only gave the manufacturer 2J per cent. ; neverthe-
less, even some Queenslanders voted for low duties when
Federation arrived. The collapse that followed was so
bad that it was a question whether the business should
not be abandoned, and had not greater assistance
arrived through the Lyne tariff only one thing could
have happened. Even now the future is none too clear ;
for there is an overdraft of £21,298, while the
paid capital is only £23,339, and the need of new
machinery presses upon the co. During the past year the
debt to the bank certainly was reduced by £2070 ; but
not much was done in the way of plant renewals or
additions, and that is likely to be a pretty heavy item in
the future.

Goodlet and-Smith, Ltd. (Sydney), who manu-
facture and deal in builders’ goods:—

June, Profits. Dividends. serves, serves.
1906 .. £12,921 6 per cent. =£9,900 £3021 £22,424
1907 .. 14,589 7 „ „ „ 11,550 3039 25,463
1908 .. 16,944 8 „ „ ~ 13,200 3744 29,207
1909 .. 17,636 8 ~ „ ~ 13,200 4436 33,643
This co. was formed in 1902 to take over a co. which was
formed 10 years earlier to purchase the old-established
business of Goodlet and Smith. The earlier co. was only
very moderately prosperous ; and the new co., though it
started with a 6 per cent, dividend, had to reduce it later
on. By 1905, however, flush harvests, big mineral yields
and high prices all round put heaps of money into the
pockets of the people ; and Federation and Protection
caused a big share of it to be spent on this
and other cos.’ Australian manufactures. Hence the
great prosperity shown above. No doubt, owing to the
building boom, this co. has had a very special share of
the fatness of these last few years ; and tlie figures may
ease olf by and bye. On the other hand (l) Federation
and Protection are here for good now, and (2)it is quite
possible that during - these last few years considerable
sums have been spent on additions to plant and have
been paid for out of undisclosed profits. Some of the
balance-sheet figures are traced hereunder
1906. 1907. 1908. 1909.
Liabilities .. ..£90,176 £91,923 £95,348 £100,104
Properties .. .. 100,670 100,670 100,670 100,670
Machinery, plant, etc. 89,867 89,867 89,867 89,861
Ships 2,200 2,050 540 500
Stock 62.685 59,402 66,914 77,161
Cash and book debts 32,177 36,997 39,815 37,149
Properties and machinery, plant, etc., thus remain un-
altered in the balance-sheet ; but whether the additions
paid for out of revenue have more than counterbalanced
the depreciation, it is impossible to say. Yet almost
everything depends on that. The co. may be stuffing
away thousands of pounds of profits, or it may be writing
off too little arid thus eat.ing up its capital. In favor of
the theory that the additions are real additions is the fact
that the profits have been continuously on the up-
grade, and that looks like an increased output. The
capital is £165,000: in proportion to that the
liabilities are rather heavy. In good times, no doubt,
the co. makes a rattling profit out of some of
this debt (its nature isn’t specified); but in bad times
borrowed money, carrying a rate of interest which doesn’t
then come down, is liable to be a heavy burden. Cos.
with heavy debts are therefore always likely to show
erratic net profits ; especially in a country like this which
depends so much on industries that hangupon the uncer-
tain seasons. With regard to the “ ships ” item, the
shVinkage isn't due so much to writing down as to sales.

In 1900 the capital was cut down from £168,750 to
£loB,ooo—and the shareholders didn’t get the £60,750,
either. Anyway, not then ; though they might have had
some of it previously in the shape of excessive dividends.
The cleaning-up wasn’t, however, followed by any great
improvement in the look of the published accounts. The
trouble appears to be a heap of borrowed money on one
side and a heap of unproductive assets on the other.
Unfortunately, the whole of the property—hotels,
brewery, land, etc.—is lumped into one item ;
therefore, it isn’t possible to say the figure
at which the large Cascade estate stands in the
books. It must be something very considerable, though.
An effort is being made to sell parts of this property in
allotments ; but all that the directors commit themselves
to is a very vague remark now and then—“ Sales still
continue.” The accounts don’t suggest that anything
very much is being done. Meantime, while so much pro-
perty is earning little or nothing, the co. has to pay
interest on over £llO,OOO of debt—more, that is, than the
shareholders’ entire capital. This gives the position in
detail: —

Gross profit on goods.. .. 11,599 11,547 10,447
Rents 3,482 4,108 4,085
Management, discounts, etc... 7,698 9,302 7,938
Interest paid 3,054 2,771
So freeholds, etc., are still swelling ; and, though a few
repairs are charged up to profit and loss, nothing else is
written off revenue to provide for the general decay of
premises, plant, etc. It is just possible, no doubt,
that che unearned increment on the freeholds is
a good set off to this decay; but unearned
increment is an animal of very gradual and uncertain
growth in Hobart and suburbs ; while decay is sure and
rapid. It seems highly probable, then, that something
ought to be written off ; and yet after paying a 6 per
cent. div. there is mighty little left to write off. It isn’t,
therefore, impossible that at some future date it will be
found that the 6 per cent shouldn’t have been paid. Any-
way, it is to be hoped that this matter of possible depre-
ciation of value has been considered : because if later on
some more capital has to be written off on a revaluation
of assets, it is likely that hard things will be said.

The erection of the new 300-ton Blast Smelting
Furnace for the Great Fitzroy mine, Q., is now rapidly
approaching completion at the mine. The dimensions of
this furnace are as follows—l6Bin. x 44in., and 24ft.
high to feed floor. It is water jacketed from top to
bottom. These jackets are made of steel plates flanged
in one piece. The furnace was designed by the mine
superintendent, and has many features of excellence and
novelty in the detail of its construction. The jackets are
made interchangeable, only one type and size of jacket
being used ; and they will further be-constructed so that
they are reversible from top to bottom —thus practically
the life of the jackets is doubled. This furnace, as well as
the previous one, was built at the works of the Austral
Otis Engineering Company, South Melbourne.* [2

* Company’s own ore only ; but the metals recovered

Besides the loss shown above for the half-year, £8241 is
written off for depreciation. Owing to labor troubles,
the works were shut down most of the term, and the
mine is still being worked on only a very small scale,
pending a recovery in metal values. The co.’s own
reserves of ore are now comparatively small; wherefore
it is laying itself out to do a big custom-smelting busi-
ness—it is quite satisfied to let others root out their ore
when metals are cheap, intending to rush things in its
own mine when they are dear. The co. had in hand over
10,000 tons of purchased concentrates, etc., when the
trouble started at the beginning of this year, and it had
to pay for these goods ; but when it got into a position to
smelt and market the stuff the lead market was much
weaker ; hence a loss on the transaction. The half-year’s
figures may therefore be ruled out so far as being any
basis for valuing the mine is concerned. Still they sug-
gest one of the constantly-recurring possibilities ahead of
Broken Hill; for nothing was settled by the recent
trouble. The co. has a surplus of £406,600 of more or less
liquid assets, as against £457,730 six months ago and
£452,503 on May 30, 1908.

Besides these reserves there is a contingency account,
which has been built up in the last 18 months to £5OOO ;
it is probably a fund which may be drawn on in case the
co. finds that it has over-estimated the value of its
output through a sudden fall in metal values. The
co from which the present co. bought this mine (it
was really a reconstruction), secured the whole turn-out
for a trifle ; but the developments at depth have been
wonderfully good. Last half-year the net profit was
£14,209 ; that was after writing off 10 p.c. of the book
value of plant for depreciation, and after paying for all
development, including shaft-sinking. The net profit
works out at 4s. 3d. per ton. No estimate of ore reserves
is now published ; 12 months ago they were put at
1,100,000 tons. About 140,000 tons have come out since
then ; but assuming that development has equalled ex-
traction, then at 4s. 3d. per ton the reserves are worth
£233,750, or 335. per share. An extra £3 per ton for lead
would bring the profit up to 11s. 6d. per ton, or £632,500
for the lot—£4 10s. per share. The present market
value is £4 6s. A new mill is going up ; it will be finished
about the end of the year, and after that the output (and
very likely the profits) will be about doubled—while the
ore lasts. North B. H. ore reserves will, however, have
to be watched. As said just now, the reserves 12 months
ago were 1,100,000 tons ; and even allowing for good
developments, a mill capable of eating up over 250,000
tons a year will very soon get to the bottom of that lot.
How much further down the ore goes nobody knows.
* * * '*
The tailings deal made by the Broken Hill Junc-
tion directors looks bad for the sellers on the face of it, but
under the surface it looks worse for the man who did the
buying. Many years ago, when the Junction was treat-
ing rich semi-friable ore from the upper levels, tailings
were dumped which, owing to their being slightly porous,
offered special facilities for the air to creep in anil oxidise
them. This oxidisation has been going on for
years to a greater degree than in the dumps of
the other mines, and the flotation processes at
the present time won’t look at the stuff on that
account. The tailings could be re-ground and treated
over concentrating tables, but the oxidised coating on
the lead would be washed away with the slimes. The big
dump, which is the bone of contention, doesn’t assay more
than 7 per cent, lead, and as 2 per cent, of that is not
recoverable, it would take 17 tons of such tailings to make
one ton of concentrates, assaying perhaps 56 per cent,
lead and 260 z. silver. That is better work than was
accomplished when tailings from the old dump were put
through the mill in 1906-7 ; but even that would land the
co. in a small loss. The co.’s previous experiment, made
in 1906, showed a loss of £l2O a week. Naturally the
directors didn’t want any more of that. Although a sec-
tion of the shareholders is kicking against the deal, it
seems to be one of the sanest things the directors have
done for many a day.
* * * *
The old Prince Charlie, at Copeland (N.S.W.),
which was worked down to 260 ft. some 20 vears ago, is
being opened up again for Capper Bros., of Maitland. A
tunnel has been driven for 1200 ft., and a 10-stamp bat-
tery has been put up. The reef is erratic ; but in the past
10 months three parcels have been put through—2so tons
for 15dwt. per ton ; 60 tons, 60oz. ; 75 tons, 750 z. All
in addition to concentrates. Other claims are also being
worked. Years ago the surface at Copeland was very rich
in pockets, and the place can boast of a 350 z. lump.
* » * *
Kyloe (N.S.W.), formerly owned and badly-
handled by an Adelaide co., is now run from Sydney.
Sydney mining cos. used to be the most hopeless in Aus-
tralia ; but things have improved in the last few years.
Capital is £60,000 in 20,000 10 per cent. pref. shares (10s.
each)and 100,000 ordinaries, also 10s., both lots fully paid.
Present accounts are for five months to July 3. Revenue
(including “ ore on hand, £4042”) was £10,754 ; expendi-
ture, £4652 ; balance profit, £6102. From this is written,
off £4047 for depreciation and preliminary exes., leaving
£2055 net. But besides new plant, etc., £2673 spent on
mine development has been charged to capital account
and not to working expenses. The financial position on
July 3 was—Liabilities, £265 ; liquid assets (including esti-
mated net value of ore on hand), £9189; surplus, £8924.
The half-year’s results are, however, not of much value as a
criterion, because almost all the ore won has been from
development work. J, VV. Ashcroft, the managing direc-
tor, estimates a profit of £32,762 from the proved ore,
and another £23,875 from the “ probable ” ore between
300 ft. and 400 ft. But from this £9187 is to be written off
to cover cost of development, charged up to capital
account, leaving £47,450 net. This is on the basis of
copper at £55 per ton. A movement of £5 either way, if
it applied to the whole lot, would make a difference of
£16,450. These calculations are, however, based to a
great extent on the new concentrating mill, which has
yet to show what it can do, and, as a rule, a concentrat-
ing mill takes a good while to do anything satisfactory.
On the basis of 19s. for pref. and 17s. for ordinaries, the
market at present values the mine at £104,000. Kyloe
promises to be a good property ; but the market is dis-
counting the future with tremendous energy.

Associated Gold Mines, Ltd. (W.A.), netted
£125,792 for the year ended March last, and after writing
off £64,234 for development, shaft-sinking, diamond-drill-
ing and depreciation, there is a balance (ineluding£l4,3Bs
brought forward) of £75,943. Dividends and dividend
duty absorbed 165,016, and directors’extra remuneration
took £1238, leaving £9688 to be carried forward. Invest-
ments, which include £130,778 in high-grade securities,
are valued at £136,530. During the year £107,000 Consols
were sold and the proceeds put into Transvaal 3 pfer cents.
Cash resources amount to a further £47,293, from which
has to be deducted £12,526 due to creditors.
* * * *
Chaffers’ first clean up at the Boulder Main
Reef mill (W.A.) showed a profit of £ll4O. Mining costs
were high, hut this is put down to the heavy initial ex-
penditure in timbering, and the fact that the treatment
necessitates the separation of the oxidised and sulphide
ore in the stopes, which are at the junction of the two
zones. A considerable reduction in costs is promised
within the next few months. Chairman Lewis Aarons,
who once ran Aarons’ Exchange in Sydney and then
became a member of the London Stock Exchange, visits
the mine early in October.
* * » *
Kalgoorlie mineowners are to pay a trifle more
for their water after September. They are now supplied
at ss. per 1000 gallons, with a rebate to 3s. 6d. in the case
of low-grade shows, and to Is. 6d. if the fluid is used for
sluicing tailings ; but as it costs the State 9s. to supply
that quantity, an increase to 7s. 3d. is commended to the
“sympathetic consideration” of the purchasers. The
latter realise that some extra charge is inevitable, because
for years past the scheme hasn’t paid anything at all
towards the statutory sinking fund ; so it is probable that
the amount so politely asked for will be handed out
without much of a murmur.
* * * *
The small syndicate which recently got hold of
the North Boulder (W.A.) for next to nothing, has raised
800 tons of something described as “ high-grade stuff,” and
the English co., which previously owned the show,
must be sorry it didn’t hold on. The whereabouts of
several patches of ore are known to the present owners ;
but when these have been taken out the troubles will
start. Just about that time the public may be asked to
come in and share the wealth.
* * * *
Outlay still exceeds income.at the Boulder No. 1
Co.’s St. George mine (W.A.). The management reports
the discovery that by mixing hard ore from below 100 ft.
with the soft pug above this point the Ridgeway filters can
do better work ; but shareholders are dog-tired of being
buoyed up with this or that variety of encouragement,
and are beginning to ask how much longer operations are
to be carried on at a loss. As previously remarked, it
would be more sensible for the co. to give treatment best
for a time than go on dropping its bundle in this way.
* • * *
A belated circular informs Australian holders
that Golden Link (W.A.) now has 80,000 tons of from 395.
to 425. ore in sight, which vigorous development is ex-
pected to increase to 120,000 tons of 40s. ore before the
end of the year. This tonnage, it is claimed, will justify
the erection of a recovery plant with a capacity of be-
tween 4000 and 5000 tons a month, and costs (including
development) are not expected to exceed 21s. 6d. to
225. 6d. per ton. The circular also states that the plant
can be completed within six months after it has been
ordered, and by the time it is ready for work a very sub-
stantial reserve of payable ore should be available. But
as this puts the treatment clock back at least twelve
months—six months to find the ore and six months to
find the plant—the shares have started to sag - .

* * * *
The death knell of the Cumberland (W. A.) is
sounded. For some years this mine helped to keep
Norseman going, but, like several other shows which
lived and died in the district, its span of life does not
extend for more than a few hundred feet below the sur-
face. The co. admits that the end is coming, and is
getting ready to go into mourning. There is still a little
money in hand, and shareholders may be asked before
long to club together and find some more so as to finance
the purchase of a new property.
* * * *
British Exploration of Westralia, Ltd., has
reduced its capital from £500,000 to £200,000 in 400,000
10s. shares, and has tacked on an assessment of 4s. per
share. Since its promotion in 1900 by A. E. Morgans, it
has never done anything to vindicate its existence, and
how shareholders came to assess themselves so heavily in
order to give it a bit more rope is not understandable.
Better have let the bank foreclose, as it seems hopeless to
expect, with the burdens it is now carrying and the in-
different prospects ahead, that it will ever pay dividends.
Black Range (W.A.), which didn’t feel very
bad before, feeis still better now that a good lens has
been picked up north of the crosscourse. When the half-
yearly report was made up (duly 26), the No. 4 level
(350 ft.) had been taken out 103 ft., the last 23ft. averaging
2ft. of stone worth 15dwt. Now, less than a month later,
the stone is sft. wide and still worth 15dwt. The deeper
levels have not gone out far enough to pierce the cross-
course ; but there is now no apparant reason why the
gold should not go down as well on the north as on the
south side of the break. The half-year gave a profit of
£20,480, after paying for all the development work
(£4483), and writing off plant depreciation (£1170), and
the £5OO paid for the Youanme option which the co.
turned down. Money spent on the Pioneer option is also
included with the expenditure. On June 30 the co. had :
Liquid assets (excluding stores) .. £20,251
Liabilities 141
Surplus £20,110
There are 72,500 shares in the present co. ; the old co. in
1905-6 paid £23,625 in divs., and the new co. has brought
up the total to £114,125, of which £16,312 was paid last
half-year. Any statement as to ore reserves wouldn’t be
worth much ; all that can be said is in a general way that
there appear to be more years ahead of the mine" than
there are at the back of it.

The first report and accounts of the Great
Pitzroy (y.) are out. This is for the new co., formed to
succeed the one originally pushed off by G. P. Doolette
and Philip Charley. Period covered is from July 17,1908,
to June 30, 1909—nearly 12 months. The new co. took
over the whole concern and issued 374,262 shares paid to
18s. ; then 2s. was got in = £37,426. Robinson and Clark,
of London, took 25,388 shares nominally at par (£1), and
thus the co. has had £62,814 to go on with. On June 30
its financial position was—
Assets. —Stores, etc, .. ..£26,233
Cash, etc. .. .. 21,641
£47,874
Liabilities.—Sundry .. .. 4,519
Surplus £43,355
Heavy expenditure is being incurred ; so Lionel Robin-
son’s firm has been given a two years’ option over 100,000
more shares at par ; the directors may call on them to
take up 15,000 shares by the end of November. As the
market is standing up at over 255. Robinson will, no
doubt, be willing. A further 50,000 shares have been
taken by the Mac Georges, of London ; but that is since the
accounts were issued. Twq bigger furnaces and a concen-
trating mill are going up, with other plant; one furnace
is ready. Using only the old 100-ton smelter the
co.’s working account showed £16,967 profit. But
knock off £2500 for commission on new capital
issue (which means that the new 25,000 shares only
netted the co. 18s. a piece), £2299 for general exes",
£11,687 for depreciation, development, etc., and only
£5OO remains to carry forward. As to the development
account: £9672 was charged to capital, and £4565 is now
written off, leaving £5107 to be provided from future
profits. Still, this is a pretty liberal writing off, as over
600,000 tons have been added to reserves.
Great Fitzrov reserves total 981,000 tons, with
250,000 tons of “probable” ore. The ore handled was
34,261 tons ; the working account revenue equalled £2 2s.
Bd. per ton. Costs (including management, but excluding
development and depreciation) were £ll4s. per ton, leaving
Bs. Sd. profit. Costs will no doubt be reduced ; they may
possibly be brought down to 30s. per ton, including every-
thing. But the average value of the reserves appears to
be at the least 7s. 6d. per ton less than that handled
during the year. Further, when the concentrating mill
starts losses will be heavier. It is very doubtful, there-
fore, whether the reduced costs will not be more than
counterbalanced. If so, Ss. per ton is a very liberal estimate
of profits, even taking copper at £6O as a basis. 8., M.
and Co., the general managers, a while ago figured on a
“ surplus ” of £lO,OOO a month from 18,000 tons ; but that
was with copper at £6O. At £55, which is a safer figure,
about £2600 would have to come off that; and the £lO,OOO
(equal to about 11s. 2d. per ton) apparently didn’t allow
for management exes. At Bs. Bd. (last half-year’s margin)
a million tons would be worth £433,330 = 21s. Bd. per
share on the number of shares issued at the date of the
last balance.
* « * *
East Mexican, Charters Towers, is sending out
two footwall crosscuts in search of a reef parallel to the
main Day Dawn. The latter was followed for 100 ft. along
the Mexican’s bottom level, which is a continuation of
No. 6in Mills’ United. The rich stone carried downwards
towards the New Brilliant Freeholds’ top level (now
known as No. 1 East) ; but apparently the good stone has
pinched out in the higher levels of the E. Mexican, for
No. 5 has failed to find anything payable, although some
good stone is being stoped further west along the level on
Mills’ United side. East Mexican’s position is most tanta-
lising ; it is practically hedged in by payable stone, which
occurs just outside its boundary. Those who hold on
believe that by extending a level along the boundary
something may be found. Even the fact that No. 5
failed to find a payable shoot doesn’t altogether damp
their hopes. The theory about a footwall reef is now
being tested by the two crosscuts.
* * * *
Cassilis, Gippsland, has a maw as wide as a
rock-cod’s. Twelve months back itembarked on a hydro-
electric scheme, necessitated by the working out of local
fuel supplies. To date, £19,000 has gone on that project,
and there still are oddments wanted—a new motor or a
new air compressor. So profits have been diverted to the
machinist or the German manufacturer. All the while
the mill has been eating up the ore reserves. These
now tot up only 9240 tons, containing 8680 oz.
On that basis the mine, with working costs at 355.,
shows £16,170 profit in sight. With a register of 60,000
shares, that is ss. 4d. per share. But £3871 is due to the
bank, and the air compressor will take another £2500
before it gets at work. Therefore, a third of the profit
is earmarked. Crosscutting is going on for the lode at
715 ft. The co. wants to hit a shoot like the one at No. 6,
but the country is very tight and the lenses of ore seem
to be hardly so persistent at depth. On the other hand,
extensions of the Cerisa and the Cassilis lodes are being
developed, so there is still a hope that the machinery man
may be paid off and a bit of profit set free for shareholders.
Good old Jubilee, of Scarsdale (Vie.)! Here it
comes with a record that smells of fat men and fat divi-
dends. Never a look back since Tom Harvey spotted the
work that the old co-operative party was doin')-. Now,
with a modest plant, it has put up a record of £132,000 in
divs., and has got down to 1600 ft., with the co. still on
what Aleoclc, true to Bendigo training, describes as
“ payable ” stone. All the same, there is a shrinkage in
the values treated, partly because the richest stone has
been already taken from the higher levels, and
partly because of the gradual impoverishment of
the lode as it goes down. Most of the share-
holders have been stickers, and as £3 13s. 6d.
has been paid per share, and the mai ket has never touched
that point, they have made money by hanging on. The
market, too, hasn’t liked to face the Baillieu cMtn-Daley-
citm-Riiey crowd, so no funn3' business has ever been
attempted with shares.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
From a man
who has been
wandering i n
the wild and
cock-eyed Nor’-
West:—
Broome, the
centre of the Wes-
tralian pearling in-
dustry, is boom-
ing. Shell is
higher than for
years past, and
the market is
steady. Pearls are
in good demand,
and surplus stocks
are said to be
practically ex-
hausted. Follow-
ing on the Monkey-
-11 ear scrimmage
and the American
financial panic,
there was a tre-
mendous slump,
and some of the
big dealers who
owned luggers
made haste to get
out of the wet.
The luggers were
bought by local
men, and now
there are over a
couple of hundred
whites in the
business, either as
full or part owners
of vessels, and as
many more are
engaged as shell-
openers. Japs and
Malays still do
the diving and
the monotonous
work of pumping
air to the man
on the ocean bed.
The only white
diver who tackled
the job was Alick
McLachlan. H e
owned a lugger
and went in for an
oil engine to work
the air pump ; but
a big blow swept
the coast, and
poor Mac lost his
lugger and his life
as well.
Broome is un-
der municipal
government, has a
fine artesian water
supply, and is not
at all a bad place
for white people
to live in. Except during- three months in the
year, splendid vegetables are raised three or four
miles out.
Carnarvon, away to the south, is growing steadily and
sturdily. It loses most of its streets after the Gascoyne
has its periodical overflow, and the local photographer
always has his camera ready for flood pictures. Onslow
is another thriving town—mostly sheep. Point Sampson
has a good jetty and lots of Indian Ocean for big steamers ;
so the little town of Cossack, which possesses neither,
will be shortly wiped out. A new town site is to be laid
out at Sampson, which will then be the port for Roe-
bourne—which might with advantage be obliterated also.
The only reason for keeping it going is the fact that
several costly public buildings were erected there many
y® ars a g°, a nd the Government doesn’t like writing them
off as a dead and total loss.

Phil Harum : The naming of Long-
reach (Central Q.) streets mentioned by “Myrtle ”
(B. 29/7/’O9) was due to a streak of originality in
the surveyor who laid out the place. The same
streak was responsible for the naming of the
streets of Barcaldine. In Barcaldine we have
Oaic-street, Willow-street, Pine-street, and so on.
It is the tree town. In Ilfracombe, a smaller
settlement between Barcaldine and Long-
reach, a Yankee notion has been adopted,
and the stranger find himself in First-'
street, Second-street, Third-street, etc.
“ The Prooshan ” : A feline re-
cently deceased at Tumberumba(N.S. W.)
was a mighty cat before the Lord. He
was of the Thomas religion, and was the
property, of Mr. J. Sheppard, late
(manager of the Manus cheese factory.
There were of pure cat in Thomas.
His owner left the milk or cream around
Tom’s beat without the least fear of the
animal touching it; for he was a remark -
ably honest cat, as well as a champion
mouser. He was also a great rabbi ter—
in fact, he was the best rabbit-cat in the
country, and accompanied his master on
hunting expeditions. Mr. Sheppard kept
a couple of ferrets, and after slipping
these two ultimatums into the mouth of
the burrow, the string net was applied
over the entrance. In a short time the
rabbit exodus began, and when the junior
rabbits came along and wriggled out
through the net, Thomas, the cat, was in
his glory. He was a swift retriever or
fieldscat, and did great work in the
scrum. He has been known to run simul-
taneously ten different ways after the
same number_of young rabbits, and catch
them all. No more numerous cat was
known in these parts. His owner refused
£5 for him, and his untimely end per
steel trap was much regretted.
“Moorabinda” : I am glad “Zola ” put
in a good word for the parson in his pur (B.
12/8/’O9) when giving evidence on the other side
of the question re the Australian and his alleged
‘‘tired feeling.” A great many city folk think
that the parson’s job is all beer and honey. Per-
haps it is when said parson has but one congregu-
tion to minister to, as is usual in city churches.
But it is a different matter when the case of the
country parson comes to be considered. I am
personally acquainted with a minister who was in
charge of a certain N.S.W. (Methodist) “Home
Mission Station.” This minister was (and his
successor is) responsible for an area covering 6000
square miles, and as he had no local preachers to
assist him (a very common predicament Outback),
the order of the day was travelling. The work
was especially hard for the last 12 months of his
ministry, owing to a severe drought which made
travelling difficult. The carrying of horse feed was
imperative. A brief sketch of his monthly
“Bogan trip” will describe the situation. He
was seen on the track early (in order to be at
Wyoming—s 6 miles by unformed roads—in time
for service at 7.30 p.m.), jogging along in a sulky
loaded with horse feed, wallet, and water-bag.
About 30 miles were passed over before the halt
on the banks of the Bogan. Very soon after the
halt, the missionary might be seen seated in the
best shade to be found in the Nyngan district, not
far from a camp-fire, with billy on one side, lunch
on the other, and a paper (or perhaps sermonical
notes) on his knees. Here in solitude he waited
until his nag had emptied her feeding bottle;
then on to Wyoming. Next day he went to
Burdenda. Tottenham, Lansdale, and Summer-
lea occupied another three days. The parson
landed home on the Saturday ready (?) for
Sunday in Nyngan. This work went on,
3ear in and year out, winter and summer.
And summer at Nyngan often means about 118° in
the shade. Country parsons are, as a rule, fairly
good bushmen, and manage to avoid an all night
out, although the parson I speak of, through no
HS own > nearly had that experience.
Whilst travelling some 50 miles from home,
lopsy, the nag, took sick and pegged out within
ten minutes, leaving the parson and his wife (who
often made the long Bogan trip with her husband)
16 miles from a house. That distance had to be
tramped under a heavy load in the moonlight.
Another experience was driving for scores and
scores of miles over black plain country in water
about two feet deep. And yet that Methodist
didn t grumble at his ill-paid lot. Again in the
very hilly district of Nundle (N.8.W.) another
parson friend thinks nothing of travelling 100
miles or so each week of the year, per bike, horse
and Shanks’s pony. And I could quote dozens of
cases to prove conclusively that our Australian
country clerics, at least, are not often afflicted
with that tired feeling—but of course there are
loafing sheep in every flock.
* * * *
“Flinderino ” : It is not very uncommon
on the far northern (Q.) rivers to see two crocodiles
having a willing fight. Usually it is the she croc’
attacking the bull croc’ for eating too jnany
baby diles. Old dad has a bad habit of chew-
ing up newly-hatched youngsters, and the enraged
mother resents the unfatherly conduct. But if all
the saurians that are successfully hatched were
allowed to grow up, all the rivers and creeks of
North Australia wouldn’t hold them. A neighbor
of mine is rearing some infant saurians with the
object of one day touring the world with a team
of performing crocodiles. He has a poultry farm
near a creek, and the little crocodiles used to come
along a guttering he had made between the poul-
try yard and the creek. They’d come up to steal
chickens and ducks. Although they were scarcely
a foot long, they would show fight when caught,
and if you didn’t grab them close to the head,
they d grab you. He keeps them in wired boxes,
containing two kerosene tins cut down lengthwise.
One tin holds water, the other straw or sawdust,
omall birds and pieces of goat flesh are put in for
tucker. Croc’ junior, like his old man, prefers his
food extremely dead ; so he plants the tucker in
the corners of the box until it fills the neighbor-
hood with its howls for deep burial.
CONFUSING.
THE KID : “ Who are those men over there,
Daddy?"
OLD JUMBUCK : “ Shearers , my dear—men shear-
ing the sheep.”
THE KID : “ Daddy , you're fooling me. You
said last night that the damned ungrateful
dogs had started shearing again.”
“Lantana ” : A swagwoman is not a very
common happening-, but a few days ago an old
travelling lady of 80, accompanied by her grand-
son, a youth of 17, passed through Moree
N.S.W.). The boy led a degenerate horse on
whose back wobbled their pack. They had no
tent, possessed only a blanket a-piece, and the one
night they spent at Moree was passed under a
tree. I had a yarn with the old lady, and she told
me that her husband died many years ago, leaving
her nearly destitute, with half a dozen kiddies.
They had all grown up and made homes for them-
selves, but had left the old mother to shift around
on foot. She spoke lightly of tackling a walk of
25 miles next day, and said she “ had some notion
of settling down with her grandson,” that young
person having offered to retrieve the intervening
generation’s shortcomings by supporting her for
the rest of her days.

From a
man in the
North :
The decision
of the Austra-
lian Estates,
Ltd., to put up
bovril and boil-
ing-down and
freezing works
at Wynd ham
(W. A.) recalls a
few older ex-
periments.
In 1901, Mr.
Nieman, a Mel-
bourne chemist,
after having- a
yarn with Cap-
tain Bradshaw,
of the Victoria
River, formed a
syndicate to ex-
tract beef, and
took a plant up
to Port Dar-
w i n. Fro ni
there the party,
consisting- of
Nieman, his
wife and family,
an engineer and
four men, pro-
ceeded by lug-
ger ; but they
were wrecked
at Port Keats
through mi s-
taking the
entrance of the
Fitzmaurice for
the Victoria.
The lugger re-
mained on the
mud for five
weeks, and was
then towed off
by the Wai-Hoi.
The party had
an anxious ex-
perience, being
watched by the
blacks all' the
time. The Port
Keats’ blacks,
it may be men-
tioned, are the
worst savages
in the North ;
two years ago
they murdered
Bradshaw and
three w h i te
men. Nieman,
however, pu t
fear into their
hearts by exploding- some dynamite amongst them
when once they got too close. When the party
ultimately got to the Victoria Nieman found he could
only buy bullocks at £4 a head, instead of the 80s. that had
been mentioned ; so after a few months the business was
abandoned, and Nieman quietly settled down in Palmer-
ston, a poorer but a wiser man.
In 1886 a syndicate was formed to boil down buffalo on
the Adelaide River. At that time there must have been
about 50,000 there; so plentiful were-they that Paddy
Cahill’s tally for a month’s shooting ran into a thousand
head. This scheme, however, never came to anything.
Some five years ago the indomitable Bradshaw floated
the East African and Australian Cold Storage Co., which
commenced operations at Arafura, and put up buildings,
etc., at Blue Mud Bay, in the Arafura Sea. The object
was to extract bovril and ship frozen beef. The co. held
20,000 square miles ; but the coastal country is unfit for
cattle. All the cattle have been removed from Arafura
and Hodgson Downs.
The Victoria River and adjacent country, where the
new co. is to work, is magnificent grazing land. Nearly
the lot is held by the Kidmans, Forrests, Emanuels,
Buchanans, Duracks, and Copley and Patterson. There
are also a few “ cookies ”on 100,000-acre patches. A few
years ago a strip of country between Hall’s Creek and
Wyndham was cut up and thrown open for selection.
Cattle were plentiful on the Ord, Sturt and Margaret, and
all a man had to do was to put up a yard. All some of
the selectors possessed was a few horses. Now they
have nice herds of cattle. The Territory is the only
place left for this game. The Victoria, Macarthur
and the tablelands are still good places wherein
to pick up a tidy mob of clean skins. Two
men on the Victoria “ sat down ” on a billa-
bong with a bullock-waggon. In two years they
had 600 cattle. In Darwin they used to say the
bullocks had five calves a year.
* * * *.
“Bushman,” from the Atherton big-
scrub (Q.) :
Re bush-felling, I contend that “ Janor”(B. 13/5/’O9) is
quite right, and most of his racy critics quite wrong.
Throughout the North Coast of N. S. Wales, the whole of
the coastal districts of Queensland, and also the whole of
the timbered portion of Maoriland, the bush consists of a
heavy forest of large trees growing up through a dense
undergrowth of small shrubs, vines, etc., with the addi-
tion of supplejacks in M.L. To clear the land in those
places you cut all the small undergrowth, vines, etc.,
with a slasher (or scrub-hook); this is “vining” in
N.S.W., “brushing” in Queensland, and “underserub-
bing”inM.L. The large timber is then felled out of a
face ; sometimes in M.L. it is felled up to a certain size ;
whilst in the other places mentioned fig trees (Moreton
Bay fig) are left standing; and in the northern parts of
Queensland the Johnstone hardwood is also left. This is
the work to which “Janor” rightly gives the
palm for “exhilaration.” I have tried many ways
of earning beer—farm work in all its branches, butcher-
ing,_ carpentering (bush), navvying (from ganger down),
engine-driving (stationary, traction, portable and loco-
motive), mining, kauri gum digging, timber getting,
splitting and others ; and I unhesitatingly vote for bush-
felling. You need both brain and muscle if you are to
succeed at it; the strongest man or the best axeman
isn’t necessarily the best bush-feller. Good judgment in
“driving” is worth more than physical strength.
(“ Driving ” is the making use of falling trees to assist in
felling others.) The judgment is required, in the first
place, to sufficiently kerf (why do bushmen use the
carpenter’s term “scarf ”?) the front tree or trees, so that
the back one or ones will drive them without hanging up
in them ; and yet you don’t want to waste work by
kerfing more than necessary. And, in the second place,
you need judgment to so adjust the kerfs in the driving
trees that they will strike the front ones in the right
place. In flat country there are never any very large
drives; but in hilly country it is different.' There the
trees, growing one above the*other up the hillside, branch
out towards the light, thus overbalancing them down hill.
(Unobservant bush men assert “ the valleys draw i he trees”
to explain why they almost in variably fall in that direction.)
The writer, on one occasion, in Hawkes Bay Province
(M.L.), assisted to work up a drive, in which 49 large
trees, mostly mountain ratas, were felled at the one time ;
and, as the drive went without a hitch, it was a most exhila-
rating experience. Given favorable conditions, hilly
country for drives, calm weather (wind always makes
bushfeiling dangerous), and bush free from vines (these
bind the tops of trees together and often cause them to
swing around in the wrong direction), and bushfelling is
one of the cleanest, healthiest, and most exhilarating of
all avocations, even if it is also one of the riskiest.

“F.C.” gets more advice concerning his
shade-tree :
“ F.C.” feels inclined to try the Moreton Bay chestnut
(B. 15/7/’O9). If he has any stock that he values he won’t.
Nearly all the year round it is dropping beans, and these
the stock eat, for the vegetable has something enticing
about it after the manner of opium or Darling pea.
The cattle either die of dysentery, or the chewed
beans form into a solid mass and kill
them. “ F.C.’s ” first choice, the Queensland sugar-gum
(rusticus curlibus), is, as its name implies, a purposeless sort
of a thing, being only useful for staining the ground
underneath it, and restarting bush fires any time up to
six months after they have apparently burned themselves
out.

LITTLE: “ Yes, but it wasn't necessary. He married his landlady!"
“Starfish” : “F.C. ” asks (8., 15/7/’O9) for
further,information rc shade trees. Let me draw
his attention to the Naval Washington orange tree.
It gives good shade, grows big rapidly, lives
long, is evergreen, and moreover is a pro-
ducer of goods. The best for quick growth
and foliage is the seedling, which attains a good
shade-giving size in about 15 years; but for rapid-
ity of fruit producing the budded plant comes
first. A seedling gives fruit in about seven
years, a grafted tree in three or four, but the
latter do not “stay” (i.e. produce fruit) as long
as the former. Buds are big enough to put
their hair up in 20 years. They are not so'
elegant or thick in foliage as seedlings, which
grow so dense that the centre branches require
pruning to let in the light and air. Suckers
should always bo removed, as they are mostly
wood and sap the life of the tree. Otherwise,
orange trees need little or no pruning. Another
ad vantage of this tree is that, when it wants water,
it says so. It makes a sign. Its leaves begin to
curl up. The trees thrive best in sandy or loose
soil, and are ill-adapted for mineral, clayey, or
salty ground.
“Portite”: In your issue (5/8/’O9)
“ Whaffor ” was responsible for some uncalled-
for and absolutely misleading statements about
Port Macquarie. This celebrity stated that a
haul of 250 schnapper was made on the pilot
boat. The pilot crew has very little time to
spare, and never goes fishing, though the town
has two small steamers engaged in the schnap-
per and crayfishing business. “ Whaffor ” says
that the bar is bad. Well, vessels drawing up
t° 10ft. cross in and out night and day, so it
is not too bad. As to the ancient cemetery
becoming covered wdth lantana, that is
not true. The story about the ungodly
playing euchre in the horse-boxes in the
Anglican church is an unadulterated lie,
and needs no comment. The local people
waxed furious over this slur upon their
spiritual character, and swear that the
next time “ Whaffor ” presents his yellow
countenance at the Port he will be bom-
barded with schnapper heads, old mullet,
oyster shells, and boiled crayfish. Ah
Chin, our local cabbage-raiser, has been
blamed by some for the paragraph, but I
really believe our heathen vegetarian is too
honest to lie in that fashion.
“Jimmie Pannikin: Once, outside Bar-
ringun, near the Queensland border, T overheard
a proposal of marriage. The ladies were getting
their seoond wind, while the gents were having a
drink and a draw and a spit, as the Chester-
fieldian M.C. put it. I was studying moonlight
effects in a patch of shadow when the pair stopped
close to me. Said the m. n, “Look here, Liz, I
arsked ya out here to say a few words ta ya. I
want ya ta marry me. Ev course, ya young, and
I’m much older ; but I’ve looked after meself.
I m quite as spry as the best o’ th’ young blokes.
Look at the property I got. I have 3000 wethers
on me back, 450 head a cattle on me front flats,
8000 lambing ewes on me middle front, a seven-
roomed house and garden on me front back, a
hundred head ev horses on me middle back, a
coupler thousand quid in th’ bank, not a cronk
tooth in me head, the best beard Back-o’-Bourke,
and I love ya like blazes. Will yer marry me,
Liz, next month?” A stray camel from the
Afghan camp came tearing through the lygorum,
and three people fled back to the ballroom and
safety. Did Liz marry the man ? Ido not know.
HOW WE SAVED AUSTRALIA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Australia rose in anger,
And then sat down in fear,
When first the cry came surging,
“ The flaming Japs are here.”
Then Sosh and Anti-Soshite
Joined in a mighty shout,
“ Call, ere we all are murdered,
Our brave boy-soldiers out.”
Our colonel had been waxing
His whiskers since the dawn ;
But the cadets he marshalled
Upon the croquet lawn.
“ My lads,” said he unto us,
“ Great work is ours to-day ;
The whole world’s eyes are on us.”
We loudly said, “ ’Ooray 1 ”
Th 're were just fourteen of us—
Bill Smith had hurt his thumb.
His mother wrote explaining
That William couldn’t come.
We shed a tear for Billy
And his ill-timed mishap,
And, ’midst a blast of cheering,
Went forth to meet the Jap.
With all due ceremony
We formed a hollow square ;
Whatever else was lacking,
The hollowness was there.
Our colonel stood before us ;
He smiled a scornful smile
Upon those brown invaders
Who gazed on us the while.
“ Come on ! ” then loud we shouted,
“ O soldiers of Japan.”
They wavered for an instant,
And then in haste they ran
To where their boats were riding
Upon the swelling tide ;
And as they climbed aboard them,
“’Ooray 1 ’Ooray ! ” we cried.
The thirst of blood us urging,
We chased our fleeting foe
Eight down unto the ocean,
And sadly watched him go.
Then spake our gallant colonel :
“ The sight of us, my men.
Has quite demoralised them ;
They’ve gone back home again.”
Tnen cityward we carried
Our colonel, shoulder high—
Our pretty, gold-laced colonel,
Who’d sworn to do or die.
And when we met our people,
Oh, how the cheers did ring !
And all joined in together
And sang “God Save the King ! ”
They took us to the panto,;
We had a glorious night;
They toasted us in sherbet—
“ The heroes of the fight.”
Meanwhile the Japs went rushing
Across the deep blue sea.
’Twas thus we saved Australia
In the brave days to be.
WESTRALIA.
OSCAR WALTERS.

TO REGAIN CORRECT WAIST
MEASUREMENT.
The Appeoved Method.
To begin with, what you most certainly must
not do, if you are too fat, is subject your constitu-
tion to the strain of violent exercising or go in for
starving the body down to normal weight. Such
treatments will pull you down for a time, but will
make you look limp, unhealthy, wrinkled, and
prematurely old. The approved method requires
no sacrifice of comforts and enjoyments. The
only special effort you need make is to go to, or
send to, your chemist and get him to make up the
following prescription :—One half-ounce of Mar-
mola, one ounce of fluid extract of Glycvrrhiza
8.P., one ounce of pure Glycerine 8.P., and three
and a half ounces of Peppermint Water. Of this
harmless mixture take a dose of two teaspoonfuls
after each meal and at bedtime, without departing
from your ordinary habits. You will not only
quickly regain correct waist measurement, but all
traces of superfluous fat will soon disappear with-
out leaving a wrinkle ; neither will there be any
flabbiness, because there will be a re-development
of muscular fibre. This simple, healthy treatment
will do you lasting good in every way.*
PERSONAI
ITEMS
Theodore Martin died last week, aged 92,
and the cable reported his decease as that of a
well-known author. Formerly he was known as
the husband of Helen Faucit, a famous actress of
the old stilted school, whom he married in 1851.
Helen Faucit took the fancy of Queen Victoria
(she always had a leaning to “respectable” lady
mummers), and it was through his wife’s acquaint-
ance with the Queen that Theodore Martin
became a sort of literary-man-in-waiting at Court.
He got the job of writing the “ Life of the Prince
Consort” and the “Life of Lord Lyndhurst,” a
bigoted Tory who led the opposition to Parlia-
mentary reform during the first years of Victoria’s
reign. His last book was “ Queen Victoria as I
Knew Her,” into which the aged courtier put
almost as much butter as was expected.
ALICK PEACOCK , OF VICTORIA,
AS HE IS TO-DAY.
By the Oldest Inhabitant :
Re the death of Martin Hood, of Melbourne, that very
a<fed teetotaller who managed a brewery. There is this
to be said m his favor, that he managed the brewery that
brewed the worst beer in the State. His employer’s bad
beer and bad management of a very bad system of tied
houses led to his downfall. Even a teetotaler is justified
in managing a brewery when the brewery brews such
bad beer that people would sooner drink out of a
waterhole.
N.S.W. ex-Justice W. J. Foster, recently
dead, was educated chiefly at Cheltenham College
(England) and Trinity College (Dublin). At the
former institution, a schoolmate of his was the
late Justice C. J. Manning. At Trinity, Darley
and Owen also received their education. Trinity
College, Dublin, seems to have a contract to
supply N.S.W. with Judges—good, bad and in-
different.
Ex-Justice W. J. Foster (“ Water Jug”),
of N. S. Wales, wasn’t the favorite at the Bar
that this generation might judge from the
Acting-Chief’s buttery panegyric the other
day. “This is a matter in which common-
sense must be used,” he told a Sydney jury
in 1888, “and a jury is more competent to
deal with it than lawyers would be.” Long
before there was any talk of “ the unwritten
law ” Foster adopted it; once on circuit, a
man having been found guilty of shooting
another for philandering with the missus, he
sentenced him to three minutes’ gaol—or it
might have been five ; this writer disremem-
bers. Anyhow, the prisoner was out of the
court before the Judge was. Foster was only
on the Bench six years; but he drew his
pension steadily for 15 years afterwards. The
illness which drove him off the Bench was so
gradual, in fact, that G. B. Simpson, who
stepped into his shoes, is now the senior
Judge; and “G. 8.” himself will probably
retire in a year or so. The late Judge’s
mother was a niece of the Duke of Welling-
ton’s wife.
Sfcarchbishop Clarke, of Melbourne, is
now dreadfully worried about the continued
vacancy of the New Guinea billet, lately
discarded by Stone-Wigg. It carries a salary
of only £3OO a year; and his Grace despairs
of getting a man at the price. As the
Church insists that Ah Chow is a brother,
and that the color of his skin doesn’t count
once he is inside the fold, what about
offering the billet to one of the Chinese
missionaries? Other white workers in the North
have had to submit to brown and yellow competi-
tion on the ground that the work wouldn’t run to
white wages; and it seems a fair thing now
that the Bishop profession should also find out
how it feels.
According to a cable from London, Win-
ston Churchill’s appearance in the Commons at
the end of an all-night sitting, “in slippers,
Pyjamas and overcoat, caused merriment and pro-
tests from the Ooposition.” Yet Churchill is
only a plagiarist; 16 years ago George Dibbs,
summoned hastily from his bed to attend a divi-
sion in the N.S.W. Assembly, appeared with even
less than that on. Australia has supplied the
world with many great ideas for which it has got
little thanks, and the fact that Britain has bor-
rowed without acknowledgment our plan of
having undressed Ministers at divisions is only in
keeping with all the rest. Australia, by the way,
has had at least one disguised Minister at a divi-
sion a.s well as an undressed one. He wasn’t so
disguised that his friends didn’t know him, but he
didn’t know himself. He said his name was
“ Hiccup.”
The late Samuel Hordern outlived brother
Anthony by 23 years. Brother Anthony had a
turn for enterprise and high finance. Sam’s con-
fidence was centred on Sydney and the great
Haymarket business. Anthony’s eye was fixed on
Westralia. The elder brother’s aim was to run
the West as Cecil Rhodes wished to exploit
Matabeleland. He conceived the idea of the land-
grant railway from Albany to Beverley (234
miles),and contracted to construct it in considera-
tion of 12,000 acres in alternate blocks for every
mile of rail. Also he was to introduce 5000 immi-
grants, at the rate of 1000 a year. He was buried
at Albany, just a month before Governor Broome’s
wife turned the first sod.
“Sr”: I know a member of Vic. Leg.
Assembly who, struggle as he will, can’t get over
his nervousness. He is a well-read man and a
skilful scribbler, but it is out of order for a mem-
ber to read his speech, and so the poor wretch
suffers agonies. Determined, however, that his
constituents shall not suffer, he writes letters to
himself, and when he overcomes the horror of
standing up and making a catch at the Speaker’s
eye, he has a sheaf of letters in his shaking hand.
Mostly the constituent’s letter is an ungraceful,
wooden thing; but these communications are to
the point and are backed up by terse argument.
Other members wonder at the general knowledge
of this member’s numerous correspondents. Little
by little he is breaking himself in ; but for some
time yet he will probably have to write to himself
and tell himself that he is his own obedient servant.
“Jean Sibi,” in a tone of reproach :
Truly the Labor member has to walk a chalk-line if he
hopes to escape the Fat Man’s reproach. An eye is ever
upon him, and if he gets drunk only once and has crayfish
in his hair he is liable to get two columns of publication,
with large headings that would do justice to an earth-
quake. Take, for instance, the case of King- O’Malley.
He owns some small houses at Oollingwood. They are
probably too small, and certainly the yards are too
small. Still, he didn’t build them—he only bought
them, and he probably can’t afford to pull them down.
The houses are no smaller than they would have been
if O’Malley hadn’t bought them, and thev are a good
deal cleaner and in better order, for O’lfalley spent
£BOO on cleaning and repairs, which apparently the
previous owner didn’t. Nevertheless, a Tory paper
drew attention to O’Malley’s sins in a leading articie
and Parson Woodfull spoke publicly of him by name,
and alluded to his houses, and hinted that this wasn’t
the sort of thing one might expect from one “supposed
to be a friend of the workers.” Be it noted that the
good kind preacher didn’t mention the name of the
man who built the possibly lamentable houses and let
them get out of repair—only that of the man who
bought them and spent a lot of money on making them
habitable. The kind preacher, in fact, doesn’t seem to
make a practice of mentioning the landlord’s name
unless he is a Labor man, and then only if it is an
inferior property. His apparent reason is that O’Malley
is “ supposed to be a friend of the workers,” so it
appears that he doesn’t mention the name of the pious
church-going owner of slum property, because that
person is fully understood to be an enemy of the
workers. As an enemy of the workers, he possibly has
a claim upon the sympathy and forbearance of Wood-
full. Yes, as I already mentioned, the Labor man
requires to walk a chalk-line.
One George Stiff permanently lost such
suppleness as remains to a man after 80 years of
existence, at North Botany last week. The event
recalls that only one member of the Stiff family
has hitherto earned anything like immortality. It
was a namesake of the deceased who supplied
London Punch with its well-known counsel to
those about to marry. For the one word “Don’t”
this most famous of Stiffs received a cheque for
£5.

“ Charlie ” Harris, who passed over at
South Yarra (Vie.) the other day, was no great
orb in the tirinainent, but he was a useful citizen
all the same. For 15 years he was secretary of
the Saddlers’ Union, and for as long or longer was
Secretary of the Eight Hours Committee, besides
being president of the Trades Hall and other
things. Twice the party ran him for the (State
Assembly, and once he only missed a seat by 11
votes.
Way Lee, South Australia’s most con-
spicuous Chinaman, died last week. He was in the
tea, cracker and general dry-goods line, and years
ago carried on a guerilla war with old Henry Parkes.
Way, by virtue of his long residence in S. Aus.,
reckoned he ought to have the right to wander all
over the continent, but Parkes banged the
N.S.W. door in his face. Subsequently
he had some sort of a revenge—or thought
he had. He had a branch business in Broken
Hill, and in his premises, or very close to it, a fire
started, which 18or 20vears ago wiped out a quarter
of the main street. The police never got to the
bottom of it; but Way Lee shouted across the
border that he knew where it started, and why,
and he would give the information if he
was admitted into N. S. Wales. Probably he
didn’t know ; but he sacked one of his local repre-
sentatives all the same.
Count Mastai-Ferretti, who afterwards
became Pope Pius IX., and Miss Letitia Foster,
aunt of the late ex-Justice Foster, of N.S.W.,
figured in much picturesque gossip for quite a
heap of years. Indeed, until the lady died, in 1852,
making the tnen young “Water Jug’’her heir,
the story was common property in Cork, Clogher’
Kilmore, and other spots where her father was
the Anglican bishop. Count Mastai-Ferretti was
in the army before he took to religion, and he and
Miss Letitia are credited with having liked one
another so well that a marriage day was named.
The rest of the tale is fragmentary, for the lady
bolted from home, or, as the family put it, “dis-
appeared.” Later she reappeared. Pius IX.,
when Pope, extended special marks of favor and
consideration to members of the Foster family
who visited Rome. The lady referred to was the
Bishop’s fifth daughter. She ultimately married
barrister John Henry North, a brilliant member
of the Irish Bar, but died childless. This fact
accounted for her kindness to nephew “ Water
Jug.” The full story of that disappearance from
home was never told.
Be a deceased ex-Judge :
Ex-N.S.VV. Supreme Court Justice Foster, who died the
other day, was one of several cousins of some consequence
in Australia. John Foster (afterwards Foster Vesey Fitz-
gerald) was twice Colonial Secretary, and once' (1854)
Acting-Governor of Victoria ; William*Foster Stawell was
Chief Justice of Victoria ; William Fane de Salis was
Chairman of Directors of the P. & O. Co. ; and Leopold
Fane de Salis was a member of N.S.W. Legislative Council.
“Water Jug” Foster came from the law and church
branch of the family. His uncle was Baron Foster, of
the Irish Exchequer ; his grand-uncle was Speaker Fos-
ter of the Irish House of Commons, afterwards Baron
Oriel; his great grandfather was Anthony Foster Chief
Baron of the Irish Exchequer. Then there were John
Foster, Chief Justice of Common Pleas (Ireland), Sir
Bobert Foster, Chief Justice of England ; while ano’ther
ancestor was Chief Justice Fortescue, of England. All
this is about as bewildering as an indenture of conveyance
Then there’s the Church. W.J.’s father was a rector and
his grandfather a bishop. The latter’s uncle was the first to
husband the famous Duchess of Devonshire. Uncles on
the ex-Judge’s mother’s side were the Duke of Wellington
and Commander Sir Edward M. Pakennam. Further, the
departed pensioner claimed descent from royalty in
several ways and directions. The late lamented was
geometrically featured. His head was a circle ; his fore-
head was a square ; from ear to ear, and from ears to chin
he was an equilateral triangle ; his mouth and brows were
straight lines.; his nose was an isosceles triangle with the
bridge as the base; and his eyes were points. He
looked benign as butter, and his voice was soft as a" girl’s.
He was lithe and little, demure and detailed, gentle and
deprecating. He was precise as a sheet of postage
stamps, and perfunctory as a church verger. His chief
characteristics were sobriety, a keen scent for safety-
piety, unctuousness, alertness, and a habit of licking his
lips. In home life he was ideal; in pious circles he was
saintly. His conscience was ever easy. He drew about
£55,000 from the Treasury one way and another—mostly
another. J

The late Sam Hordern did 1 wot believe in encouraging
even his widow in absentee habits. During life he
allowed her £lO,OOO a year for household expenses, etc.
By the terms of his will, she is to receive £2500 a year,
and an extra £lOOO for the upkeep of the Darling Point
house. Further, while in Sydney she is to reside at the
family mansion, Retford Hall, Darling Point, and she iis
not to be absent more than two years continuously front
the State.
At a meeting of supporters of ex-Premier
Tom Bent, held at Brighton (Vic.) one night last-
week, an enthusiastic admirer of the old meddler
and muddler seized the opportunity to get off this;
most astounding sentiment: “Mr. Bent’s good:
name is unstained. He is like One who was held
to have done no wrong, but the Jews saidl
‘Crucify Him!’ and He was led away.” The
comparison of old land-boom Bent with the author
of the Sermon on the Mount is the last possibility
in the amazing career of the globular Cabbage
King.
