“ Oby,” apropos of some recent events :
The scene which took place in the N.S.W. Assembly
when W. J. Foster was diddled out of his first chance of
a Supreme Court Judgeship was lively, scandalous and
recriminatory. It was all the worse from the fact that
the principal actors were cordially disliked by everybody
who listened to their accusations. Foster was penurious
and selfish. Parke*,was a toady, a schemer and a pre-
varicator, yet with streaks of greatness. Alfred Stephen
had engineered the Supreme Court billet for his slim,
tired andapparentiysickly son. Poorold “ Water Jug” !
he always looked old and emaciated —had certainly been
promised that Judgeship ; but he was borne down by beef
and multitudes. Still, he got his billet later on and made
a very poor fist of it.
ALFONSO, OR THE MISLAID COOK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Kin" Alfonso greatly prides himself upon his prowess
with the chafing dish. He will often toss up an omel tat
the royal table. This is his Majesty’s recipe : Fry in
butter little shredded pieces of chicken until they are
well browned. Then remove, and after turning the
whisked eggs in the pan, place the chicken upon them,
just as the omelet is turning. —News Item.
In olden days a king
Had for his tools of trade
A sceptre and a ring ;
(Not eggs the fowls had laid).
In olden days the lord
, Of any fair domain
Knew how to use a sword,
And carve his foes in twain.
The kitchen he despised,
And roundly cursed the cook
When dishes that he prized
The groaning plank forsook.
He chased the deer for sport,
And dragged it by the tail
Back to the lighted court
Where flowed the good, brown ale.
Then fled the varlet cooks
And fell the beast upon
With carving knives and hooks
To make it venison.
My Lord would often rise
From out his council seat,
To gallop with loud cries
After the morning’s meat.
But as for juggling pots
With broken hen-fruit crammed
Instead of brewing plots :
A king would see you d d.
The varlets for a song
These lowly duties did ;
When anything went wrong
They climbed a tree or hid.
Each to his job did look,
And in mid-air he’d swing
Who’d say he’d found a cook
Where he had sought a king.
N.S.W.

National Pass, Wentworth Palls.
1. THE SHAWLS, MONS MEG CAVE. 2. IN THE TEMPLE OF BAAL
3. STALAGMITES, ORIENT CAVE.
For many years before the advent of the motor-car, Mount
victoria, the last of the Blue Mountain stations proper, was
the celebrated starting point for the 36 miles coach journey
to the Wonder Caverns at Jenolan. Nowadays it divides
honors with Blackheath, Medlow Bath, and Katoomba,
which centres are frequently made the starting point for a
riin by motor to these sUbterrafieart mftrVels. After having
seen the Wonderful canyon scenery of the Blue Mountains,
and viewed the series of magnificent gorges from dizzy
heights, and having tramped along rustic paths by ferny
glens and babbling cascades, the visitor makes for the
Caves, as a sort of grand finish to his tour on the mountains*
The unparalleled attractions of this famous Caves system
will be enhanced by the new cave, the “ Temple of Baal,"
which is to be opened at any early date. Its chief central
feature is a splendid chamber with the height stnd majesty
of Milan Cathedral, and a richness of mural decoration
which is unsurpassed by any known at Jenolan. There
are some exceedingly novel incidents in the corridors and
passages of this cave, among them being a complete though
miniature representation of an earthquake. The elongated
fissure in the surface, with its attendant disruption of the
surrounding earth, being plainly visible, While its caUso-=>
the subsidence of the bed of the Underground river—i§
clearly shown in sections. The cave has been Well protected,
and the interior work includes a number of stairways and
passages, which make it safe and comfortable. The eiectrie
light is being carried to the dome of the big chamber, in order
that its massive proportions might be clearly discernible.

RHEUMATIC VICTIMS
O
A Way to Cure the Trouble-
some Complaint*
#
Drive the Cause of Rheumatism
out of Your System—This Man
did and was Cured.
You can reason this out for yourself if you are
a rheumatic sufferer. If you had a splinter in
your hand that caused a festering sore, you
wouldn’t rub an ointment or something else’on it,
expecting a cure. You would remove the splinter
—the cause of the trouble ; then the sore would
heal. Now if you have lactic acid in your blood
that causes torturing, burning rheumatism, what
chance has a liniment or something rubbed on to
cure ? None whatever. It’s the cause you must
remove, remember that.
The question of curing rheumatism is then a
question of driving the acid out of vour blood.
You’ll suffer a long time before you find anything
like Dr. Williams’ Pills for rheumatism. Why
they are for the blood. They tone the whole sys-
tem to a point that enables it to expel the rheu-
matic poison through the natural channels of the
body. The following case of a man who suffered
with rheumatism and was cured proves their
value in this complaint.
“For years I suffered off and on from rheu-
matism,” said Mr. August Freidrieh, of Burra,
S. A. “By degrees it got worse and was particu-
larly bad in cold weather. It was all through me ;
in every part of my system. I’d get up in the
mornings with my fingers swollen and twisted and
stiff. My joints would swell and turn red and
were fearfully tender. I was so stiff I could
hardly move. If I’d got one jot worse I
would have had to throw up my work, that of
trolly driver for T. F. Robertson & Co. I could
hardly put the collars on the horses. Each morn-
ing when I was getting dressed I went through
agony. The children used to lace my boots, for I
simply couldn’t bend. Perhaps for weeks I’d be
free from pain, but then with the least change in
the weather I d.be stricken as if by lightning.
The pain was agonising. I could riot eat, and
every month saw me worse. My wife rubbed me
with liniments, but I got no lasting relief. I felt
as if my fingers were in a vice and being twisted
round and round. At night I’d sit down to my
tea, and just as I was comfortable the pain would
come on. I got little or no rest at night, lying
awake for hours with the pain. I tried all sorts
of ways and methods to cure myself, but I must
say that Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills alone are
responsible for my cure. I started them after
reading Mr. Orchard’s case in the Burra Record.
After the third box I felt that they were doing
me good ; my appetite was better. From that on
the aches and pains got less, and in the end I was
cured. I’ve not had a twinge since, and I shall
always speak well of them. Besides curing my
rheumatism Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills toned me up
in every way.”
It’s important to get the genuine with Dr.
Williams’ name. A shopman once told us that
only “mugs” bought the cheap imitations he
offered. Even the storekeeper doesn’t think
much of the person he can get to take a substitute.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are sold by chemists and
storekeepers, price 2s. 9d. per box, six boxes
15s. 3d., or they will be sent direct on receipt of
price by the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. of
Australasia Ltd., Sydney.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
The cable announced the other day the
importation of a vast quantity of frozen Chinese
pig into the Cold Coun-
try ; so another nail is
put into the agricultural
Hodge’s coffin, and when
one more Hodge has died
or emigrated, England
will be still a bit more
dependent on the for-
eigner for to-morrow’s
meal. Some day when
the Bull Dog navy has
been badly lamed by the
inventive geniusof a rival
power, and John Bull’s
trade routes are damaged,
all the facts concerning
Freetrade will come to
the surface. But it will
be too late then; for
when the British army
is fighting with its back
to the ancient castle and
very little in its stomach,
7,000,000 people will be
dying in London of starv-
ation. About that time,
no doubt, someone will
arise and yell loudly for
Protection, and anyone who breathes the name of
Cobden will be shot at sight.
The Ma State is in more trouble—some of
the sort of trouble which has made it a reproach
among its “daughters.” At the conference of
Ministers of Agriculture 18 months ago it was
agreed that no State should prohibit the importa-
tion of fruit or vegetables from another State on
account of a disease that was common to both
States; yet the other day Ma’s Minister of Agri-
culture calmly tore up that agreement and pro-
hibited the landing of Tasmanian spuds and
threatened to stop Victorian ones also, on account
of their being allegedly afflicted with Irish blight.
Tasmania has that curse in spots, and Victoria has
it in one locality ; but Ma herself also laments its
occurrence on the Clarence River. Of course,
nobody wants to see the disease spread further
than can be helped, but isn’t a State Insect’s
promise as binding as an ordinary citizen’s?

T.A. : Surely the time has come when
this country should either crawl into the ash

cease bombarding Sing Soon with beer bottles
and blue metal, and direct these missiles in the
direction of chose people who are strenuously
assuring us that the idea of this country attempt-
ing to make steel rails is preposterous.
Thus did the Sydney Telegraph give the
show away on Saturday
The State Premiers knew that if the.y could not get
good terms from Mr. Deakin, who would be likely to he
in strong sympathy with them, their chance from the
next Government would be infinitesimal.
In other words, they grabbed the chance of the
present Fluke Ministry being temporarily in
power to close the matter ; and now they have the
impudence to suggest that' this fluked agreement
shdl be embodied in the Constitution “for all
time,” although they admit that from the next
Government (representing the probable opinion
of the people, say, next year) they wouldn’t have
Buckley’s chance of getting the terms now pro-
posed.
“M.A.” on sweated agriculturists,;—
It is now verging on four months since the underpaid
employees at the Hawkesbury State cow-farm (N.S.W.)
sent Minister Perry a petition stating the worst of their
many grievances, and praying him to admit them under
Chairman Addison’saward, given some two months before.
The State’s Hawkesbury cow-men work from 78 to 86
hours weekly, and their pay is from 25 to 60 per cent, less
than Addison granted in his award to the Sydney men
similarly employed. Now they learn that “ the matter
is receiving consideration,” and they are indulging the
hope that another three or four months should see this
** consideration ” well on the way towards a final decision.
They argue that the Wade Government can’t go on
making private employers do things they, as employers,
refuse to do themselves—and that just shows one the
silly things some people will “argue.”
THE INCUBUS.
The S. Australian Labor party intends to
make the extension of the Legislative Council
franchise the battle cry at the next election. The
party wants to win a vote for every man and
woman in the State for both Chambers ; and when
that’s done the next logical step will be to wipe
one of the two Houses out of existence altogether.
Arthur Griffith, M.L.A., has arisen to
propose that every N.S. W. mother should receive
a bonus of £5 towards expenses when baby arrives.
The proposal has a lot to recommend it, provided
there are not too many inquiries about baby’s
father ; it is the mother of the baby whose father
must not be too closely inquired about who, as a
rule, is most in want of £s—or even £l5. In fact,
there are times when the wild prodigality of
£25 would do her no harm. Has Griffith, M.L.A.,
considered this aspect of the case? He can’t pro-
pose to shut out from the State bounty the mother
who needs it most, and he will be clawed all over
by Cats if he proposes to let her in.
When it comes down to the hard bedrock
of actual experience, it turns out that there isn’t
any job in a white man’s country that isn’t a white
man’s job. In Victoria, 30 odd years ago,
some people were quite sure that mining
wasn’t white man’s work; and they
started to introduce Chinamen—and that’s
how the A.M.A. came to be started;
the veteran W. G. Spence is still on hand
in the House of Representatives to tell
the story. Afterwards, sugar-growing
wasn’t white man’s work. It took years
to knock out that fiction ; but it’s dead
now. Then cotton-growing couldn’t pos-
sibly be white man’s work; quite a lot
of sensible southerners were so sure on
this point that they ruled cotton-growing
out of their list of possible Australian in-
dustries. Now, however. Archdeacon
Campbell, the practical scientist who looks
after the soul of Cairns, and between
times tries to fill the empty spaces of the
North with population, reports the results
of his experimental cotton plantation. He
declares that, not only is it a white man’s
job, but, so far as the vast bulk of the
northern land is concerned, cotton is far
better able to pay white man’s wages
than sugar-cane is, and offers far better
working conditions.
In spite of the yarn of the Argos
that there really isn’t any need of a
graduated land-tax to burst up the huge
Western District sheep-walks, because
they are already being rapidly burst up
by their owners, there are only 76,000
acres under crop for wheat or hay in the whole
district this year. The total area under crop in
Victoria is 2,302,300 acres, so the splendidly fer-
tile and well-watered western country accounts
for less than one acre in every 30. And, of
course, it isn’t because the land won’t grow
wheat; in fact, this district has easily the highest
average yield in the State for the scrap that the
big squatter does cultivate. Last year, when the
average for Victoria was down to 6’55 bushels per
acre, the Western District average was 15 05
bushels—ls'os bushels compared with the starva-
tion I’o2 bushel reaped in the Tatcheradivision of
the heart-breaking Mallee, on to which settlers
have lately been pushed.
The Improvement Lease, which seems to
be a sort of Jonah, has landed N.S.W. in more
trouble. According to the Crown Lands Act, a
lessee must get 60 days’ notice before being
thrown off his lease. The Land Board and the
Land Appeal Court, and also the legal advisers of
Wade—mostly himself—decided that this two
months’ notice need not be given in resuming an
improvement lease, wherefore numerous holders
were summarily ejected. But first the Full Court
and now the High Court have gone against the
Crown, which has consequently to pay a stack of
Costs for the information. The Government, in
addition, will have to re-invest the leases in the
lessees, and begin again with 60 days’ notice, and
pay large sums to recompense the cockies to
whom it granted the lands. Or it will have to
bring down a hurried Act to validate its error,
and then hand over much cash as compensation to
the improvement lessees.
This is how the S. M. Herald seeks
(21/8/’O9) to excuse its frenzied opposition during
the past four months to the Fisher Government’s
sea-defence proposals, which have now been
adopted holus-bolus by the representative of the
Fused Government in London
Had Mr. Fisher, when he unfolded his defence policy,
felt himself at liberty to tell us that he was taking a
course which was generally approved, if not actually
prompted, by the Admiralty, it is quite certain that
those proposals would have had a kinder reception.
It is typical. The Herald wasn’t prepared to
consider the Fisher scheme (so long as there was a
suspicion that the same was of local extraction)
on its merits or in any other respect. But if some
one had only made it plain that the dashed thing
came from London, then the crawlsome old im-
porting sheet would have rushed forward and
cheered it like mad.
By the Old Soldier :
“ Alexem’s ” remarks (B. 19/8/’O9) about the unpopu-
larity of Spain’s little war in Morocco don’t tell quite all
the trouble. The Spanish army is even more unpopular
than the war. It is one of those useless, sham-
aristocratic encumbrances which irresponsible govern-
ments are accustomed to inflict on the people. The
Spanish army has never, since Roeroi, been worth its salt.
It only made the flimsiest pretence of opposing the
Yankees in Cuba and the Philippines, and it has gone on
in Morocco as if it wanted to be beaten. The Spanish
army of the last two centuries has scarcely ever shown
itself dangerous to the enemies of its country, but has
been very dangerous indeed to the people of Spain. Its
principal use has long been to make ” pronuncia-
mentos,” and for many years it also served to furnish
lovers for Queen Isabella and her mother, men
like Narvaez, Prim and Serrano. Now' that Spain pretends
to have a constitutional monarchy, the generals no longer
“ pronounce,” because their pow'er is too well understood
to make it necessary. In 1905 the Cortes passed a law
which made anyone accused of “insuking the army”
liable to be tried by court-martial. Members were plainly
told that if they did not pass it they would be turned
into the streets of Madrid. During the last session the
Catalan deputies, feeling the measure was directed
against their province, demanded the repeal of the law ;
but Maura, the Premier, refused, though he said he w'as
not coerced by the army. The war in Morocco is only
one, and one of the smallest, of Spanish grievances. The
army itself is a much greater grievance.
Coroner Cole, of Melbourne, recently
remarked that the prevalence of infanticide in the
Yic. capital reminded him of “ the worst days of
the Roman Empire.” And the big estates of the
Western District also suggest the worst days of
the Roman Empire. Perhaps the two evils go
together.
“ LO, THE WISE INDIAN!”
A Hindoo, in a land ballot on the Upper Tweed River
(N.S.W.), secured the pick of 13 blocks against 180 white
men who were applicants. This occurrence has created a
great deal of ill-feeling in the district, and at a public
meeting at Tyalgum the state of the land laws which
allows of such a position was vigorously denounced.—
Daily paper.
It would be better not to complain too soon. Per-
haps Bunda Chunn will employ white labor.

In aid of the South Sydney Hospital a grand
plain and fancy dress ball will be held at the Odd-
fellows’ Hall in Elizabeth-street on Friday, Sep-
tember 3. Tickets for the rotatory fun in aid of
the good cause are cheap—only 3s. apiece. Mr,
Leslie Gross is provisional hon. sec., and the
mayoresses of Redfern, Waterloo and North
Botany, who are working for the charity, will
be delighted to sell you admission cardboards.
Observing the clouds of unexpected people who
own motor-cars, the thought occurs : Can they all
afford the juggernauts? A decade ago a
carnage and pair were a sort of hall-mark
ot wealth. Hardly anyone sported such an
eonon whose «K»me was less than (say)
£dUOO per annum. To-day many a thousand-
a-year citizen runs his high-power petrol convey-
ance, and runs it pretty constantly too. Now a
carnage and pair may be sustained with ease at a
cost of £250 per annum. The carriage, once
bought, lasts for years, and if it is of an
attractive design in the first instance, will
rail to pieces before it takes on an out-
ot-date appearance. The indigenous carriage
steed wears well. I know a family which has
been sitting behind the same pair of equities for
e past seven years, and to mv unhorsey eye
the animals still look as good as new. How dif-
terent is the case of the motor! Its original
cost is dreadful. There are numbers of
them careering about the city as 1 write
which cost up to £l5OO landed here. Tyres are a
big item. A “ blow-out ” means, I am given to
understand, an expenditure of not less than £ll.
Un a recent trip to the Kurrajong my host
experienced four of these expensive misfortunes.
Ihe petrol-bill is a consideration. A chauffeur is a
considerably more costly menial than a coachman,
there are entertainment expenses in connection
with motor-owning that were never entered on any
carriage-keeping balance-sheet. And finally, a
demon of unrest and dissatisfaction appears to pos-
sess the car-possessor, so that he is never quite satis-
hed with the vehicle he has got. but always longs
to call his own the last brand of dog-crusher which
whirled past him a cloud of dust. There seems to
be a concensus of opinion among frank and un-
biassed experts that a really good motor is liable
one way and another, to run its proprietor into the
dis hii sement of about £6OO a year or more. No •
for the life of me I can’t grasp how the average
motor-enthusiast manages.
What a negligible section of our gilded youth
goes in, publicly, for chorus-damsel entertaining
nowadays ! Are the stage maidens going off in the
matter of looks and allurement ? Or are the
young men with ready cash to liquidate growing
old and parsimonious before their time? Who
shall say? The fact remains that in the resorts
where one has a right to look for the idle and im-
mature male, adoring at the directoire gown and
garish hat of her who sings and dances for a
living, there is little or nothing doin tr . I
can number on the fingers of one "hand
the well-known Sydneyites of affluence who
mZ- turn® charn ”"K young employees of
Messrs. Williamson, Meynell, Clarke and Gunn
tea and sundries m the Australia Winter Garden
of an afternoon And even this little band of
stalwarts has taken to doing its dash in a diffident
almost ashamed fashion. The type that remains
constant at the shrine of the footlights lady—so
constant, that is to say, as to worship there in
public—seems to be getting as infrequent and as
out of date as the stage-door habitue. And the
stage-door haunter, so I am credibly informed
has not existed among the ranks of really modish
males since the death of the late Queen Victoria.

play.

A romantic theory was that of Mr. Law Har-
greaves regarding the ring bolts and carvings at
WooHahra Point—namely that they were the
work of Spanish grandees, cArca 1650. But now
the Historical Society, which has no soul for
romance, comes forward with what seems a more
hkely though less thrilling explanation. Wool-
ahra Point, which is a part of Point Piper, was
once owned by Captain Piper. He was a great
entertainer. A big garden party was given by
him in 1817, and (according to the Historical
Society) the ring bolts were fixed to hold the
vessels while the guests disembarked, Point Piper
being exposed to the full force of the north-
easters j 1 he wind-condition of this point is up
aganist tne argument of the Span : sh grandee, who
would hardly, unless he were a demented grandee
careen and clean his galleon in so exposed a spot
vhen shelter was handy. The carvings are said
to have been made by the convicts who assisted
at the party—this being their method of killing
tune while their superiors tore food.

0™ of the Sydney dailies has added to its
.births, Deaths and Marriages” an “Engage--
ments feature. It suggests all sorts of possibili-
ties. if engagements are to be announced m
forma there will need to be another section tb
notify the public when fractures of these contract's
occur. And if the public interest requires that
people shall be informed in print of their friends’
marriages and impending marriages, what’s the
matter with also telling them of the decree nisi,
which is so often a notification, not only of the
death of one marriage, but of the birth
of a couple more. And if the pronounce-
ment of the decree nisi is to be thus
placarded, it is only logical that the service
of the divorce papers should be also notified. The
arrival of a citation is tantamount nearly always,
da y s > t 0 an engagement announcement!
And finally why not add to the * k Deaths ’’ column
a list of people who are not feeling very well, and
who are liable to get worse at any moment.
Yours affectionately,

YESTERDAY A POOL WAS I.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VICTORIA.
Yesterday a fool was I,
Saw a pretty face,
Saw a flash of ankle,
Fluttering of lace.
Saw a glance which seemed to say
“ Follow, follow me.
Follow, follow Corj'don,
On to Arcady.”
Yesterday I looked in eyes
Grey, or blue, or green ;
Changing as the sunset
In a opal sheen.
Siren eyes that ravished
All the soul of ine.
“Whither, whither Corydon?”
“ Off to Arcady.”
Yesterday I gazed on lips
Smiling up at me ;
Tan talising—pouting
Most bewitehingly.
Lips that whispered, pleading,
“Follow, follow me.”
Phyllis fled : I followed on—
On for Arcady.
Yesterday all fooled was I
By a pretty face ;
By a flirt of ankle,
Fluttering of lace.
By a glance which seemed to say
“ Follow, follow me.
Follow, follow Corydon,
On to Arcady.”
Yesterday I oft will rue.
Phyllis knew a thing or two.
GORDON MASSEY.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, August 23, 1909.
My Dear Yarrik,—
The non-race-going residents of Caulfield, also'
those who do go to the races but would like the
local racecourse enclosure to be something of a
recreation ground on the 350 days of the year
when no charge is made for admission, have suc-
ceeded in getting the Minister of Lands to make
a “proposal.” The proposal is that the V. A.T.C.
shall disgorge a trifle of its profits to the Govern-
ment trustees of the reserve, “to be expended in
such a way as to provide special improvements
and accommodation for the benefit of the public”—
on non-racing days. The Caulfield racecourse
reserve has been sinfully diverted from the pur-
pose for which it was intended. It was dedicated
to the use of the public as a park ground for re-
creation and racing, but the trustees have practi-
cally allowed the racing club to take full control
of it. this is the way things generally happen
when a club is very energetic and some trustees
are very tired. Personally I feel that I want to
boil a billy and hold high picnic on Caulfield on
some summer holiday— not that I ever do these
things; but I want to do them when I find I can’t
do them.
The papers are making a great fuss over quite
minor flood affairs—about the small cocky who has
lost his cow or seen his fences swept hilariously
down stream, or his pet chimney sitting down
hard and suddenly in someone else’s grass pad-
dock. . These are all so trivial. The really
pathetic spectacle of the floods and disaster —the
harrowing, heart-rendingscene—isthatofSquatter-
dom sitting on its soppy billiard table trying to keep
its feet dry. Flooded out in every part, no
snootin’ don’t you know, can’t get to the city
because the railway is a ruin, no telephones to
wire agonised Fat troubles over, no papers, no
letters, and nothing to talk of but the weather
and there are too many cusses in that. So Squat-
tocracy has to be content with social evenings at
home. The sheep community is suffering from
brain fag, and even bridge loses its fascination,
ialk about the cookies and their spoiled few
acres ! Lord, what do they know of misery
in a flood ?
Victoria s only baronet was duly released from
the matrimonial yoke last week on the petition of
Amy Mary Clarke, wife of the above. There was
nothing for the lady and her lawyers to do but to
produce evidenceof theotherlady (or “person”) to
the satisfaction qf the Court. Possibly, the Court
had been unofficially aware of the person for some
years past, but it listened to the evidence with an
air of pained surprise, and granted Amy Mary
Clarice aforesaid a decree nisi, with Costs against
the absentee. In a sporting way of speaking “it
was no race. Ihe wife had a walk-over, to the
grief of many good matrons, who would have
give.n their latest Paris hats to see Connie Waugh
in the witness box. The fair one, though missing
in the flesh, was represented in the spirit by two
letters which had drifted into the possession of the
petitioner’s solicitors, and these were the only rays
'm suns h* ne that gladdened the dull proceedings.
.., letters brief and to the point—sounded so
like the writer that an identification of her signa-
ture seemed unnecessary. The first one (dated
March, 1J06) said : ‘ Lear Rupert—Lo not forget
that we dine at the Savov to-morrow nio'ht
7 o clock—Yours, Connie. RS.— Do not go to the
old theatre afterwards.” The second billet doux
told its own story of confiding love: “Dear
think I will go to Sydney for a few days.
Will leave next mail boat. Be at your rooms 4.30
'nu^° rrow ‘ 11 seme money — Yours, Connie.”
I his was dated 2£ years later than the other It
takes time for even a baronet to attain that posi-
tion in a Connie’s regard, where she addresses him
as Dear Heart,” and commands him to meet
her at his office with his cheque-book in his
pocket.

A scornful man-who, evidently, would object
to being called a ‘ gay dog ” —makes rude remarks
upon the I estival of St. Bow-Wow at the Exhi-
bition Building :
It might be remarked, as a preface to a chat on a dog
to the h p ; e* Predilection for animals dates back
to the lirst Accident. For it was Eve’s craze for
anacondas that drove Adam to go on the land. And it is
noticeable that Eve’s fad for domestic pets is still heartv
enough to puzzle the psychologists. One mav stroil
hpho'm u th ,t Sh °n at Melbourne Exhibition, and still
behold her descendants clutching various assortments of
nHin a " d nol , S , e ' • T ?, me > a dog has always been purely
utilitarian He is either a ratter or a target for boots y
I never could see anything sentimental about him, except
h.s tail; nor anything adorable except his distance
But to women especially those of uncertain youth-he
is a piece of poetry, a face rest, and a butt for all the
delicious argot of the nursery. You will see elderly
spinsters, who would be severe and didactic on any sub-
ject from aviation to strawberry culture, change to the
burbling fanaticism of a young mother when a do-- is
mentioned. They bend over their exhibits in the show
f .f, as the - v stroke their dinky darlings—the doggies
without parts and magnitude, such as tov and Yorkshire
terriers—they will become eloquent on Towser’s historv
and dietary eccentricities. Most of these ladies are iri-
and eS tw ln - an f° dd Wa b' °' le or two ma - v wear pince-nez
and that air of rugged masculinity that develops when
the last hope has taken himself and his serious inten-
tions over the curve of the horizon. But in all their eves
is the glare of the propagandist or the Chapzander zeaiot
I saw a row of them, strange and reverent, before a col-
lection of those pure white, woolly bow-w'ows that are
generally used as sofa ornaments or boudoir requisites
These dogs, like the shivering diminutive toy-terriers do
not excite quite the contempt which attached to a Greek
emperor, but they are treated with a care and attention
that would never be extended to a hungry tramp Thev
repose on cushions in the bebe tints of pink, or butter-
cup, or the sweetest blue. They have their mistresses to
comfort arid succor them when they are abungered or
athirst. Their kennels are canine palaces in polished
wood, and they are called such names as “Pinkie” and
“ Bowy.” And thepoodles-the King Charles and Japan-
ese spaniels ; and all the dogs that are not dogs but
toilet articles are treated with similar consideration
Women do not appear to prefer the big dogs—the borzois'
tne great Danes, the bull and Irish terriers—and I never
saw one cuddling one of those serial animals that
nr*' summer dogs, because in winter their protracted
entrance keeps the door open too long, although their
Bologna-sausage shape is most suitable for a squeeze.
. o , women of the matured, maidenly variety prefer the
tiny animals ; and when I think what a solace a mena-
gerie must he to them in their declining years, I am
deeply grateful for the menagerie’s creation.
The masked ball of the Artists is to occur
earlier than the first fixed date —in the last lap of
August instead of during one of the early sprints
of September. The glories of it are spoken with
mysterious fervor. Processions and sets of
artistic flavor are being cemented together
with much rehearsal and agonised imagination.
Policemen and fairies and such common or garden
folk are to be strictly taboo. Unless one’s
costoowi (to quote Mrs. Wiggs) reaches such a
point of excitement that the Gippsland tiger is a
mere peaceful saucer of milk by comparison, the
would-be reveller will be shot into outer darkness.
Fantastic and wild or transcendently beautiful
one must be in toggery, for the V. A.S. intends to
knock things. As a discount to the boh-est bohemi-
anism of the revel, tickets this time are declared
available only when accompanied by the endorse-
ment of an invitation. You are invited, but you
pay; and even if you rush in and pay. it doesn’t
follow that you will be invited. So there you
are. All this excitement is to transpire on Satur-
day the 28th—two days after this issue pushes its
red nose into the world.
W. G. Baumann, of Baumann’s Cafe, Sydney,
died a few days back. Father and son, the
Baumanns have been in the Australian catering
trade for something like 40 years. Thirty years
ago the father, who is still alive, had a cafe in
Swanston-street, Melbourne; but he moved on
to Sydney shortly afterwards.
William Webb, the Bendigo flour miller, who
died recently worth a shade over £28,000, left a
couple, of curious legacies. To the widow and
children of John Flanagan, an old servant. £lOO
is to be paid for their kindness to his sick dog
Fido, and a similar amount goes to Samuel Trea-
grove for his care of a second dog. Webb was a
busy man who couldn’t spare time to think up
new names for dogs ; so the second animal’s name
had to be Fido, too. The owner was too busy
even to call him Rover.
An Australian girl writes from London :
Mrs. Norman Bayles is dashing round somewhat con-
siderable in the Fog City. She is generally perched up
on the car beside her husband, who handles the wheel like
an expert, and preserves his cornflower buttonhole pure
and unspotted through it all. He runs over no dogs, nor
does he drag the mangled lamp-post behind him in his
wild career.
Among the Australians who are brandishing a bold
cleek on the links about London are our old friends the
Hartley Williamses. The Victorian ex-Judge is still a pink
and silver exquisite, and his dame’s figure remains the
wonder of her chums.
Another keen Australian golfer is Frank Dutton, the
evergreen. After 20 years in John Bull’s country—the
old Adelaide bachelor knows how to get the best out of
life and the most for his bawbees. He belongs to half a
dozen swell clubs, and to all the swagger associations
for the promotion of sport and pleasure. Also, as a
cavalier of dames, he maintains his form, as the sports
say.
The English women have started to sport flowers—
fresh or artificial—with their outdoor garb. Malmaison
roses and violets are the most popular blooms thus flour-
ished. Some women even carry a posy or one large single
bloom in the hand. The idea is of French origin, but no
one eversees the female relatives of John Bull carry or
wear a flower like one to the manner born as does the
Parisienne. Another new French idea exploited just now
is the giant brolly, ala Directoire. This implement, in
addition to the long-stick lorgnette, makes the ordinary
duchess more formidable than graceful. It is only an
actress who can employ the Directoire gamp with any
effect, and then it is purely stage effect.
Melba is to spend the coming week with us
indulging in a sociable loaf. Three concerts
happen at so much of Ballarat as hasn’t been
washed away, and later at as much of Geelong as
has managed to keep dry. Melba’s coming might
cause the rains to cease and the floods to subside.
In all her Australian trip—from March to August
and from to Toowoomba, also from
Cairns to Thargomindah—the lady only snared
two rainy nights for her warbling. Drought fol-
lowed her footsteps, and unless things alter it
will be possible next summer to identify her track
by the bones of famished cattle on each side of the
way.
Hugh Ward changed his matinee day last week,
and invited all the artists from the other theatres
to come and see him act. It was a cute idea.
Every actor and actress turned up. It was an in-
teresting house to outsiders—mummers on the
stage and mummers all round. A most sym-
pathetic audience. Suppose the fellow feeling
made them respond to every minute point.
Rose Musgrove was not playing. She stayed
in bed nursing a whitlow on her finger-
poor dear. The Braytons brightened one box.
Lily was togged in her favorite old rose color and
a huge hat. Titheradge and Kingston, opposite,
challenged that picture with their Dorothy Grim-
ston, a most artistic frocker, all in black, furs and
all, with half an acre of violets to show up the
effect of her brown eyes. Everybody indulged in
mutual admiration in the Winter Garden at tea
time, and it was quite a successful party.
Mrs. George Langridge—one of the earliest of
Amy Castles’ admirers—gave a big crush to
welcome the little lady home. It was at the
Salon, with a musical programme dashed in. Most
of the guests got into the room—but just got there,
they spent the rest of the time holding their own
few inches of space. A fraction of the room
below the stage was cleared for Miss Castles—
just one scrap of high land in the flood of clothes
and flesh. She has most beauteous frocks, that
£ II- b The reception gown was a dilution of
amethysts and sapphires, well mixed and poured
evenly over her snug little body. Topping this
were ermine furs and a most amazing hat—black
and large and velvet, with a great white feather
like the one Gilbert, King of Cadonia, clips with
bis garden shears. Mrs. Auctioneer Langridge,
who held the guest up for inspection, was tailor-
made and smart in violet. Mrs. J. C. Williamson
held her own with an ermine jacket. How do you
expect “Johanna” to writein cold black ink about
a whole jacket of ermine—the adorable sort
of thing that snuggles on to one with its
clinging satin lining and its enchanting ivory
whiteness? That and a muff too. A sheath of
white cloth skirt was all that appeared below this
voluptuousness. Lily Brayton looked like a copy
of a Titian-haired lady much in evidence in the
last important divorce case. A satin substitute
for a frock clung about her, and a transparent lace
coat hugged that. There were sables somewhere
in the middle distance in an escort’s arm,
and the lady looked out of a cart-wheel
hat. Miss Grace Palotta looked Frenchily
smart. You can t beat black in a crowd.
Her black was the blackest, and a cunning little
hat of black had a dull gold twist to its anatomy.
She came with Mrs. Clapp—a dear old lady
mother of the dressmaker Clapps. The Pinschofs
were in force. Mrs. Beatrice-Daniels-Roberts
that charming lady with the much painted
(portrait-painted) hair, wore black to the
glory of her crown. Mrs. Hogan had a
beaming smile and furs. A few men strayed in.
The Maclnerney lawyer-doctor was there, and
his big Major brother, and Dr. Felix Meyer with
his Saxon-fair wife. The new Russian Consul
(when are we going to stop getting new ones?)
looked sad and weary—mebbe because he was
getting squashed in a far corner. Another man
was the Castles tenor, Nicol Anderson, who
came along with the Tait Brethren. I think Amy
Castles felt that she was welcomed home.
A report blows around that Australia may soon
lose its beloved Bland Holt and the cheery, happy
Mrs. Bland. They are said to have ideas of going
to England, where the lady has golden relatives.
These relatives are not merely gilded, but 18-carat.
Mrs. Bland is a spoiled child of fortune— barring
the fact that she isn’t spoiled—and Bland’s habit
of lavishing gifts upon her has grown into a fix-
ture. Among other results of this habit, she has
one of the finest collection of turquoises on which
a burglar ever gazed at 2 a.m.

Scraps of news drift through regarding Miss
Ereda Sternberg—a young scientist who was right-
hand woman to dead Dr. Maclnerney and a help
to young medico Nelly in excursions among those
atoms that look like nightmares under a micro-
scope. She has been chasing banshees in Ireland
for recreation, and is now in London inspecting
the local germ at the new bacteriological school.
She intends to pick up a few extra handfuls of
knowledge in Paris and other scientific cities
before returning.
From “Rosna,”of Adelaide :
The vice-regals went forth and made Renniark happy
last week ; and the whole irrigation township had its
curled and wore a cauliflower in its buttonhole. There
was a Masonic ball, whereat the Exes, danced with the
local Lord High Orange-growers ; there was a show to
open, a luncheon to dispatch, and a drive round the
settlement. Then having collected a few addresses, the
party steamed back down the Muddy—that is to say, the
Murray.
Bryceson Trehearne’s young Repertory Theatre has had
another bout of literary plays—two nights of them—in
the North Hall at the Conservatonum. The audience
was too big for the building, and quite half of it had to
be persuaded to go home. By 7.30 every available square
inch of hall was occupied. By 8 o’clock the crowds had
filled up every aisle, completely obliterating the view of
the moderately early birds who had c .ught the back
seats. It took considerable rhetoric to prove to them
that the performance could not begin until they ceased
to obstruct the view. Two of Yeats’ plays were given—
the tragedy “ Deirdre,” and the charming little legendary
play, “Kathleen ni Houlihan Synge’s brilliant Irish
picture, “The Shadow of the Glen”; Oscar Wilde’s
“ Florentine Tragedy ” ; “Pericles and Aspasia,” a Greek
satire by Courtney ; and “ Winter Dawn,” a clever tragic
little sketch by Gibbon. For the last named, young Carlien
Jiirs had composed some tuneful accompanying wails to be
used by a string quartette. The incidental music for
“ Deirdre,” comprising prelude, songs and such, written by
Mr. Treharne himself, were a special feature of the show.
For an amateur business, the interpretation was remark-
ably good throughout. The audiences contained most of
the University bigwigs, and, among others too numerous
to mention, Miss Catherine Helen Spence, S. Australia’s
foremost literary woman, carrying her 84 years as though
they were but 20.
Mr. Carlyle Smythe is over in the Slowly Village. He
came a week before his Foster-Fraser lion, just to give u's
a friendly warning to get our best intellects and manners
tidied up and ready, so that the great writer can include
them all in his next book, “Australia at Shirk.” The
much-travelled has taken the Theatre Royal, so that
F.F. may have a fitting place wherein to lecture us
severely.
The golfers have been pursuing a tournament at the
watery waste where the Seaton links ought to be. It is
great ftfn swimming from one tee to another, and using
your opponent for a lifebuoy, and, besides, it’s good for
the complexion. The competitors for the championship
didn’t invite Pluvius down for the final, however, and
their last efforts were achieved under heavenly weather
circumstances. A long trail of spring-frocked persons
trudged doggedly after the would-be champions, and
were afterwards whistled up for tea and buns at the
club-house. The viceroy, a golfer himself, took a
daughter down, while the lady of the house and the
sist.er-twin were saying kind things to orphans elsewhere.
The Carrington-street Orphan Home has had its 49th
birthday, and has moved out to its new quarters at
Mitcham by way of celebration. Mrs. Farr, the founder
of the Home, was likewise celebrating a birthday, but
almost forgot it in her care for the orphans—after 50
years. And yet they say our sex is fickle. Her Ex. first
opened the new Home, and then led the procession to the
lawn, where there were tidies and pincushions to sell to
the rattled visitor.
The same afternoon St. Peter’s College sports packed
the pretty school oval with afrocky bevy of fond mammas
and sisters and cousins and aunts. A young army of
coming Rhodes’ scholars leapt over the earth, and were
duly rewarded with valuable articles they didn’t want.
The Way dame handed them the same, after which tea
and Bedlam were let loose together in the big schoolroom.
Peter Dawson was kidnapped from the Otway by his
adoring family, and concealed for three whole days and a
bit in his native village. His proud ex-preceptor (C. J.
Stevens) and the Choral Society, wherein he sang as a
boy, welcomed him the first night, with Josiah Symon to
disburse the necessary epigrams. He has come back a
thinner, but not an older, Peter, and what he has lost in
flesh he has put on in voice—a joyous and unspoiled
boyish star, who devotes remarkably little conversation
to the subject of Mr. Peter Dawson.
Jessie Redpath, one of our young singer maidens, died
last week, after only a few days’ illness. She was a
daughter of the manager of the Lobethal Woollen Mills,
where we make our good Australian tweeds. Her mother
bad died only five weeks before, and her flatted was already
on his way from London to marry her. A year or so ago
the damsel took her voice to Europe to get it polished ;
but the illness of her mother called her back home.
Yours affectionately,
J OHANNA.
Melbourne people were reminded that old
Time is still a flier when they read last week that
Arthur Snowden had been entertained in a Sun-
day-school room because he was 80 years old.
Snowden used to be a mayor as well as a lawyer
hence the handle to his name, and it doesn’t seem
so long ago since he wasn’t more than 60. Ahy-
way, it is satisfactory to know that he still goes
on Sunday to the church* and is deferred tb by the
brethren as an “ exemplary parishioner.” Thete
are many sorts of exemplary persons—opeh-
handed, close-fisted, genial, jokeless, and Other-
wise—and Snowden is a shining: example of One
of the many sorts.


A WOMAN’S FACE.
THE STORY IT TELLS.
Did it ever occur to you that every woman’s face is a book
of revelations? If she has been negligent or dilatory, her com-
plexion will be defective, her skin unpleasing. But if her com-
plexion is perfect, her skin radiant and clear, you have revela-
tion that she is one of the wise women who know.
Miss Marie Dainton, whose likeness is presented on this
page, enjoys a popularity of which she may be justly proud.
Her extraordinary versatility, her vivacious acting-—her vivid
power of impersonation, her finely trained voice, places her in
the highest altitude of musical stars in England. Whether as
the magnet which draws the Smart Set to the Palace or Pavilion,
or as the prima donna in light opera, Miss Dainton holds her
own in the heart of the British public. Her “ Peggy Macree,”
at Wyndham’s Theatre, is not soon to be forgottem”
Miss Dainton has a dazzling complexion. Read the letter
here produced, and you must conclude that she is one of the
wise women who KNOW.
Now the care of the complexion is, in nine cases out of
ten, a question of Skin Pood only. But when you come to
think of Skin hoods, think also, please, how little you
really know about Skin Foods or the needs
and requirements of the complexion. No
amount of carefulness or discrimination
will be of any use to you if you do not
know. How, then, will you learn, for the
pitfalls are many ? If you lack experience
of your own, learn by that of others.

from the most noted and prominent

Australian stage, until they have reached

praise for the Yalaze toilet preparations

And, remember, when you read this,
that testimony so overwhelming must
serve as a more than sufficient guide to
the average woman who wishes to be
certain that she is right in her selection
of what is essential to scientific and com-
petent care of the complexion.

A MUSICAL RENAISSANCE.—The advent of the Player-Piano
is being followed by a great popular awakening of interest in music ; in
fact, the is already doing for music what the invention of the
printing press did for literature. It is widely disseminating musical
knowledge and musical appreciation. Familiarity with the masterpieces
is no longer confined to that small class known as “ Musicians.”
In the past the difficulty has been to secure a Piano by a well-known
maker with the Player apparatus inserted inside. By reading the above,
you will agree we have overcome this difficulty. They are easy to
manipulate. After inserting a music roll you simply work the pedals
with the feet and move four levers in front of the keyboard. The
apparatus causes the piano hammers to strike the strings and produce
the desired tones.
The playing apparatus does not interfere in any way with the Piano.
Should you desire to play the keyboard you can instantly do so, as each
action is independent of the other.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
As the end of the ’oofball season approached in-
terest in Vic. League games centred on the question
whether Essendon or Melbourne will make the fourth of
the semi-final quartette, and whether South will hang- on
to the topmost place, with its double chance for the pen-
nant. “Skinner’s hireling's,” with some luck, decided
the latter question on Saturday, when they scored 41
points to Essendon’s 37, winning the game, just as they
did the previous one against the same team, by a chance
kick at the last moment. The episode was less scientific
than willing, a large majority of the free kicks being given
against the “ Red and White Angels,” as the sarcastic
barrackers call them. The ’Dons, playing better foot-
ball throughout, led by 14 points at three-quarter
time. After that South gathered itself together, flung
skill and caution to the winds, and rushed the ball down
and through by main force. The “same old” strove to
keep the sphere on the wings or out of bounds, or any-
where distant from its own goal, but in vain ; the
Southerners fell on it and their opponents with great
violence, and drove it through once more. South’s bar-
rackers are an ill-balanced lot. They get nasty when they
lose, and when victory comes their way they go dotty.
Consequently, when the “ Angels ” drove the ball down a
third time and someone booted it through, they went
temporarily insane. The ’Don goalkeeper claimed
that his boot touched the article last; the umpire
gave it to South. Rightly or wrongly, the goal, which
meant.Bo much to both teams, was a gorgeous slice of
luck to South, for the ringing bell gave Essendon no time
to reply. A 35,000 crowd, with a liberal allowance of the
“ force,” viewed the occurrence. For somi; abstruse
reason the collision of ’Wood and ’Varsity attracted a full
house, which got equal value for its coin. By the laws of
logic ’Wood should have run over Uni.; but to all ap-
pearance the Magpies were “ mozzed” or something, and
they had all their work to hold their own. Certainl.v the
kicking of the students was a pleasing change from
the samples they have placed on view of late,
and their finish was a thing of beauty ; but
the fact that ’Wood could only make a draw of
it—39 points each—wants more explanation than
this writer has about him. The Magpies are hereby
notified that their recent performances have been both
irregular and unseemly, and if they aren’t altered there
will be trouble. Carlton collided with Richmond and
piled up 100 points to 60 in a cheerful, but vain, endeavor
to collar the lead from South. ’Roy capsized Saints by 77
to 31, and Melbourne downed Geelong by 68 to 45. If
Melbourne can manage to wallop ’Roy next Saturday,
there are possibilities about it ; wherefore this writer
urges it to rush in something extra, if only for the sake
of varying its normal position in the middle of the caval-
cade.
The only notable Victorian Football Ass. dis-
turbance on Saturday concerned Brunswick and Brighton,
inasmuch as the winner would be practically certain of a
place in the “ final four.” The Brickies had the slight
advantage of playing on their own ground, but that
wasn’t near enough to counteract the superior system and
determination of the Bentites, who held a comfortable
lead throughout the discussion, and won handily by
82 to 52. There is still another game of the round to play,
but as Brighton is now a lost proposition for this season,
it may be passed out with a second-class certificate
as a team of somewhat irregular boggers-in who,
in their second Ass. season, made a valiant attempt
to reach the summit, and would have succeeded if
they hadn’t encountered better men, or something
to that effect. Also if they can rid themselves
of the obsession of Bentism they may yet be premiers.
Of the other uproars, ’Town scrambled home from ’Cote
by 57 to 52; ’Scray made sure of its semi-final by notching
52 points to Port’s 38 ; P’ran passed a.dirty doing to
Preston—63 to 35 being the tale thereof; and Essendon
made certain of beating P’ran on points, should the latter
lose next Saturday, by piling up the notable tally of 113
points to North’s 12. It was a considerable feat on Essen-
don’s part, but this habit of leaving things till the last
moment is risky, and calculated to drive capital out of
the country, besides causing other calamities.
As concerns the “ administrative” side of the
game, the explosion of M.F.O.’s sec. Harper faded out to
a mere flash in the pan. That modest gentleman is said
to have met the Ass. delegates privately and assured them
that lie had been misreported, that he didn’t say half he
was said to have said, and that he didn’t really mean
the half of what he had said. Harper may at least
soothe himself with the n flection that the obnoxious
features he referred to are certain to obtrude them-
selves presently, and that he had the opportunity to
tackle them manfully and failed—most grievously. It
isn’t every man who can make for himself the oppor-
tunity, even though he has no use for it when be has
mad it. During the week, the League disqualified two
obstreperous’bailers till the end of next June, the Ass.
wiped out two for a similar term and two for a couple of
weeks, and the Ballarat Ass. fired out, for the term of his
natural life, a player who so far forgot himself as to
“job” the umpire. On the other hand, the Upper
Yarra Ass. is pondering over the action of the umpire
in the match Healesville v. Millgrove. In the last
quarter a Healesville player anathematised the official so
fervent.l.y that he promptly landed the vituperator one in
the eye, whereupon the ’Ville captain, whose team was
then six points to the bad, called his men off the field and
claimed the match. This unusual official is hardly an
ideal umpire ; still, one can make allowances. The aver-
age football governing body is such a subservient, spine-
less affair that this writer would hardly blame an umpire
if he took his defence into his own hands, jumped on the
ball till it burst, and kicked half the players to the far
side of perdition.
* * * *
The All Maoris have gone stale. And small
wonder. The strenuous game they play is all very well
where the turf is a soft cushion on which to fall ; but the
Australian soil, hard as concrete, resents familiarity. The
result is that the whole team is badly knocked about, and
more or less worn out. And when the Maori gets worn
out he is not able to think. So on Saturday last, at
Sydney Agricultural Ground, a comparatively fresh team
of Australians totted up 10 points before the Maorilanders
broke the egg, and finally u r on by 23 to the Maoris’ 16.
The game itself was a good one, although the Maoris
had lost dash and vim, and their speed had been reduced
considerably. Still they played hard, and w’ent down
fighting with their tails nailed to the mast, so to speak.
Great was the excitement in Union circles in
Sydney 7 on Saturday, when Newtown and Glebe met.
Long odds were laid that Glebe would stamp the cham-
pions flat; and the arrogant red team went on to the field
fully believing that its position at the head of the com-
petition would be enhanced b.v the rout of the cheap
drapery 7 suburb. However a high stomach goes before a
puncture, and the fact that the blowing out of
Glebe was delayed till the second half made for
interest. The first half saw a mighty 7 struggle,
in which Glebe had somewhat the best of it, inasmuch as
it crossed Newtown’s line, and Newtown only just
managed to square things with a field goal. With the
score at half-time three all, the excitement among the
25,000 onlookers became intense. A mighty, staggering
kick b.v Jubb gave Glebe a field goal, and there was a
shout of great joy 7 from the men who had laid odds on
Glebe. But so far Newtown had done most of the attack-
ing, and the play had a tendency to roll down to Glebe’s line
which made the barrackers more than anxious. Twice
the Glebe line was crossed, and twice the tries were con-
verted, leaving Glebe a wreck by seven to 13. University 7
and South Sydney should have been a good struggle, and
it was. For close, hard attention to business the
students are to be commended ; but the luck and the
stars in their courses were all against them, bringing
their efforts to naught, and leaving South top dog with
15 to 13. The horrible example of the day 7 was Eastern
Suburbs, which met the despised Sydney, and went
under by 20 to three. Manly and North Sydney made a
draw of it with three points each, and St. George downed
Western Suburbs by six points to three.
Of course the real interest of the scramble
centres itself around the struggles of the A Division,
which includes Glebe and Newtown, South Sydney and
University. The result of the scramble is that Glebe and
Newtown change places, the latter being now on top with
23 points to Glebe’s 22. South Sydney and University are
still in the same order, the only 7 difference being that the
gap between 22 and 14 is somewhat larger than that be-
tween the 20 and 14 that separated them last week.
* * * *
In the Soccer Division the Rawson Cup, which
carries with it the Sydney Metropolitan Championship, is
in its last stage. Only one round has to be played, and
as Pyrmont has 27 points to Rozelle’s 22, it cannot affect
the result. Pyrmont will take the Cup and the champion-
ship, with Rozelle in second place. The only match
played in the competition on Saturday was that between
Sydney and Northern Suburbs, and on paper Sydney
should have eaten Northern Suburbs raw, but it didn’t.
A strenuous game resulted in a draw, with one goal each.
In the Gardiner Cup sudden death competition, Pyr-
mont flattened Glebe out by three to nil, and has
now qualified for the final struggle. Its opponent will be
either Rozelle or Adamstown. P.yrmont’s success this
year has been phenomenal. So far it has not sustained a
defeat in either competition, and in the Rawson Cup and
competition has won 13 gam s and drawn one out of
14 played. “ When shall its glory fade ? ”
* •* » *
The Wallabies refused to cross over to the
League for £5O per man. There is a dark whisper that
some of them demanded a minimum of £l5O each ; where-
upon the League fell down dead three times, and after
that rushed to drink and hanged itself and took poison and
cut its throat and leaped overboard. The body has not
y 7 et been recovered.
* * * *
Apparently, Soccer football is getting a hold in
Victoria. In the final for the Docherty Cup, when Carl-
ton defeated St. Hilda by two goals to one, the attend-
ance was good, and the interest taken indicated that the
most ancient game of them all is gaining ground.
Probably the fact that it is clean (so far) has something to
do with this progress. It is about time Victoria should start
to get sick of the veiled professionalism of the League,
and in a less degree the Association.
* * * *
Maoriland is in a dazed condition, and when it
recovers will probably be in the same state as when Hori
Grey went to govern it—on the verge of rebellion. The
M. Rub:by Union recently asked the English Rugby
Union to arrange for a conference to discuss amendments
of Rugby rules ; and then got a woodener in the eye in
the shape of an intimation that the English Unions would
discuss the matter ; but “ Colonials ” could be allowed no
voice in the matter.
* » * *
Mr. Alexander Knox, the vice-president and
chairman of the N.S.W. Rugby League—the man who
engineered the revolution that hurled Giltinan, Trumper
and Hoyle from power—is going for a six months’ wander
through the States and British Isles to recover from the
fatigue of his good work. The League will see to it that
he gets a proper send-off.
After being unexpectedly beaten by ’Varsity on
the Wednesday by eight goals to two, the N.S.W. hockey
men put their strongest eleven»into the field on Saturday
for the return match against the Murray Staters. They
scored four goals to one in the first half through the
Victorians’ lamentable habit of wandering at large and
leaving their goal undefended. In the second term the
dark blues played more methodically, and added three
goals to Ma State’s two ; but, as that wasn’t enough to
square the tally, the old lady won the third inter-State
match by six goals to four. The attendance won’t pay
the expenses by several pounds, but hockey is a cheerful
game that may become a national habit when football
and lacrosse have waned and gone down to the bourne
where bear-baiting, archery, and cricket are gone.
The development of the dirigible balloon
threatens to turn Arctic exploration, which was once a
tragedy, into a summer pastime. German}', of course,
will lead the way ; and Professor Hergesell, who is asso-
ciated with Zeppelin, has submitted his scheme to the
Kaiser. Hergesell’s programme is briefly that a special
“Zeppelin ” shall be built for the expedition ; the largest
yet made and a mammoth at that. This craft will sail to
Cross Bay, on the west coast of Spitzbergen, whence ex-
ploratory trips will radiate in various directions. Herge-
sell does not propose to make any “ dash for the Pole” ;
but rather to plod Polewards comfortably and surely, with
plenty of good lager and Frankfurt, and occasional stoups
of sauerkraut on the voyage. Which is, after all, emi-
nently sensible. ThedashforthePoleisoften toomuchlike
the little spring poem which the amateur bard has dashed
off —a failure. The last one fell through for want of one
more horse to eat. Generally there is one horse or one
bear too little, and that wrecks the show.
The Bleriot monoplane No. XI., with which
Louis Bleriot recently won the Daily Mail prize for the
first flight across the English Channel, is the smallest and
lightest monoplane yet made. When the wings are
folded up it is only about the size of an ordinary motor-
car, and with the aviator aboard it weighs only 5071 b.
The engine is 20-h.p.; the spread barely 23ft.; the length
the same ; the area of fabric only 161£ft.; and the weight
carried per square foot is 3'131b. Go on the air, young
man ; it’s simple enough.
The Tour deFrance, which had just commenced
when the last English mail left, is an extraordinary sort
of cycle race, quite unlike anything we have in Australia.
It is a complete circuit of France, which runs into 2800
miles. This distance is divided into 14 stages of any-
thing between 160 and 260 miles ; and spreads over a
month. The towns through which the circuit runs make
high holiday for the occasion, and fSte the riders in the
most liberal fashion. Last year the event was won by
Petit-Preton, after a desperate finish on the Parc des
Princes track, from Faber and Passerieu. This year 150
riders faced the starter.
* * * *
The world’s cycling championships were decided
at Copenhagen (Denmark) last week end ; but, at time of
writing(Tuesday), no word has come to hand as to how they
ended. Clark having gone dollar-hunting in Yankeedom,
and Ellegmard, holder of the championship, being out of
the running through a fall which injured his wrist, the
chances are that the two French cracks, Friol and Dupre,
fought the matter out between themselves ; unless Major
Taylor, by the way, unexpectedly unwound himself.
* * * * -ft
South Australia has taken unto itself a long
distance bicycle road race, the promoters being the
Mount Gambier Club. The venue of the run is from
Mount Gambier to Naracoorte and back, a distance of 138
miles. The date is fixed for October 13, and prizes to the
value of £B5 will be battled for.
* * * »
Most of the world’s out-of-the-way places have
now been traversed by the motor car. Siberia, Mongolia,
Alaska, Central Australia (twice), Central South Africa;
and the last of all, Arabia. And, curiously enough, in
every case the deed was done without loss of life.
They have some peculiar ideas about the “ kaw-
linies” in Britain, and Cycling, the leading English
wheeling journal, recently cast mud on Jack Arnst’s247-
mile ride from Christchurch to Dunedin in 12hr. 21min.,
in this fashion. “It would be interesting to learn how
the distance of 247 miles was arrived at. It is not pro-
bable that across country road in a recently developed
country like New Zealand has been accurately measured ;
in fact, it is far more likely that the distance has been
merely computed.” A glance at the map would have
shown the cheerful ignoramus responsible for the above
that Dunedin and Christchurch are two provincial
capitals, and that the road between them runs practically
along the coast, and is the main highway of the island.
Does Cycling know what a cross-country road is? Also,
a second glance would have shown that the road runs
almost parallel with the railway the whole distance, and
even a sporting writer should know that a railway is
always surveyed. As a matter of fact that road was
accurately surveyed and measured about 30 years ago,
when the country was much younger and less developed
than it is to-day ; so Cycling need not worry as to how
the distance was arrived at.
* * m *
N. League of Wheelmen is up against it.
The guarantors have received notice to “life the over-
draft and all interest thereon ” ; and as the overdraft is
£l5O, there is going to be some horrible language fly ing
round shortly. It isn’t a large sum, but there are grave
doubts if the League’s attenuated back is strong enough
to stand the weight.
* * * *
The commendable efforts of the police to sup-
press the motor hogs and cycle fiends, who whiz round
the Centennial Park to the danger of the lives of many
little Australians and also grown-up ones, have been
largely frustrated by a gentleman on the Bench who lacks
a sense of proportion. This particular Rhadamanthus let
one “hog” (who, it was sworn, had scorched at 27 miles an
hour) off with a fine of £l. In the interests of the com-
munity, it is necessary that Rhadamanthus’ own irre-
placeable cook should get run over. .
Sydney first-grade cricket will open the first
round on October 2, and in general the sport will
continue in the same groove as last year. Tnirteen clubs
will si ill strive for the premiership, and things will settle
down to a sort of Indian summer after the internecine
strife of the last two seasons. The “ Boundary Com-
mittee’’appointed by the Cricket Association got tired,
or driulk or something, and failed to do anything ; so any
fresh frontier delimination will stand over till next season.
•* * * *
The chief effort of the Australian XI. last week
was to pile up 522 runs for nine wickets against Kent —
one of the strongest bowling t earns in England. Ransford
(189) and Armstrong (107) added 192 runs for the fourth
wicket. The total against Kent is the second highest of
the tour —the previous best being 609 for six wickets
against Essex. As the season comes near its end the
scoring is growing more voluminous.
Maoriland had a glorious time at the Aust.
A.A. Championships at Brisbane last week. It practi-
cally mopped up everything, including the draughts
premiership.
The Australasian Amateur Athletic Associa-
tion threatens to “ bring- out ” R. E. Walker, world
champion sprinter, and the long-distance flyer Hefferon,
for a two months’ tour. The State Associations will
divide the exes, and look after the wanderers while they
are within their particular gates. It seems to take a
devil of a lot of money to support these wandering
amateurs.
* * * *
The Sydney Sailing Club was the first to come
out of its chrysalis and hold the annual meeting that
precedes the general stirring into life. Things are
healthy, more new boats are coming out, and there is
talk of forming a new club at Mosman. Mosman has
been regarded as the stronghold of the motor launch ; it
is just dotted over with the power craft of the mighty,
and the rise of a sailing club there indicates that our
plutocracy is not effete.
* * * *
The oracles of cricket in England are not
logical in attributing all the success of the Australians to
their superior “ fighting” powers, their superior captain,
and their infusion of new blood. Says one of the lament-
ing London papers:—“We have become too dependent
on the veterans in the field. The new blood of to-day
must be leavened with the blood of to-morrow, as well as
with that of yesterday.” Now, as a matter of fact, the
English team for the first Test match, which it won, was
about the most veteran on record. There wasn’t a drop
of new blood in it, whereas there was quite a lot of new
blood in the fifth team. The feebleness of Maclaren’s
captainship may be admitted, seeing that it has always
been associated with failure in Test matches. But the
lack of “ fighting ” power in a team is mainly a question
of the bowling on both sides. Once in a way England has
fine bowlers—Richardson and Peel of yore were cases in
point—and then the Australians looked to be comparatively
feeble fighters. But, as a rule, the Australian bowling,
whatever may be said of it in this country—is better than
the English batsman are accustomed to, and then they
mostly fail to bat with confidence.
* * * *
Tom Ivory, the octogenarian sport who died at
Lower Randwiek (Sydney) last week, won the first Caul-
field Cup with Master Avenel ; but that was one of the
comparatively recent events in his long career as trainer
and owner of racehorses. Good old white Ivory was a
power on the N.S.W. turf in the 50’s of last century. The
Caulfield Cup dates back only to 1881, and it was long be-
fore then that the Sydney trainer made the first of his
nine unsuccessful attempts to win the Melbourne Cup.
His best bid was with his own horse Sweetmeat in 1879.
Sweatineat ran second to Darriwell, and might have won
only for being interfered with in the straight. Two years
later the same game battler carried 9st. (the top weight)
into third place behind Zulu. That was the last time
that Ivory’s colors figured prominently in the field for a
Melbourne Cup. One of his that carried a lot of money,
but ran badly, was Travertori, in 1869.
* * * *
The Mclntosh is thinking about retiring from
the p istime of refereeing scraps. At Paris the crowd
rushed the platform, and Mac, who took a vigorous share
in boosting it off again, received a black eye, three bites,
and seven wounds from hatpins. He is now unable to sit
down. Then, a London sporting editor fell on him and
rent him with his pen, writing that he was too little and
too weak to bump the men apart. Certain it is that
London editor has never boxed with little “Mac.” “ Mac”
may not be very big, but he is big enough.
* * * *
Kid McCoy, a product of the recent Melbourne
novice competitions, met the stocky and experienced
“ Bull” Williams at the Cyclorama the other night, and
put up a display of game, speedy two-handed fighting
which satisfied experts that a new Australian feather-
weight champion is in sight. McCoy made the old ring-
fighter look as slow as a diver and about as dexterous as
an elderly penguin. The fight went the whole 20 rounds,
but only because of “ Bull’s ” bovine capacity for stopping
rapid, hard objects with his fine, open countenance and
feeling little the worse for it. There are some faces you
cannot disconcert with a brick, and Williams has one of
that sort. McCoy won every 7 round, and is now looking
for something superior in his class. If carefully handled,
he should go through the best we have within two years.
* * » *
“ Micawber ” : Tim Hegarty, one time a
mighty bruiser before the gods, is dead at the early 7 age
of 37. Among his victims at an early 7 age were Abe
Willis, Jack McGowen, Jack Cullen, Fred Williams, Mick
McCarthy, and “ Micko ” Walsh. When Hegarty arrived in
Yankeeland he quickly spread terror and consternation,
and he paid the penalty of greatness. No one his own
weight would take him on, and to avoid starving he had
to concede much meat. During his concessionary period
he bumped James Britt, then in his prime, and the end
was disaster and sleep. Failing to secure any more
matches, Hegarty went on a Bacchanalian tour, and kept
going till at last he was but a remnant from a select stock
of the latest fashion tweeds. And now he has gone for
good.
* * • *
Bill Squires, who was the lucky or unlucky
drawer of the biggest and roughest farm on the Peel
River Estate (N.S.W.), a patch that was but little adapted
for agriculture, has apparently come to the conclusion
that farming on that sort of land is harder work than
bruising, and he is understood to contemplate an early
return to his old job on quite a large scale. Twelve
months’ battling on that selection may 7 have not only-
enabled him to recover his old punch, but to develop
a new one.

A RHYME OF RAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne itself has not escaped. Early this morning’
the Saltwater River overflowed its banks. Fleniington
Racecourse was inundated, and the water broke through
on to the low-lying portions opposite the abattoirs.
By 8 o’clock the flood waters were 3ft. deep. The saddling
paddock is also under water, and in the luncheon-room it
is a foot deep.— Victorian flood items.
Oh ! the streaming, teeming rain !
Oh ! the splashing, dashing rain !
How it taps with melting fingers on the icy window pane !
How it roars upon the slates !
How it gurgles ! How it mutters
And the landlord irritates
As it overflows the gutters
And drips in upon the tenant and chokes up the gorged
drain !
Then the tenant grips his brolly and hops round to growl
again
Through the rain—rain—rain,
That keeps falling—falling—falling,
Till the brain—brain—brain
At its mooring chains is hauling.
Oh ! the clatter
And the patter
Of the mad, roof-dancing rain !
Oh ! the rushing
And the gushing
Of the gutter-racing rain !
Oh ! the rain that lies in pools
Just to splash the feet of fools
And the dainty shoes and stockings of the shopping
household Jane ;
How it falls —falls—falls,
Till from hovels and from halls
Goes one frenzied prayer to Heaven : “ Damn this ever-
lasting rain ! ”
Hark ! the wild, teetotal rain !
Hear the Anti-Horserace rain !
Flooding bars and jumps and grandstands with a Judkins-
like refrain !
Tearing pubs up by the roots
In a sort of water madness ;
Swamping paddocks where galoots
Stake their cash and part in sadness
When the slow, well-hated fav’rite fails to pull it off
again,
And is cursed by ev’r.v sportsman ere he hurries for the
train !
Whew ! the rain—rain —rain
That keeps pelting—pelting—pelting
Raises Cain—Cain—Cain
For the land is simply melting
’Neath the blowing
And the flowing
Of the never-ending rain !
’Neath the flooding
And the scudding
Of the lipping-lapping rain !
Oh ! the rain that stings and drives
Makes us weary of our lives,
And faint, gurgling shrieks for mercy come from valley
and from plain
Where we swim—swim—swim
In a twilight dank and dim,
And the turgid flood-tide giggles ’neath the tickling of
the rain.
N.S.W.
E. O’F.

In the inter-’varsity cricket match, which was played at
Lords at the beginning of July, t..ree Australians (Seitzs,
Robinson, and Le Couteur) played for Oxford, and the
captain of the Cantabs bailed from S’Afriua. Says London
Daily Mail (6/7/’O9) dazedly apropos the latter:—“To
gain their places in the ’varsity elevens these men have
had to excel the very flower of the great English public
schools.”
* * * •*
Fitzpatrick— who bad the management of Jack
Johnson for two years—wants Bill Lang to go to America
with a view to annexing the championsnip in due course,
but Bill will not leave Australia until he has put some
more local laurels on his fair young brow. He will meet
Bill Squires again (probably in Melbourne during Cup
week) and likewise come to blows with old Bob Fitz-
simmons before deciding on the trip to America, and if he
can’t beat them both handsomely he will stay where he
is. Meanwhile Lang has chucked up football, the
game he played for love. His retirement from the
list of “ muddied ’oafs ” was hastened by an
incident in a recent game between Melbourne University
team and Richmond. One of the University boys, pro-
fessing to have a grievance against Bill for rough play,
squared up to him right there on the field and invited
him to fight. The boxer treated the young bounder’s
challenge as a joke; but it annoyed him all the same,
and next day he handed in his colors to the Richmond
Club committee, with the remark that he had had enough
football.
* tt * *
Queensland is the happy hunting ground of the
spieler. All the so-called games of chance are played
openly, and the law doesn’t raise a fin. The various
agricultural shows are infested with every kind of gun,
and, generally speaking, these guns have a great time.
At Toowoomba show, t’other week, all the old time
swindles—three-card-trick, purse trick, envelope swindle,
wild cat, and the like—were worked for all they
were worth. Two-up was greatly in evidence, and pick-
pockets drove a merry trade. Brisbane is chock-a-block
with betting shops, and guns from southern cities hold up
lamp posts galore. The Exhibition is bound to prove a
mighty fine jubilee for the bloke that lives on his wits,
and unless the law takes a strong grip of things here-
abouts the spieler brigade will get a strangle hold on the
country. Under the reigning conditions the gun is en-
couraged, instead of getting the dirty kick out, or the
cold shoulder, or the glassy eye, or the metallic laugh, or
the boot.
• * * *
“ J.P.” : It is now possible to gain some idea of
Ketchell’s chances against Johnson. Judging by the
Yankee details, Black Brother gave a very helpless display
against O’Brien at Philadelphia. When the big fellow
stood off and peppered the black’s grinning dial with
quick, shooting blows, the champion’s attempts to
counter with heavy swings were laughable. When it
came to a question of speed and cleverness, O’Brien
feinted his man to death, and had Johnson constantly
throwing up his hands or ducking to avoid blows that
were purely mythical. But when O’Brien faced Ketchell
on June 9 in the same city he found the Michigan boy a
very different proposition. Ketchell, fighting with great
courage and coolness, did anything he liked with his
opponent, and inside three rounds had him doubled up on
the ropes in such a bad state that the referee stopped the
fight to prevent further damage to O’Brien’s beauty.
O’Brien, who ought to know- something about it, now
says Ketchell is the hardest hitter in the world, and that
he will wipe the Black Smudge clean off the championship
roll. He further says, what everybody else is saying, that
Johnson has no punch, and that the only effective gun in
his battery is his right-handed upper-cut. If Ketchell can
only evade that upper-cut for a few- rounds, it is within
the bounds of possibility that the White Race will not
need to call upon Mr. Jeffries, after all.
When bartering about terms with Kauffman,
Brudder Johnsing stipulated that he wouldn’t fight
in Australia. “If I was broke to-morrow and had
a £lO,OOO guarantee for a battle in that golderned
country,” he said, “ I’d rather stay right here in America
and dig the streets.” Yet in Australia he got a fair fight,
and won ; and even after that we allowed him to criticise
George Reid. Now, down in Texas, when he proposed
visiting those parts, they waited up all night with guns
and a hot fire for broiling.
