“ Jean Sibi ” offers Melbourne’s furious hoofball
barrackers food for reflection :—“ I have only been a cur-
sory student of Victorian football, but it seems to me that
the game might be improved in one important particular.
At present, when a man bolts with the ball, the rules im-
pose upon him the option of bouncing it every 10yds., or
of losing' it altogether to his opponents under a penalty
kick. Now, connoisseurs of the game will recognise that
a man running with the ball under such conditions will
often provoke the umpire to some dubious judgments.
The umpire, who, by the way, happens to be inferior to
most of the angels, is often forced to discriminate in a
curious way. If, for instance, the running man is bouncing
the ball, and is pushed by a member of the enemy, the
umpire may often have to decide whether he shall penalise
him for holding the ball too long, or, on the other hand,
penalise the pusher for pushing- the push. Followers of
football are well aware of some of the results of those
decisions, and, in former days, w'ere wont to present the
unfortunate umpire with a book of the rules as an ironical
token of respect. Now I suggest that, instead of aiming
language and sarcasm at the umpire, we made it easier
for him by altering the rule regarding the bouncing of the
ball. Supposing that we made it allowable to run with
the ball without bouncing it at all , and further ukased
that a man must drop it immediately he was touched by
an opponent. Would not that guarantee improved and
faster football, as well as relieve the umpire from the onus
of ordering penalties that the modest regard as injudi-
cious, and the immoderate describe in crimson lan-
guage ? ”

An awful thing happened at a small but tony
race meeting in England last month. A totalisator was
being worked in the ring dedicated to bookmakers 'vho
do small ready-money wagerin-- the Little Silver Ring,
in fact; and seeing that the !’ h public thereabouts
had enough horse-sense to begin to perceive dimly the
advantage of being able to make their own market
instead of leaving it to be made and limited by the band
of Noses, the indignant bookies gathered up their
voracious bags and closed their sinful books and withdrew'
themselves from the enclosure. Whereupon the stewards
of the meeting stopped the totalisator, and besought the
Noses to resume pizness, which they accordingly did. In
Britain there is no legal prohibition of the tote, as in
N.S.W. and Victoria, but the racing clubs have not
yet decided to introduce it. Neither have they thought
seriously of drawing any income from betting, save
w'hat they derive from the bookmakers’ payments for
admission to the “rings.” Presently an iconoclastic
steward will suggest the introduction of totalisators,
under club control, and then a storm of opposition will
be raised, no doubt, by the noble lords and other
punters who punt “on the nod,” whilst the slowdy
awakening army of ready-nlbney bettors plead feebly for
a trial of the co-operative system. Finally the question
will be shelved for 10 years, in order that the authorities
may have time to think of what would happen to the turf if
there were no more bookmakers to . bawd the odds on the
course or “milk the dead ’uns”at the betting clubs.
England is the birthplace of bookmaking, and the cry of
“ 2 to 1 bar one ! ” is so hallowed by old associations that
a tote which laid 4 to 1 bar one, after paying a 10 per
cent, commission, would be resented by many good old
Tories as a demoralising contrivance.

THE BIG BLOCK,
BRISBANE
IN BANANA LAND
“Leo Lear”: The Lower Burnett has
gone ahead with seven-leagued strides since the
surveyor got to work on the large cattle leases,
and the scenery is changing as fast as it does in
a nine-act bellowdrama. The unromantic cocky
has no regard for ancient landmarks and
legends, but planks his cow-vards on
the old cattle-camp, drives his cream-cans
down the old “bull-pads,” and seeks the elusive
corn-cob on the clearing where Duffurn used to
lift the station’s weaners. Gayndah has been
surrounded by stations since the days of the pro-
phets ; and, like other Australian towns in a
similar situation, it laid itself down to sleep 20
years ago. Now it drowsily resuscitates, and in a
stupefied “ Whaffor ? ” manner looks round to find
further extension blocked on all sides by banks of
prickly pear. Moreover, its title of “Capital of
the Burnett” is being plucked from its brows, or
roof, or wherever it carries the distinction, by
Biggenden. This settlement of the uncouth name
was until recently only a name on the map; but it
is now the centre of a newly-populated area. A
co-operative butter factory is thelatest move there,
and as it will draw supplies from all kine depastured
on the former large cattle stations of Degilbo,
Wetheron and Ideraway, among others, the future
of a local cream-churning plant looks as bright as
any cow-trade venture can be expected to look.
Also, as sugar-growing experiments have given
very satisfactory results, a Biggenden mill seems
to be as probable as a Federal land-tax or an Elec-
tive Ministry ; and those things are dead certain.
The soil in the neighboring Coulstern Lakes scrub
carries a complexion like The Bulletin’s cover,
and the things it won’t grow are about as incon-
spicuous as the things Australia can’t manufacture.
Northward.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A WIND blows up by Conran, with icy
sweep and roll,
A Bird of Desolation, snow-feathered,
from the Pole;
A stinging sleet behind it, a flying scud before,
It mourns among the forests, it wails along the
shore;
When northward from Cape Byron the mangoes
and the maize
To gracious breezes anthem a psalm of sapphire
days;
When northward yet from Townsville is echoed
warm and clear,
A pagan psean pulsing with Summer-all-the-year.
Your cowslips of St. Kilda their daintiness unfold,
Your tulips glad the gardens, your daffodils their
gold
Display in florists’ windows, soft show’r their
blossoms down,
Those orchard trees that flatter the folks of
Plobart town.
But North the white magnolias and red hibiscus
throw
A gleam of blended glory like mingled flame and
snow.
And oh, those Coral Islands along their seas of
blue !
And oh, that strong nor’easter among the tall
bamboo !
The daisies and the hawthorn in ordered walks of
Kew,
The English oaks and beeches are comely sights
to view,
’Tis pleasing, too, to frivol along the Esplanade—
Encased in winter garments—with some town-
talking maid.
But Southern thought is straitened, as Southern
skies are cold,
And South your social virtues are measured by
your gold ;
While North the ice of Custom is melted by the
sun,
The waters of Convention in wider channels run.
The manners of Port Phillip would seem uncouth
and strange
And somewhat out of focus beyond the Blackall
Range;
As curry lacking powder, or custard wanting spice
The virtues of Port Phillip, at Cairns were merely
—vice.
I weary of your winters, their raw winds and
their sleet ;
Your blue-nosed sons of Business who crowd the
hurried street;
Your stately stores and dwellings, bridge parties,
functions, shows;
Dull thoughts like housemaids marching from
villas all in rows.
Dull lines in ledgers volumed, dull loves and dull
intrigues ;
The crowded sub-divisions, the closed unpeopled
leagues;
New riches furred and feathered, sleek sins and
social ills ;
The landlords and the “lydies,” the beer and
butchers’ bills.
I weary of your wasters, who loaf in “Pitt” or
“ Bourke ” ;
The punters picking doubles, the failures out of
work.
The gloordy bards and artists who earn infre-
quent crowns
And browse on counter lunches and curse the
luck of towns.
For mean and sordid worries, for jealousy and
hate,
The railings at 111 Fortune, the winnings at sour
Fate;
For these, or want of money, or over much of
wine,
You cannot find, my cousin, a better cure than
mine :
Pull out, pull out. dear cousin; that narrow-
chested crowd
Will dig along without you, nor shall it weep
aloud
With grief at your departure ; and when the dis-
tance drowns
The echoes of the tram-cars, you will not miss the
towns.
And oh, the green plantations beneath Australian
suns,
And oh, the Wide Australia beyond the cattle-
runs.
To give you joy of motion, to give you sense of
room
A pack-horse out of Bowen, a lugger out of
Broome !
In singlets and cork helmets, in Assam pants
arrayed,
We’ll trek and travel nor’ward to join the White
Brigade,
The Land of Free-and-Easy, where grows the
sugar-cane
And rum and black molasses, has called us back
again.
We’re talking “Thursday pidgin,” we’re smok-
ing fat cheroots,
With something at our heart-strings that pulls
them by the roots,
We’re “to our necks,” fair cousins, of greedy Melb
and Syd.,
The greasy business “guyver,” the silly social
“kid.”
We’re nor’ward bound, sweet cousins, unto a tropic
clime
Of mangoes, maize and melons and Summer-all-
the-time.
We’ve busted up our dollars. Well, let the gilt
go hang,
There’s loot in shell and rubber, there’s silver in
trepan g.
The cowslips of St. Kilda, the ordered walks of
Kew
Are always sweet and proper—we leave them all
to you ;
We’ve pawned our winter garments, impenitent
go forth,
To sword our oysters open, for pearls, along the
North.
VICIORIA.
E. J. BRADY.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
r T~'HE Lord who lives beyond the Moon
Is brighter than the Sin. at noon !
Though farther than the farthest star
He knows what men and women are !
The Lord who lives so far azvay
Can never hear the sinners pray ;
But righteous people need not fear:
Still will their prayers reach his ear.
For hist! you must not speak of this —
The Lord is mine and I am His.
And He has shown me in my sleep
Who are the goals and who the sheep,
And given me poiver as a priest
To bid to, and bar from the feast —
The Marriage Supper of the Lamb
Whose humble instrument I am.
And graceless goats who would there browse
Shall not come nigh the heavenly house ;
In Outer Darkness they shall wail
Nor shall their prayers aught avail.
But I will curse and harry them
Far from the Nezv Jerusalem ;
And pen them in God's prison grim,
And keep their tears azvay from Him !
Andfire on them shall fall and beat ,
Andflames flare round about their feet ;
And they shall burn and squeal and squirm,
Wrapped round by the Undying Worm !
Bui to the bright Lord I will speed,
And for the Righteous biter cede ;
And they shall walk with harps and crown,
And tread the Godless down — down—down !
The Righteous round the Pit's red rim
Shall stand and sing a joyful hymn,
And praise the goodness of the Lamb
Whose priest and servitor I am.
While I will reach down star by star,
And cast them where the wicked are ;
And burning worlds beneath my feet
Shall be to God a savor sweet !
Far He is mine and I am His —
Of course you must not mention this —
And He has given me the keys
Of Hell to handle as I please. . .
Perhaps to you these precincts seem
Much like the shadow of a dream !
But they are stone, and cause me pain
Because in Heaven I would remain.
Hush ! Go before and with your wings
Hide from me all those hideous things.
Slip off yow; shoes and walk unshod,
And I will take you up to God.
N.S.W.
O. C. CABOT.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, August 21, 1909.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
The Countess and her Earl spent an hour or so at
the races last Saturday, and saw the worst day
in a frock sense—on record. Mrs. Dudley wore
one of her usual pallid shades —grey this time.
Later she paid a flying visit to the Exhibition
Grounds, where some 400 of Queensland’s brightest
assets were employed in making a map of Aus-
tralia. About the same time his Ex. and the
Hore-Ruthven couple fled in the motor-car to the
Orsova to see sister-in-law Mrs, P. Hore-Ruthven
off on the long sea road to London. And one or
more of them watched the finale of the Marathon
race on Saturday afternoon at the Exhibition
grounds. I don’t know much about the nature of
this event, but I believe the runners start at
Marathon and sprint from there to Waterloo, a
distance of 23 centuries or thereabout. The event,
I believe, is mentioned in Gilbert’s “ Pirates of
Penzance.”
A surprise visit to the Sal. Army’s Prison Gate
Brigade ended the viceregal day on Sunday, the
forenoon having been taken up with the Jubilee
naval and military service in the Domain. Then
the viceroy and party paid a flying visit to Rock-
hampton, and arrived back in town on Friday.
Into the remainder of that day they crowded a
trip to the Exhibition and a visit of condolence to
a broken-legged trooper.
The Lobsters were out in full force on Monday
evening to witness that Empire lesson, “An
Englishman’s Home,” including Julius Brown’s
sudden end, and Beatrice Brown’s soul-stirring
shrieks. Beatrice Brown can shriek. She can
also act, but her shrieks distract attention from
her acting. They are the finest things of the kind
that Brisbane remembers. Of course, the appalling
anti-climax was received with howls of delight.
There was once a lady not far from here who
went a-playing bridge and stopped out late. That
wasn’t so bad ; but she went home in a taxi. Now
even that might have been overlooked if only she
hadn’t, on arrival, discovered the entire household
locked in slumber and realised at the same
moment that she couldn’t get out of her gown
(cut long at the bottom and short at the top)
without aid, unless she rent the garment asunder.
What was to be done ? She looked at the
taxi man (he was young and pleasant), and ex-
plained her dilemma. Now, taxi men are nothing
if not versatile. He rose to the occasion, and,
holding his lamp in one hand, battled away with
the other at the hooks, clips and buttons. All
went well until he reached an inch above the waist,
where the space within waslimited, and thingsthen
came to dead stop. But he declined to be beaten.
He gave the lamp into the hands of the partially
disrobed one, and, with instructions to “ hold it
round at the back,” he fought the last button
with both hands, and won. That would have been
all the episode, only the milkman’s boy was out
driving up the cows, and he told the butcher, who
told the maid at a rival house, and now it’s all over
the town about the lady who was unbuttoned
before daybreak on her own doorstep,
Recent remarks about the curious and untidy
doings in the local civil service have caused the
person whose feelings were most hurt to publish
or inspire an article in his own service review,
which probably had to print the thing. The
article states wildly that the civil service is
quite satisfied, and everything in the garden is
lovely. And dozens of officials laughed horribly
when they read it, for the rottenness of things is
a by-word, and the trail of the money-lender is
over the whole show, and the stories of muddle
and incapacity are hair-raising.
James Blair—fighting Jimmy—has been elected
chief of the Hieiand clan in Queensland. The
presidentship of the Caledonian Society isn’t such
a big thing in itself, but its political significance
is shown when one remembers that William
Kidston and Bob Philp were also nominees for
the billet, and that Blair beat them hands down.
The daily press omitted to mention the election
and the way the bairns of Falkirk had deserted
the old banner.
We have had another visitation ! It is the
Eisteddfod this time, and a cloudburst of talent
has fallen upon us. A queer, demoralising thing
is an eisteddfod. When five choirs have informed
me oftenly that the Lord is their shepherd I don’t
take any further interest in the matter; and when
about ten lots of assorted minstrels have said
“ Come into the Garden, Maud,” my only opinion
is that the garden is too crowded and that there
isn’t any room for Maud. When the 19
mezzos say their “Hearts are Weary,” I say
the same—and so is my head. The only thing
that keeps me awake is the pleasure of watching
Prof. Ives, with his mouth in a flycatcher attitude,
waiting for the false note. The professor says we
have one voice that should make Brisbane famous
—that of Vera Parker, a very young contralto,
whose golden notes, he predicts, will be heard of
one day,
The National Councillors held their annual dis-
turbance on Saturday. Mrs. Joey Bell was re-
appointed president. The Countess assisted the
evening’s proceedings with an address on kinder-
garten. Reggie Roe, who now steers the ship of
Education, also- discoursed, and Captain Rome
came to hold the vice-regal wraps.
Three jolly private dances have been given
during the jubilee whirl. Mrs. Joey Bell and
Mrs. Harry Baynes were hostesses on two nights,
and the Robertson clan filled in a third.
Sincerely yours,
MARIO.
The march of the motor ! Longreach and
Winton have thus far failed to get the 125 miles
that divide them covered by a railway, so a motor
car service has been started. The machine honks
out of Winton one day and honks in the next.


GOLDEN GYMPIE.
Gympie is an example of an alluvial field which
was metamorphosed into a rich reefing area.
The metamorphosis was accomplished by Queensland
Pluck, Capital, and Labor.
Since 1877 Gympie has yielded about
2,875,304 OUNCES OF FINE GOLD,
worth approximately nine and a half millions
sterling.
And Gympie is still one of the most flourishing
mining districts in A ustralia.
No. 2 QUARRY, IRON ISLAND, THE “ SUBURB” OF MOUNT MORGAN MINE.
Photo, by F. Wham, Mount Morgan Mine.
COPPER ON WHARF, CAI It NS IQ.).
Around Gympie plenty of good land is available,
and there are fortunes in the products which
may be grown in the soil as well as in those
which may be dug from it.
The mining community furnishes a market for the
producer, no matter what part of Queensland
his land may be.
Queensland To-day: The Queen State
of the Commonwealth.
No. XXXIV.
THE LURE OF GOLD.—V.
By J. D. FITZ GERALD, BARRISTER-AT-LAW.
Gympie is one of the names which stands out in
the varied and picturesque history of Queensland
mining enterprise. Canoona is the exemplar of
the unsuccessful “rush,” followed by failure,
hardship, and positive distress. Gympie is the
very antithesis of Canoona. It is the exemplar of
success. It was a providential disco very, beeausethe
influences which it set in motion are still eddying
onward. Gympie did this service for the young
colony—it restored confidence, not alone confi-
dence in the ability of Queensland to rival the
southern colonies in the production of the precious
metal; but confidence in the stability of the
colony itself. In 1866 a financial crisis occurred
in the young colony which withered up public
confidence. The hopes founded upon the com-
mencement of a railway-building policy had
been dashed down; settlement was not pro-
ceeding at the pace anticipated ; production
dragged on slowly, and stagnation generally
prevailed. The men in the colony began to
despair; those who could leave, left; those who
intended to come went elsewhere. At this
moment the news came that a fresh alluvial dis-
covery had been made by a prospector named
Nash, while travelling north, at a spot between
Brisbane and Maryborough.
Then came the picturesque features of another
great alluvial rush —this time a successful one.
Though another fate has come to Gympie to-day,
STEAMER LOADING IRONSTONE FOR MOUNT MORGAN, AT IRON ISLAND.
Photo, by F. Wham, Mount Morgan Mine.
IRON ISLAND, A PICTURESQUE “ SUBURB” OF MOUNT MORGAN MINE.
HERE THE IRONSTONE FOR FLUXING IS OBTAINED.
Photo, by F. Wham, Mount Morgan Mine.
making it a great reefing centre, the traces of the
old workings remain to tell the tale of the first
assault on its treasures. The scarred, torn sur-
faces of the ground, the small mounds of the
“tailings’ heaps, the holes—some fallen in, some
failed up—all give evidence of the feverish on-
slaught made upon the earth in order to drag her
guilty secrets from her. As the scientist can re-
construct a mastodon from a few bones, so we may
reconstruct the busy mining rush of the year 1867
from the traces of to-day—the rough, bearded men
who flocked in upon the reported discovery of
payable gold by Nash ; the primitive city of tents
which soon sprang up; the sinking of shafts—the
f h Trom^
the cosmopolitan settlement, with the British
peoples as the foundation, and blended, the Ger-
man, Frenchman, Italian, Pole and, above all, the
Hebrew, generally as storekeeper, gold buyer and
financier. Often in this capacity the Jew found
himself in competition with the Chinaman, who
also was gold buyer and general storekeeper, but
rarely a speculator as the Jew was.
Around the men who were “on the gold ” a
community sprang up of traders, hotelkeepers,
caterers, tradesmen, all risking much, for there
was nothing to guarantee to them that Gympie
would be more permanent than Canoona. They
all took risks; the whole thing was a sublime
piece of gambling; an adventure of an heroic
nature, which, fortunately for the adventurers
and for Queensland, “ came out all right.” No one
could then, in a prophetic mood, have predicted
the 2,375,394 ounces of gold, which have been won
from Gympie alluvial and reef mining—nor could
anyone have conceived, in their wildest dreams,
WARRIOR I ISLAND NATIVES PREPARING BECHE-DE-MER FOR THE
CHINESE MARKET.
that the field would —up to the end of 1907 —pro-
duce gold of the value of £9,501,216.
In the early days of gold prospecting, the lucky
digger who “ struck a patch ” did all he could to
screen his operations from the observation of
others. Thus it is related that three Frenchmen
who had located gold on Canal Creek, near the
Darling Downs, hid their secret so closely that it
was not for some years that they were disturbed
in their stealthy operations, and their good
fortune shared by others. Nash, the prospector
who discovered Gympie, worked on for
sonl e time, despite the bait offtfred'bvtbeGovern-
ment of a reward for the discovery of a navable

of rhe reef -. V SB> !Une ;, Thencame t'l> ef inffing
of the reefs, and here another romantic chapter
opens in the story of Queensland settlement.
The reefs, when opened, showed fabulous golden
contents the veritable “jewellers’ shops”
of mining metaphor. “Four men” (says the
author of “Resourceful Queensland ”).“ in six
weeks, from a space only measuring 20 feet, got
£15,500 worth of gold.” If the fever was intense
on the discovery of alluvial gold, if Jhe excite-
ment blazed almost into flame when a lucky digger
found a nugget weighing 804 oz. and worth nearly
£3216 —what must it have been when men, with a
pestle and mortar, or with even more primitive im-
pliments, were getting at the rate of IOOOoz. to
the ton of quartz ? This was before the days of
machinery.
No wonder that the picturesque incidents be-
came more lurid. Primitive passions were aroused
by the presence of great wealth in a small com-
pass, while the police services of Government
were inadequate if these passions had broken out,
to control them. But the pioneers of the mines
in Queensland were law-abiding men; and
though, from time to time, the “ mates ” who had
“struck it rich” felt it incumbent upon them
to go through the form of self-protection by
watching in turn, with revolvers loaded, the rich
spoils of their newly-discovered reefs, there was
no lawlessness of the kind which we read of in
regard to the mines of the United States—lawless-
ness upon which a distinctive American literature
OP s»x- xSffs&s&i
“poor man’s” goldfield, in the sense that every
miner had a prospective fortune in his swag. But
it became a more stable poor man’s field in the fact
that the days of the wage-earners began, and the
often fortune-favored opportunities of the “ tribu-
ter” i.e., the miner who works for a percentage of the
gold which hi * labor wi “s from a mine owned by
a company. The steady investment of capital in
Gympie mines continued, and such enterprises as
the “Scottish Gympie’’ and “Great Eastern No.
2 South ” —of world-wide repute—are the result.
It was the reefing stage of the Gympie field which
attracted from afar the young Scottish miner who
recently, at a most crucial time in the history of
the Commonwealth of Australia, held the reins
of power and controlled the destinies of the nation.
It was in such reefs that he worked underground
until brought to the surface by his comrades and
sent to represent them in the State Parliament in
Brisbane, and afterwards to the Federal Parlia-
ment in Melbourne. The pick of the miner has
been discarded for a mightier instrument—the
pen of the Cabinet minister. Richelieu is made
to say in Lytton’s play that—-
Beneath the rule of men entirely great
The pen is mightier than the sword.
That pen is, of course, also mightier than the pick
—though there is much evidence, not only on the
scarred and tunnelled crust of the earth round
Gympie, but in the vaults of London, Berlin,
Sydney, Melbourne and Brisbane, that the pick, as
a symbolof a marvellous progressiveness in ayoung
nation, is not to be despised. In the connection
in which they are mentioned now, the pick and
pen take this significance, which will be appre-
ciated by the toiler in Britain, Germany, France,
or any European nation —or even the great demo-
cratic Republic of the West—that it is a symbol of
a free democracy, which opens all avenues, even
the highest rewards of statesmanship itself,
to the man of ability who is worthy of pro-
motion. Of no country can it be so truly said, as
the fact proves in Queensland, that “the office is
open to the man fittest to fill \t”—La carrier e
ourerte aux talents; the Napoleonic maxim of
efficiency.
Gympie has had a wonderful past—its future
will be no less wonderful. Though there is no
great spurt in gold production to-day, expert
opinion is not wanting that newer and richer zones
of gold-producing “ black slate’’ (the distinctive
formation in which the gold is found in this field)
will be opened out. There was a period of panic in
the earlier days, when the gold-bearing strata
“ petered out.” But bold hearts did not despair,
and as has been the case with both Queensland
and Victorian mines, the strata lower down, when
tested, proved to be richer than that in the
shallower levels. The scientists are obtaining a
better grip of the geological formation, and as
time goes on will be better able to direct the opera-
tions of those who have invested their money in
mining. As W. H. Traill shows in the “Official
Year Book,” Gympie was in despair when the
reefs ceased to be gold-bearing when a depth of
200 ft. had been reached. Even the geologists
abandoned hope; and it required men with grit
enough to spend their money in the teeth of
expert advice to go on long enough to get through
the unproductive stratum until they reached the
second body of ore. As part of the history of the
past, and the promise of the future, the romance
of the Gympie goldfield should be worth studying
by the intending immigrant.

GOVERNMENT SAVINGS BANK OF NEW SOOTH WALES, Sydney.
The Commissioners are prepared to pay interest on deposits up to £5OO.
ADVANCE DEPARTMENT.
THIS Department offers most advantageous terms to settlers or intending settlers on the land, a few of
the important features being:
(a) Advances made from £5O to £2OOO. (d ) Loan instalments (interest and principal)
(b) Low AND FIXED rate of interest during- are payable half-yearly.
currency of loan. (e) Borrower has the right to repay the loan
(c) Money may be lent for any term up to on any instalment date; interest to that
31 years. date only being charged.
The tenures upon which the Advance Department may lend are:—(l) Freeholds, (2) Certificated Con-
ditional Purchases (with or without associated Conditional Leases), (3) Uncertificated Conditional
Purchases (with or without associated Conditional Leases), (4) Homestead Selections (before or after
issue of the Homestead Grant), (5) Settlement Leases, (6) Settlement Purchases, (7) Conditional
Purchase Leases.
SAVINGS BANK DEPARTMENT.

Some unheroic remarks about cabmen and
taxicabs:—
Oabmen are accidents. No young man ever sets his
teeth and says, “I will be a cabman ! I will give up
smoking and drinking, devote my nights to studying the
traffic regulations and veterinary science, and gradually
work up until I become a cabman l” No ! Cabmen are
just accidents—like most other varieties of robbers. With
the taxicab coming round the corner of the near future,
I refuse to raise a lament over the passing out of the cab-
man. I fear and hate cabmen. I only use cabs when I
am forced to, and when I do I overpay the driver to an
extent that makes him incoherent with joy. In conse-
quence of this cowardly habit cabmen are always abjectly
grateful to me, and one or two have dropped the reins
and burst into tears when they saw me coming. This
display of gratitude would not be altogether unaccept-
able if I could spare the money and was not afraid of
them ; as things are I can’t spare the money, and I am
afraid. So, when a cabman thanks me, I feel I want to
drag him from his cab by the hair of his head and throw
him into the middle of next week, so that he won’t be
found or seen again until the ensuing Wednesday’. It
would be a picturesque revenge, and would vastly’
astonish the grateful brigand ; but. unfortunately, I am
not sufficiently’ powerful to do it. Some day’, when I
meet a strong, wrathful man, I will confide that dream to
him, and take him about in cabs until I find a specially
offensive driver. Then I will rush my friend into the
conflict and watch him slay the vehicle in cold blood.
“DAD’S AND DAN.”

The Garden of the South.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I WAS born within a garden where the fairest
blossoms grow,
Where the hills are tall and timbered, and
the dear old rivers flow.
I was born to sing the splendors of that glorious
domain.
To tell of all its excellence, to plead, and to
explain.
I have ridden o’er its ranges, I have strolled beside
its streams
(0, the rippling phosphorescence where each
mirrored planet gleams !);
I have frolicked in its jungles with glad singing
souls like mine
(Ah, in such a maze of mem’ries, it is treason to
repine !).
I have seen the silent sweetness of its scintillating
sheen,
With the beauteous bars of sunrise and the tender
tints between ;
I have seen the sun of summer sailing through the
skies to set
Past the Dying Day’s dominions in the Bed Sea
of Begret.
I have seen the dark dome studded with innumer-
able stars,

waving branches play

GILROONEY.
Working Back.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
BATLEY was nearly done. Horses and
stores had been abandoned leagues back ;
his black had deserted ; and Batlev was
lost, not knowing, even within 290 miles, "what
part of the country he was lost in.
The last time he had taken an observation, or
had possessed an instrument to take it with,
seemed ancient history, if he ever thought of it’
for Batley’s mind was unhinged with his woes,'
and he realised little except, in a dull, mechanical
sort of way, that the wilds were shortly going to
swallow him up.
The only thing he could not forget was thirst.
He saw water everywhere—water that, when he
clutched at it, crushed up in his Angers like dried
caves and grass. Not that there was no water
about; but the spinifex plains had taken too
much out of him. He was incompetent to find
water now. Fifty yards seemed miles, and the
thirst was being pushed out by another yearning
—for rest—rest for his weary bones, everlasting
rest. Was that shady spot, or that, or that the
place where he would sink down for the last time?
He did not fight against the feeling long. There
was sweetness in the idea of rest, so presently
down he flopped.
Then Batley saw visions again. A few yards
away a young woman, beautiful and naked, stood
staring at him. The sun lit her up in her round-
ness and softness and unconscious magnificence of
pose, as she poised with uplifted arm, a vessel of
water on her head.
Naturally Batley only took in one thing in all
the picture—the vessel of water. It was a kind
of bucket roughly chopped out of a piece of fern
tree trunk, somewhat porous, for he could see the
maddening liquid dripping from it in clear sprint
diamonds on to the bearer’s pearly shoulders.
Helplessly beating his hands in front of him at
this most cruel vision of all, he tried to speak
Water ! Water ! Pour it on me ! Drown
me in it! his soul would say; but from his baked
mouth came no adequate sounds, and in the exer-
tion and excitement of it together the fool of a
Hatley fainted.
When he came to himself, unless that, too, was
part ot the dream, he was wet enough—drenched
With water in fact—and she was there putting
pa msful of it into his mouth, his head being
pillowed on her arm. °
That went on for a long while—long enough for
BatJey to notice the girl had turned into a man
naked, and with the long flowing hair still, but a
young man beyond doubt.
This did not astonish Batley at all. He was
taking things as he found them! S
Long before Batley was tired of it. the youth
got him up to help him to somewhere. Batlev
was only apprehensive at losing sight of the fern
!mkfnpH hIC ! h ' S nake - d guide left behind > and
contained water —precious water.
other naked human forms appeared. Two
"i three youngsters ran up and wonderingly kept
by them. Then a short, thick-set, grey-bearded
male transpired—a male with big limbs, a tanned,
hairy Hercules. He was followed by the rest of
the family, including the maiden of his vision,
illuminatingly like her brother.
Batley was not at all shocked that all these
individuals were as in the costume of the sun cure,
mother-naked. He was scarcely surprised. It
wtis not worth troubling about. Nakedness was
evidently the fashion there. Perhaps they were
a new tribe of white aborigines. It didn’t matter.
His own clothes were the real anomaly, so worn
you could see the most of him through them. He
nearly blushed for shame, and hoped they would
not notice.
Their faces were kindly, all except that of the
old man. whom he thought he caught scowling
once or twice. Not a word of English did any of
them seem to understand when he found his
tongue.
The small fry were jubilant. One of them, a
little savage of about ten, catching Batley’s eye
pointed up a big gum and then let fly a waddy!
Down came a bright plumaged parrakeet, which he
promptly presented to Batley. That famished
wanderer thought he was intended to eat it raw
and nearly started to do so. But they got him
inside their dwelling—part logs, part sods, con-
taining one single, but sizable, apartment
A large pieceof thick bark, indifferently flattened
out, was mounted in the centre fora sort of table
and there were logs and stones about to sit on 1
opears and clubs were handy on racks, and one or
two nets, roughly made with raw river flax were
hanging about.
Batley noticed these things as they were around
feeding him. The provender consisted of some
kind of coarse meal bread and pieces of cooked
wild turkey. He ate with his fingers, scooping
up water in his hands from another fern bucket’
whenever he wanted it, as the rest did.
He stopped eating at last, because the eldest
nude female, the mother of the family, took the
tood away for his good, he reckoned, not from
any parsimony. Then she made signs to him to
stretch out in a corner and go to sleep. He did
so, and dreamed more dreams.
When lie woke again, refreshed and hungry he
seemed to have been sleeping for about two days.
tie sat up in the bed—just a heap of dry grass on
the ground—and took a look round. No one was
about. Noticing that he was stark naked he
went to dress himself, but could not see his things
a i:. yw !'f e - N T could he remember taking them
ott. the amiable savages must have stolen his
clothes somehow while he was asleep.
He sat there thinking about it, looking with
intent at a bundle of kangaroo skins near him so
unused is civilised man to having nothing on.
Hearing noises outside, he spied through a con-
venient opening and found the family were
engaged running races and in other athletic exer
c ’ s ®f; °t, the youngsters saw Batley’s head
at the hole. This stopped the proceedings, and
caused them to all tumble indoors and set about
preparations for a general meal. Presently there
appeared a big wooden platter with what looked
like hot fresh roasted rabbits on it. It was
planked down on the “table,” where there were
already some bread cakes and some pineapples
Dinner was set.
I here was a good deal of business that was un-
intelligible to Batley, but he understood well
enough when he was signed to occupy one of the
stumps and fall to. He felt slightly ridiculous in
his bare condition, but as no one took any notice,
not even the beauteous Diana of his first vision,
he was shamed out of his squeamishness. Once
seated round the table, the matter of clothes was
not so embarrassing. The old man deftly hacked
off small joints with two sharpened pieces of bone
and the rest held them in their fingers, and fell to!
During the meal, he noticed something dangling
over the table at the end of a string. He looked
at it more closely. It was a shilling. As he let
it go, he caught old Hercules frowning at him
again.
But, whoever these people were, Batlev became
one of them, and in a few weeks’ time he was used
to their mode of life and, as you might say, was
of the family. They hunted together, cooked
together, ate together and gambolled together.
Their methods of making each other understand
were soon picked up, being mostly very evident
signs, with an aboriginal expression here and
there, and even a word or two of English. This
hist circumstance confirmed a conclusion that
Batley had already come to, that really they were
of Anglo-Saxon origin, strange as it might appear.
They seemed to have no names, but called each
other by imitating some distinctive animal or
bird cry such as “ mopoke,” which was the sign
of the youth whom Batley always thought of as
Adonis, as he thought of the eldest girl as Diana
and the father as Hercules. The sign for Batley
was the cry of the whip-bird.
The least companionable of the lot was the boss
of the pack—the dad—who seemed to shun any
attempts whatever at intercourse; but Batley
soon got on passable terms even with him.
Diana was quite a beauty, and in the first shock
of this nude existence she had been more em-
barrassing to Batley than all the rest put together.
But he had got over it all, and could now hold
her hand in the moonlight for hours with almost
as little bashfulness as she.
He even set himself to teach her to talk and
understand English, so that he would be able, when
he went away, to make a speech, through her
thanking the whole family for the uniform kind-
ness he had invariably experienced on all occa-
sions, etc., etc. He would have made a fine mis- I
sionary, Batley would.
But that marvellous mystery, or lunatic, or both,
her walrus of a dad, came across the grammar
class one day and blew up like a burst boiler,
making relations quite strained for a while.
Evidently the unteachable old savage was de-
liberately bent on keeping the others as savage as
himself. Resentment swelled behind Batley’s
breastbone, and his life soon became a brooding
on conundrums that took up a lot of his spare
time.
One day the boys, smart as blacks at noting
every little mark, sprung some fresh kangaroo
tracks. I hen spears and some skins were brought
out, and the boys chopped off bushes sufficiently
tall to hide themselves behind. Batley was given
one, and all the male hands made off, following
tne tracks at a great rate.
After about an hour they stopped, and the old
man and Mopoke dressed themselves up in the
skms as kangaroos, and the others, with their
bushes held in front of them, went forward more
cautiously.
linding three fine roos browsing in a clear
space, the boys (with Batley) advanced gradually
towards them behind their bushes and got fairly
close. Then Mopoke began showing himself, in
fine kangaroo style. The biggest of the real
animals looked at him with interest.

on like lightning with its dangerous hind feet. But
the old man stood firm as a rock, his strong spear

out a yard or so in front of him so that the animal
just spitted himself on it. Also he got one in the
neck from Mopoke that settled his hash.
The boss was on his feet again directly, volubly
excited about the two other kangaroos, and roar-
ing out in good English to the boys, “Let drive
at ’em ! Let drive ! What are you waiting for?”
But Batley did nothing but drop his spear
and stand stock still looking at him. The old
fraud himself, realising his slip, could only stare
back helplessly, his face a disgusted red and his
’roo skin, disarranged in the scramble, half on
and half off.
Next morning Batley, on waking, found his old
clothes alongside him, patched in a serviceable
manner, and fit for a long journey.
It had crossed his mind before that he belonged
to Melbourne, Australia, which continent he
supposed himself still on, and where there were
no doubt anxious people who might have to be
thought of some day. But he had always post-
poned this question. Now he was almost glad
that things were brought to a head by what
seemed a notice to quit.
He would soon know something about the
family now. Old Hercules must have something
or 6ther to say before he went, and the burly
ancient must cast some light on the midsummer
madness of their existence.
As a matter of fact, Batley and Diana’s father
did find themselves met, as man to man, for some
such purpose very shortly. Batley had his duds
under his arm, rolled up as he had found them.
He knew better than to put them on, to be laughed
at by the women and boys, until he had absolutely
got to. He was so used to going about in his
plain skin now that the bare thought of getting
into clothes again gave him the creeps.
The color of his own skin was now tan, like all
the rest, and the soles of his feet were thick and
hard as leather. Fur and a tail would only be
questions of time.
There was a long silence. Batley at last said
“Good-morning,” and, getting only a nod and
grunt in reply, he plunged disgustedly into
business.
“ What direction do I go?”
Then .Hercules, after carelessly pointing, pro-
ceeded at last to loosen his tongue.
“It strikes a Government road about 90 miles
off. The boys will see you through. You will
take supplies for a fortnight, for you may have to
wait there several days.
Thus far, he spoke very deliberately, as if
choosing his words, or as saying a speech prepared
beforehand. Now he continued more freely :
“ You are free to stop on if you like. We have
no quarrel with you. But it would be unwise.
You belong to civilisation, and would regret it in
time —when it would be too late. You belong to
civilisation —we don’t ! We’ve done with it, and
are working back. Man should have stopped at
the brutes. They still have natural affections ;
man has improved it away —even his heart’s arti-
ficial. I’d rather be a wild ape than move among
’em again !”
There was so much bitterness in the words that
Batley’s desire to question and to know things
further was all withered up.
He found a big hot breakfast in preparation at
the camp. All hands were busy and hearty as
usual, and everything was on the same old joyous
footing. Diana, fresh as the morning, with the
sun playing about her hair and her glowing skin,
was as gav as the rest, and further unsettled
Batley’s mind. '
So Batley set about the business of departure.
They were to be off (he and the boys) secretly one
morning after breakfast. Batley was going to
have no good-byes.
The naked mother, somehow, got to be an excep-
tion, and went on very like a human being at the
severance of the one link that connected her with
the other world. She broke out passionately and
shed a light on the myster3\
“It was a wicked, wicked will that did it —
nothing else in the world ! ”
“Will?”
“Yes, the wicked will of an unjust father !”
But that was all he got. She shut up like an
oyster. So presently the four brown bodies, with
their impedimenta, were started on their 90-mile
journey.
Batley, out of spirits all the time, did not even
revive at the sight of a real telegraph line and a
cart track. Beside the track they camped for a
week, waiting to see what might pass.
Up to this time Batley’s clothes were wrapped
in a bundle, but now he put them on to play his
part of shipwrecked overlander. So long they
waited that he began to cheer up at last. It
looked like the will of Providence that he should
go back with the boys. Then they heard a distant
whip-crack, and down that hope went. And
finally two bullock drays came lumbering along
laden with dried skins, and the drivers took
Batley back (the boys being in hiding) towards
where people were clothed and in their right
minds.
But Batley sulked all the way and everywhere
—in drays, on coaches and horses, and on foot.
It was all one to him. He should have been
carried at corpse rates wherever he went for all
the cheery company he was. For he was industri-
ously brooding about “a wicked will” and the
shilling that someone had been cut off with—the
bob that was suspended over the table —and the
disappointed legatee, and he also thought of
Diana.
After his other travels there were train rides.
It was holiday time—there were holidays even in
civilisation, it seemed—and a compartment he
managed to squeeze himself into was standing full,
and everyone was jolly. The folk perpetrated
songs. A bright little thing of about 15, jammed
right up against Batley, sang “Castaway,” a
plain, unvarnished taleof sordid love,seduction and
desertion. She also sang “ Divorced ” and “ Met
Again”—more heartrending still; but none of it
comforted Batley.
When at last he got round to Melbourne he
made a little stir. He had been given up for lost,
and his reappearance was good newspaper copy
for a day or two.
His mother was very pleased to see him, and
took him thankfully to church with her, remark-
ing afterwards that she thought the Litany never
sounded so beautiful. Batley prayed for help and
succor for all such as were “ working back.”
His friends said he was a changed man: but,
plunging into incessant work like a horse, he im-
proved in time. He got a book out, and lived
quite another lifetime. He dulled even memory
itself in a humdrum of mere moroseness, en-
livened by profanity at printers, so that Batley"s
life became much calmer in a year or so.
Of course, he would be off again somewhere,
some day, and he might even wander up and rout
out those people yet.
But one day, while rushing down Collins-street
in hot haste to the printer’s, anxious not to forget
the exact terms of some vituperation he had
devised for the occasion, he heard, sharp and
startling, the cry of the whip bird—the “ O-o-o-
whippp ! ” sacred to him alone as his call in
another world. Why, Diana herself had called
him with it a hundred times, and this was so
shockingly like it that here in the crowded Block
he was brought up standing—all attention.
It came from some jolly person in a big motor
car, engaged apparently in driving a party of
gypsies round the town. He was grinning in
Batley’s face as if delighted to make a fool of him.
Not satisfied with that, he was nodding and nod-
ding and nodding with his big head as if he knew
him, and so were the lot of them. Some of the
youngsters were shouting and whip-birding again,
attracting everybody’s attention.
And Batley, instead of walking on and taking
no notice, gazed at them in a sort of mesmerised
way and then went for the car ir. a bee line, as mes-
merised people do, without even begging pardon
of persons he bumped on the way—well dressed
people, too.
And then, afterwards, he squashed into the car
himself and went off with the party, forgetting all
about the printer. It was a big family party—a
stout dad with a pointed beard, an embonpoint
mamma, and a number of assorted daughters be-
having with an extraordinary mixture of gladness
and confusion and merriment.
Of all the talk and jabber they had together not
more than a dozen words are worth repeating
here.
“Working back? 0 that’s all right —the will
was a forgery ! ”
G. BRON.
The Return.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
RED even lies on the unruffled stream
Like fire on a drawn sword, and through
thin mist
Blossom and bloom grow gold in one last gleam
About the altar of a passioned tryst.
Out of the dim green silence cries upstart
Where leaves and lovers bend and whisper
and kiss ;
“ Sweet sight, sweet sound, sweet odor, Ah,
sweetheart,
Stay by me till to-morrow cndeth this !
“ For now the moon upon the mountain looks,
Stirring to fire even that face of stone,
Brimming my heart with music like the brook’s
Till I must sing to it, ah, why alone?
“ Sweetheart, look out, my ship’s upon the sea,
Yet not in that broad deep shall love be lost;
Unto the white-armed shore the wave breaks free,
Far morrow shall be fairer for the cost. ”
The old moon fades, the new moon dawns, the
stream
Smiles at the stars and drowns with even sound
Sighs in the night, and glimmers, all a-gleam,
Like her slow tears, and the wheeled year drives
round
To singing spring once more, and still the air
Smells sweet with promises that waver and
burn
Like incense, and again she hears him swear,
“Sure as the moon returns shall I return.”
The old year fades, the new year darwns, the
light
Lies on the stream like fire on a drawn sword ;
Whisper on whisper rustles in the night,
O new-found lover, old forgotten word !
For, “Ah,” she sighed, “I wait so long alone,
While green earth beckons and its shady groves
Invite, and last year’s vows and leaves are strown
Grey in the dust; but my young heart is love’s.
“ And if his voice returns, it is not strong,
But a thin whisper ; and his face a shade
Thrills me no more. O heart, it would be wrong
Still to deny thee, of a ghost afraid.”
Beyond the unheaving ocean the broad sun
Halts on the veldt to beckon evening on ;
Black broken outposts of a battle done
Lie man and rifle, and the last light’s gone.
The last light’s gone, the last light of the day
That is her bridal day ; and now the dead
Twist in their clammy graves, and through damp
clay
Creep out into the moonrays overhead.
Slow hoofbeats tramp, and groom and bride draw
nigher;
The groom bends over and the bride’s eyes
' shine ;
A shadow in.the path of misty fire,
A voice out of the darkness, “ Thou art mine.”
“ Mine though I buried am ; the creepy grave,
The wind-washed veldt imprison me in vain ;
To this light breath some Power of Darkness gave
Force to return to our old ways again.
“ By deeds and words the living and the dead
Are bound in brass ; undying passionate vows
Out of the darkness draw me ; soft words said
Shall twine like iron round your heavy brows.
“ To you that would forget me, now no more
Kisses shall come ; but you shall feel my face,
Misty and cold ; his footsteps at the door
Like mine, shall faintly sound ; in his embrace
“ This shadow that I am shall yet obscure
All that you ventured for; in the light breeze
I shall bend down and whisper, like a wooer,
And beckon in the wavings of the trees.
“ This is no will of mine that drives me on ;
I do but drift and flicker in some wind
Whose source I know not; for I would be gone
Into the drowsy grave with quiet mind.
“ Blossom and bloom enfathom me no more
With pleasant calm, nor green earth gladdens
me,
Only the tide forth drives, and on the shore
Of purpose fixed no one of us is free.”
The sad wraith faded, and the moon again
Caught bit and stirrup as the groom bent over,
With angry spurs and tight-gripped bridle-rein,
Above the foaming horse unto his lover.
No word returned upon the words he said,
No answer to his arms that held her fast;
But dead leaves crackled in dry boughs o’erhead,
And on the wind a wandering echo passed.
N.S.W.
H. M. GREEN.
